<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-v">Section V<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-v" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medical-microbiology">Medical Microbiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medical-microbiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Part Three: Parasitology</p>
<p>Chapter 1
Introduction</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="host-parasite-relationship">HOST-PARASITE RELATIONSHIP<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#host-parasite-relationship" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A parasite is an organism that resides on or within another living organism, the host, in order to find the environment and nutrients required for its own growth and reproduction. Most successful parasites achieve a balance with the host that allows the survival, growth and propagation of both, the host and the parasite. Majority of host-parasite interactions do not result in disease. However, if the establishment of the parasite results in injury to the host, it will manifest as disease.</p>
<p>Although a large number of living organisms, such as bacteria, viruses, fungi, protozoa, helminths, insects etc. can parasitize human body; in medical practice, the term 'Parasitology' refers to the study of the parasites belonging to only a few groups of organisms, namely, protozoa and helminths. A classification of medically important human parasites is given in Table 1.1.</p>
<p>The degree of dependence of a parasite on a host varies considerably from one parasite to another. For example, some parasites can survive without a host for a number of generations while others cannot. Majority of parasites, however, lies between the two extremes. Some can survive in soil for a few generations while malarial parasite needs either mosquito (for sexual cycle) or man (asexual cycle), and cannot live freely. Parasites, such as some protozoa, develop resistant forms called</p>
<p>'cysts' to withstand adverse conditions. Others such as helminths have eggs which survive conditions outside the host for a long time.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="terms-used-in-parasitology">TERMS USED IN PARASITOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#terms-used-in-parasitology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The different developmental stages through which a parasite passes to grow, mature and reproduce, is called its life cycle. Reproduction or multiplication of parasites may be sexual or asexual and may occur more than once. At least one of the developmental stages takes place in a host. Some parasites undergo sexual reproduction in one host and asexual reproduction in another, as in malarial parasite.</p>
<p>A parasite which can complete its life cycle within one host is said to have direct life cycle. A life cycle is said to be indirect when two or more species of hosts are required to complete it.</p>
<p>The definitive host is the host in which sexual reproduction takes place or in which most highly developed form of the parasite occurs.</p>
<p>An intermediate host is the host in which asexual reproduction occurs, or the host which is essential for completion of the life cycle. Some parasites have more than one intermediate hosts. Figure 1.1 shows direct and indirect life cycles of some important parasites of man.</p>
<p>Thus, mosquito is a definitive host for malarial parasite while man is an intermediate host.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>910 Parasitology</p>
<p>Table 1.1 Simple classification of medically important parasites</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-24208d48.jpeg" alt="img-0.jpeg"></p>
<p>An arthropod which helps in the transmission of parasitic infection is called a vector. The term mechanical vector is used to describe those vectors which transfer the parasite from one host to the other, but is not an essential part of the life cycle. For example, a house fly transfers amoebic cysts from faeces to food which is eaten by man.</p>
<p>A reservoir host is a host in which a parasite is maintained in the absence of its natural host. When a parasite for which man is not a normal host, enters the human body, it is not able to complete its normal life cycle. It becomes a dead-end infection, and may lead to a serious condition. In such cases, man is called an accidental host. For example, hydatid cyst caused by <em>Echinococcus granulosus</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="transmission-of-parasitic-infections">TRANSMISSION OF PARASITIC INFECTIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transmission-of-parasitic-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For establishment in the human body, the parasite must enter the human host through a proper route so that it reaches an appropriate site where it can continue its life cycle. The mode of transmission also depends on the nature of the host other than man; and the location of the parasite in that host.</p>
<p>There are three main routes of transmission through which the parasite enters the human body.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>

<p>Fig. 1.1 An overview of life cycles of parasites</p>
<p>912 Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-faecal-oral-route">1. Faecal-oral route<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-faecal-oral-route" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ingestion of the parasite by mouth is the commonest method of transmission of parasites. The parasite may enter the intestine by:</p>
<p>(i) Ingestion of food or water contaminated with faeces containing the infective stage of the parasite: Examples of parasites transmitted by this method are nematodes <em>Trichuris trichiura</em> and <em>Enterobius vermicularis</em> and protozoans <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em> and <em>Giardia lamblia</em>.</p>
<p>(ii) Ingestion of improperly cooked meat, fish or vegetables: The parasite larvae under soercystment and lie in the muscles or tissues of the intermediate host. If not killed during cooking, they continue their life cycle when ingested by man. For example <em>Taenia saginata</em> in beef, <em>Taenia solium</em> in pork, <em>Trichinella spiralis</em> in pork.</p>
<p>(iii) Ingestion of the intermediate host harbouring the parasite: For example, Infection can be transmitted by drinking water containing cyclops, an intermediate host for <em>Dracunculus medinensis</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-penetration-through-the-skin">2. Penetration Through the Skin<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-penetration-through-the-skin" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The infective larvae of various parasites enter the human body through the skin, reach the blood stream, and then migrate to the site where they can mature and reproduce. The free swimming cercariae of <em>Schistosoma</em> in water enter through the skin of a person coming in contact with that body of water. Infective (e.g. filariiform) larvae of <em>Strongyloides</em> or hookwarm can enter through the skin if in contact with soil polluted by faeces containing these larvae. Some parasites penetrate the skin through an insect bite (e.g. microfilariae) while others are directly injected into the bloodstream by insects (e.g. <em>Plasmodium</em> species, <em>Leishmania</em> species and some species of <em>Trypanosoma</em>). <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em> infection is transmitted when the contaminated faeces of an insect is rubbed over the insect bite.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-by-sexual-contact">3. By Sexual Contact<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-by-sexual-contact" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Parasites such as <em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em> can be transmitted from one person to another by sexual contact.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="31a7f4d">♦ PARASITIC INFECTION, INFESTATION AND DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#31a7f4d" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Many parasitic infections are asymptomatic or produce very mild symptoms. The word ‘infestation’ refers to external parasitism of parasites living on the external surfaces of the body. These parasites are known as ectoparasites. It is also necessary to differentiate between infection and disease. In infection, the patient suffers very little damage and shows no symptoms, whereas in disease, pathological changes and symptoms of varying degree may be seen depending on the severity of disease.</p>
<p>The pathogenesis of parasitic disease depends upon several factors, such as the numbers of parasites, their tissuetropism and mechanisms of tissue damage. Only a few parasites, such as protozoa, are capable of multiplying in the human host. After entering the host, most parasites exhibit the phenomenon of tissuetropism and migrate towards the target organ. Occasionally, a parasite may localise aberrantly in other organs or in a generalised fashion. The parasite finally localises where conditions are suitable for its existence.</p>
<p><strong>Mechanisms</strong> The actual mechanisms by which parasites can damage the host include:</p>
<p>(i) Mechanical effects such as pressure from an enlarging cyst or obstruction of vessels or hollow organs.</p>
<p>(ii) Invasion and destruction of host cells by the parasite itself.</p>
<p>(iii) Inflammatory reaction to the parasite or its products of metabolism.</p>
<p>(iv) Competition for host nutrients.</p>
<p>There is no specific incubation period for parasitic infection because it varies with the nature of the parasite, the activity, location and various stages of its life cycle within the host. Tissue inva-</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Introduction 913</p>
<p>son and destruction may cause fever, headache, pain, chills, nausea and vomiting. Pressure of growing parasites may give rise to pain; and in the brain, to various motor and sensory abnormalities. Parasites may obstruct the intestine, bile ducts, lymph channels and capillaries of the brain and cause various symptoms. Extensive anaemia may result from red cell destruction. Eosinophilia (15–50%) may be present in some parasitic infections. A generalized urticarial skin rash may appear as a result of hypersensitivity reaction to metabolic or lytic products of parasite absorbed in blood circulation.</p>
<p>The most common symptom of intestinal parasites is diarrhoea which may be bloody or purulent. If bowel mucosa is invaded, it may be indicated by cramping abdominal pain, as in hookworm or Schistosoma infections. Intestinal obstruction may result from heavy infection with Ascaris lumbricoides. Tapeworms (e.g. Taenia sp.) may cause weight loss in spite of increased appetite and food intake. Liver fluke infection may present in the form of hepatosplenomegaly.</p>
<p>Schistosoma japonicum can cause jaundice, while suprapubic pain, frequency of micturition and haematuria are highly suggestive of Schistosoma haematobium infection. Space-occupying cystic lesions of the liver, brain and other organs may be found in amoebiasis, echinococcosis, or in cysticercus infection caused by larval stage of Taenia solium.</p>
<p>Generalised constitutional symptoms are observed after infections with blood parasites. Fever, chills, night sweats, weight loss, myalgias are experienced in malaria, trypanosomiasis and leishmaniasis. Central nervous system is involved in African sleeping sickness by Trypanosoma, in toxoplasmosis or in malaria caused by Plasmodium falciparum. Huge swellings of the legs, arms and scrotum results from filariasis because the adult worm blocks the lymphatic vessels leading to extensive inflammation and fibrosis (Elephantiasis). Some commensal protozoa such as Chilomastix mesnili, Endolimax nana, Dientamoeba fragilis and Blastocystis hominis may produce diarrhoea in debilitated and immunocompromised patients.</p>
<p>Knowledge of geographical distribution of parasites is helpful in correlating the symptoms with the parasites. Although some parasites have a worldwide distribution, many are still localised in endemic areas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="criteria-for-diagnosis-of-parasitic-disease">Criteria for Diagnosis of Parasitic Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#criteria-for-diagnosis-of-parasitic-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In a clinical laboratory, parasitic infections can be diagnosed by the following methods.</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Microscopic examination Most of the intestinal, urinary and blood parasites can be detected and identified on the basis of their morphology by microscopic examination of stained or unstained preparations. Sometimes special techniques for specimen collection and preparation for microscopic examination may be required.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Immunological diagnosis Immunological techniques are being used more frequently because of the availability of more specific and sensitive reagents. These methods are also useful in epidemiology of parasitic diseases.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cultural methods This method is not in common use in parasitology. Very few parasites are cultured in a routine laboratory.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens-for-detection-of-parasites">Specimens for Detection of Parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens-for-detection-of-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most human parasites of clinical importance can be detected by microscopic examination of appropriate specimen either in an unstained preparation or by using various staining techniques. Table 1.2 shows the specimens which can be used to detect parasites. Occasionally, adult worms or their segments may also be revealed by visual (macroscopic) examination of the specimen. It is sometimes advisable to perform repeated examination of two or more specimens as the parasite may not always be present at one particular site.</p>
<p>The implementation of reliable laboratory processing techniques is an essential step in the diagnosis of parasitic infections by microscopic ex</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>914 Parasitology</p>
<p>amination of faeces, urine, blood, sputum, tissues and body fluids.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="parasites-of-medical-importance">PARASITES OF MEDICAL IMPORTANCE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#parasites-of-medical-importance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medically important human parasites can be broadly divided into two major groups, Protozoa and Helminths. Protozoa are single-celled, simple organisms, mainly consisting of a nucleus and cytoplasm while Helminths are multi-cellular, more developed organisms. A broad classification of human parasites is shown in Table 1.1. Parasites, types and forms found in various clinical specimens are listed in Table 1.2.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="characteristics-of-protozoa">Characteristics of Protozoa<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#characteristics-of-protozoa" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The protozoa are unicellular organisms, most of which are microscopic in size. The cells contain various organelles to perform functions necessary for life. The protozoan parasites are further divided into four classes:</p>
<ol>
<li>Class sarcodina (amoebae) includes parasites which move by means of cytoplasmic protrusions, the pseudopodia. Pathogenic, non-pathogenic as well as free living amoebae parasitise the</li>
</ol>

<p>human body. Pathogenic amoebae are the Entamoeba histolytica which mainly inhabit the intestinal tract, and some free living amoebae such as Naegleria fowleri and Acanthamoeba species, which can cause meningoencephalitis, especially in immuno-compromised patients.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>916 Parasitology</p>
<p>Amoeba consists of a shapeless mass of moving cytoplasm which is divided into granular endoplasm and clear ectoplasm. The movement is achieved by pushing out the ectoplasm to form pseudopodia or 'false feet'. The endoplasm then flows into it, thus facilitating the change in position. Reproduction is by simple binary fission. Most amoebae undergo encystment and form cysts when exposed to unfavourable conditions. The amoebae are transferred from one host to another in this form.</p>
<p>Although it is not a normal mode of reproduction, the nucleus may undergo division in the cyst, and during excystation under favourable conditions, can give rise to more than one amoebae.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Class mastigophora (flagellates) possesses organs of locomotion, called flagella, which are long, thin cytoplasmic extensions that may vary in number and position depending on the species. Reproduction is by binary fission. However, some flagellates have a more complex life cycle and may lose flagella in some phases.</li>
</ol>
<p>The flagellates may parasitise the intestinal tract, e.g. <em>Giardia lamblia</em> (G. intestinalis), the urogenital tract, e.g. <em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em>, or the blood stream e.g. haemoflagellates, <em>Trypanosoma</em> and <em>Leishmania</em>.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Class ciliata (ciliates) includes parasites which move with the help of cilia, which are short hair like extensions of the cytoplasm covering the surface of the organism. This group contains only one parasite which is pathogenic to humans, namely <em>Balantidium coli</em>. This parasite reproduces by binary fission and can form cysts. It characteristically has two dissimilar nuclei, a macronucleus and a micronucleus.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Class coccidia includes intestinal parasites such as <em>Cryptosporidium</em> species and <em>Isospora belli</em>, blood parasites such as <em>Plasmodium</em> species and tissue parasites such as <em>Toxoplasma gondii</em>. The coccidians have complex life cycles. They reproduce asexually by a process called schizogony and sexually by sporogony.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Medically, protozoa can be grouped as intestinal protozoa, and blood and tissue protozoa.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="characteristics-of-helminths">Characteristics of Helminths<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#characteristics-of-helminths" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Helminths that infect humans belong to two phyla: the Platyhelminths or flatworms and the Nemathelminths or round worms. Platyhelminths are further subdivided into cestodes and trematodes.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-342c67f7.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<ol>
<li>Cestodes or tapeworms have a flat, tape-like body which is made up of a head or scolex, a small neck and several segments or proglottids. There is no body cavity. Adult worms live in the intestinal tract. The worm is attached to the host with the help of suckers and sometimes, hooks on the head. The proglottids originate from the neck. The newly formed proglottids are small and immature. As they mature, they grow in size and maturity. The mature proglottids contain fully developed reproductive organs. Tapeworms are hermaphroditic i.e. the male and the female organs are present in the same individual. Each mature proglottid contains testes, ovary and uterus. The eggs are produced and stored within the segment. A mature segment with eggs is described as a gravid proglottid. The gravid segment becomes detached and eggs are released when it ruptures. There is no mouth or digestive system. The nutrients are absorbed through the body surface.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Introduction 917</p>
<p>The tapeworms of medical importance are <em>Taenia solium</em>, <em>Taenia saginata</em>, <em>Hymenolepis nana</em>, <em>Echinococcus granulosus</em> and <em>Diphyllobothrium latum</em>.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Trematodes or flukes are unsegmented, flat, leaf-like worms, schistosomes being the exception. They attach to the host with the help of two suckers, the oral sucker surrounding the mouth, and the ventral sucker on the ventral surface. There is no body cavity. The digestive system consists of a mouth and an oesophagus, which divides to form two intestinal caeca. There is no anus. The excretory system consists of excretory cells called flame cells, collecting tubules, and an excretory pore. Like cestodes, the trematodes also are hermaphrodites with the exception of schistosomes. Most of the trematodes produce eggs which have lid or operculum. The development of eggs takes place in water. Some examples of medically important trematodes include <em>Fasciola hepatica</em>, <em>Fasciolopsis buski</em>, <em>Paragonimus westermani</em>, <em>Schistosoma haematobium</em>, <em>S. mansoni</em>, <em>S. japonicum</em>, and <em>Heterophyes heterophyes</em>.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nematodes or roundworms are cylindrical, non-segmented worms that taper at both ends. The skin or cuticle is shiny and may have spines or ridges. The digestive system is a simple tube which starts at the mouth and ends in an anus. The mouth is surrounded by lips which are called papillae. In some worms, e.g. hookworms, the mouth opens into a buccal cavity which contains either plates or teeth for cutting. The nematodes are unisexual, i.e. the sexes are separate. Females are larger in size than males. Generally, the females have straight, pointed tails whereas in males, they are curved or coiled. A testis is located at the distal end of a long tube in the male and terminates in copulatory organs. These vary according to species and may consist of one or two projections called spicules, genital papilae, caudal alae or a copulatory bursa. Female worms have one or two tubular ovaries leading to a uterus or two uteri. The uterus opens to the exterior through a vulva.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The female worms are either oviparous i.e. lay eggs, or viviparous i.e. produce larvae. The eggs, when discharged, may directly hatch into infective larvae. But some species require special conditions for hatching, and two or three developmental stages before becoming infective larvae. The cuticle is shed at each stage of development by a process known as moulting.</p>
<p>The medically important intestinal nematodes are <em>Ascaris lumbricoides</em>, <em>Ancylostoma duodenale</em>, <em>Necator americanus</em>, <em>Strongyloides stercoralis</em>, <em>Enterobius vermicularis</em> and <em>Trichuris trichiura</em>.</p>
<p>The tissue nematodes are <em>Trichinella spiralis</em>, <em>Wuchereria bancrofti</em>, <em>Brugia malayi</em>, <em>Loa loa</em>, <em>Onchocerca volvulus</em> and <em>Dracunculus medinensis</em>.</p>
<p>Basic differences of cestodes, trematodes and nematodes are shown in Table 1.3.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Features</th><th>Cestodes (Tapeworms)</th><th>Trematodes (Flukes)</th><th>Nematodes (Roundworms)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Shape</td><td>Flat, tape-like, segmented</td><td>Flat, leaf-like (except schistosomes) and unsegmented</td><td>Cylindrical and unsegmented</td></tr><tr><td>Anterior end</td><td>Suckers and often hooks on head. No mouth is present</td><td>Suckers on body but no hooks. A mouth is present</td><td>No suckers or hooks. A mouth is present</td></tr><tr><td>Digestive tract</td><td>Absent</td><td>Present, but no anus</td><td>Present with anus</td></tr><tr><td>Sexes</td><td>Hermaphrodite</td><td>Hermaphrodite except <em>Schistosoma</em> species</td><td>Separate male and female worms</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 2</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-ea4eb2f7.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-techniques-for-intestinal-parasites">Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-techniques-for-intestinal-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The most commonly used specimen for the detection of intestinal parasites is faeces. Faecal specimens are examined for the presence of trophozoites and cysts of protozoa, and eggs and larvae of helminths, as shown in Table 1.2. Whole adult worms or segments of some worms may also be seen. Trophozoites, cysts, eggs and larvae can be seen only with the microscope, but the adult worms or segments of tapeworms can be seen with the naked eye. Samples of faeces must be properly collected, processed and examined for the detection of parasites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="collection-of-faeces">COLLECTION OF FAECES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#collection-of-faeces" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A faecal specimen should be collected in a clean, wide-mouthed container with a tightly fitted lid. A waxed cardboard box with an overlapping lid or a plastic cup with a tight fitting lid is best. Tin boxes or glass jars may also be used, but containers such as match boxes are not suitable.</p>
<p>The specimen should be collected directly into the container and should not be contaminated with water that may contain free-living organisms, or with urine which can cause trophozoites to lose their motility or to undergo lysis. Sufficient quantity of the specimen should be obtained for satisfactory examination (about 4–5 gm). The container should be labelled with patient’s name or number; and date and time of collection.</p>
<p>While handling the faecal specimen, it must be remembered that it can contain potentially infectious micro-organisms. Loose or semiformed specimens (refer to visual examination) should be examined within 30 minutes to 1 hour of collection. Trophozoites of protozoa cannot survive for longer periods outside the body. The formed specimen must be examined on the same day of the collection. If these time limits cannot be met, a portion of the stool should be preserved by one of the method described below. The rest of the specimen should be refrigerated at 3–5°C and stored in closed containers to prevent dessication. At this temperature protozoan cysts, helminthic eggs and larvae can survive for several days. Specimens of faeces should never be incubated or frozen before examination.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="number-of-specimens">NUMBER OF SPECIMENS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#number-of-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The number of specimens required to detect intestinal parasites will depend on the quality of the specimen, the severity of infection and the accuracy of the examination performed. For a routine examination for parasites before treatment, examination of three consecutive specimens is recommended. However, examination of specimens collected on alternate days shows a higher percentage of positive findings. A maximum of six speci</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites 919</p>
<p>mens, collected within six to ten days, may be required if intestinal amoebiasis or giardiasis is suspected. Sometimes, it may be necessary for the patient to take a mild laxative before collecting the third sample.</p>
<p>After a patient has received treatment for a parasitic infection, the faeces should be rechecked for absence of parasites. A gap of three to four weeks for protozoan infection, five to six weeks for Taenia infection and three to four weeks for other helminths is recommended for rechecking. Medications containing mineral oil, bismuth, antibiotics, antimalarials or other chemical substances may interfere with the detection of parasites in faeces. Therefore, examination of faeces must be postponed at least by one week after such therapeutic procedures have been completed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2c76ad78">♦ PRESERVATION OF FAECAL SPECIMENS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2c76ad78" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sometimes it may be impossible to examine a faecal specimen within specified limits of time due to constraints such as unavoidable delay in processing the sample. The faecal specimen, in such cases, should be placed in an appropriate preservative so that the morphology of parasites if present in the specimen, is maintained. Such a preservation is also useful if the specimen is required for teaching purposes. The preservatives which are used most frequently are:</p>
<p>Polyvinyl alcohol (PVA) fixative This fixative is widely used because it allows concentration procedures and preparation of permanent stained smears. It is particularly useful for protozoan cysts.</p>
<p><strong>Formula</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>95% Ethyl alcohol</th><th>62.5 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>PVA</td><td>10.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Mercuric chloride, saturated aqueous</td><td>125.0 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Glacial acetic acid</td><td>10.0 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Glycerin</td><td>3.0 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>It should be used in the ratio of three parts of fixative to one part of faecal material. The fixative remains stable for long periods at room temperature if kept in sealed containers.</p>
<p><strong>10% Formal saline</strong> It is a time-honoured fixative and preserves protozoan cysts, helminth eggs and larvae for long periods. Hot formal-saline at 60°C can prevent further development of eggs to infective stage.</p>
<p><strong>Formula</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Sodium chloride, 0.85%</th><th>900 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Formaldehyde</td><td>100 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>It should be used in the same proportion as the PVA fixative with faecal material. The disadvantage of formal-saline is that the faeces cannot be used for permanent stained preparations.</p>
<p><strong>Sodium acetate formalin (SAF) fixative</strong> This also is a very useful preservative, both for concentration methods and for permanent stained smears. The only disadvantage is that it is difficult to adhere the material to the slide after it is mixed with SAF. Mayer’s albumin (refer to Histopathological Techniques) has been recommended as an adhesive for this purpose. With this fixative, staining the smears with haematoxylin gives better results than with trichrome stain.</p>
<p><strong>Formula</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Formaldehyde</th><th>0.6 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Glacial acetic acid</td><td>0.3 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Sodium acetate</td><td>0.225 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>13.88 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>It is used in the same proportion with faecal material as PVA.</p>
<p><strong>Merthiolate-iodine formalin (MIF) solution</strong> MIF solution is useful especially in field surveys as it preserves the morphology of the parasites very well. However, permanent stained smears can not be prepared from MIF fixed specimens. Another disadvantage is that the iodine component makes the reagent unstable.</p>
<p><strong>Formula</strong></p>
<p><strong>Solution I</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Merthiolate (Thiomersal), 1:1000</th><th>200 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Formaldehyde, 10% aqueous</td><td>25 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Glycerol</td><td>5 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>250 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>920 Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="solution-ii">Solution II<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solution-ii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Distilled water</th><th>100 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Potassium iodide</td><td>10 g</td></tr><tr><td>Iodine crystals</td><td>5 g</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Solution I and II must be prepared and stored separately.</p>
<p>Just before use, add 1 ml of solution II to 13 ml of solution I and mix with 1–2g of fresh faeces.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b9d41142">♦ VISUAL OR MACROSCOPIC EXAMINATION OF FAECES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b9d41142" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Before processing the specimen of faeces, it should be visually examined. Its colour, consistency and presence of blood, pus, mucus or parasites should be reported.</p>
<p><strong>Colour</strong> Normally, stool is brown in colour. Variation from this colour may occur under certain conditions.</p>
<p>Reddish colour may be due to bleeding from the lower gastro-intestinal (G.I.) tract. Consumption of beet-root may also give a red colour to the stool.</p>
<p>Black-tarry colour may be due to bleeding from the upper gastro-intestinal tract or due to consumption of iron.</p>
<p>Clay coloured stool is seen in obstructive jaundice or after barium sulphate meal.</p>
<p>Green stool may result from the consumption of leafy vegetables such as spinach or sometimes due to oral antibiotic therapy.</p>
<p><strong>Consistency</strong> A normal stool specimen is formed or semiformed in consistency. Loose or watery stool may be seen in diarrhoea. Trophozoites of intestinal protozoa are usually seen in loose or watery specimens while cysts are found in formed and semiformed ones. Eggs of helminths may be found in any consistency. Large amounts of mushy, foul smelling, frothy specimens are seen in giardiasis and other conditions associated with malabsorption.</p>
<p><strong>Blood</strong> The presence of blood in or on the specimen must always be reported. Fresh, bright red blood is often from the lower gastro-intestinal tract whereas dark red colour may indicate bleeding from the upper gastro-intestinal tract. When very small amounts of blood are being passed in faeces, it may not be visible macroscopically. Such specimens are said to contain occult (hidden) blood. Some of the causes for the presence of occult blood include iron deficiency anaemia, peptic ulcer or cancer of the gastro-intestinal tract. In suspected cases, a special request is sent for occult blood test.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tests-for-occult-blood">Tests for Occult Blood<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tests-for-occult-blood" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Chemical tests for occult blood involve oxidation of chromogenic compounds such as benzidine, O-toluidine or 4-aminoantipyrine catalyzed by haemoglobin or its derivatives. This oxidation is indicated by a coloured reaction to produce blue or blue-green colour.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>(i) (a) Reagent using 4-aminoantipyrine</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>4-aminoantipyrine</th><th>0.4 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Alcohol, 95% (v/v)</td><td>15 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Acetic acid, 10% (v/v)</td><td>1 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Prepare the reagent just before use.</p>
<p><strong>(b) Reagent using benzidine or O-toluidine</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Benzidine or O-toluidine</th><th>0.4 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Glacial acetic acid</td><td>10 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Caution</strong> Both benzidine and O-toluidine are carcinogenic.</p>
<p><strong>(ii) Hydrogen peroxide, 10 volumes solution (i.e. 1 volume of H₂O₂ will give 10 volumes of oxygen on complete degradation).</strong></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Emulsify a pea-size (10–15 mm in diameter) specimen of faeces in 7 ml of distilled water. Allow the faecal particles to settle and transfer 5 ml of the supernatant to another test tube. Layer it carefully with 5 ml of reagent (i). Do not mix.</li>
<li>Add 10 drops of 10 vol. hydrogen peroxide solution.</li>
<li>Allow it to stand for 1 minute.</li>
<li>Appearance of blue colour at the junction of the specimen and reagent (i) indicates a positive reaction.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites 921</p>
<ul>
<li>Run pure distilled water, and distilled water mixed with a trace (0.05 ml) of blood as negative and positive controls simultaneously.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Interpretation</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Negative</th><th>No colour change</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>+</td><td>Pale blue</td></tr><tr><td>++</td><td>Dark blue</td></tr><tr><td>+++</td><td>Blue-black</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The negative control shows no colour change while the positive control shows an appropriate colour change depending on the amount of blood used.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; False positive test may be obtained if the faecal specimen contains meat, fish or green vegetables. The patient should thus be advised to avoid these foods for one day before the collection of specimen.</p>
<p><strong>Pus</strong>
Fairly large quantities of pus may be passed out in stools of patients with inflammation of the G.I. tract as in bacillary dysentery or chronic ulcerative colitis. <em>Amoebic colitis</em> is generally not associated with large amounts of pus. Whenever present, a portion of the pus must always be included in the microscopic examination.</p>
<p><strong>Mucus</strong>
A normal stool does not contain mucus. Mucus may be associated with blood and pus in many inflammatory conditions of the G.I. tract, and other conditions such as neoplasm.</p>
<p><strong>Parasites</strong>
Adult worms such as <em>Enterobius vermicularis</em> or <em>Ascaris lumbricoides</em>, and tapeworm segments may be found on the surface or in the stool. They should be identified and reported.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="159536a">♦ MICROSCOPIC EXAMINATION (WET MOUNTS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#159536a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The easiest and the simplest technique for the direct microscopic examination of faeces is wet mount. Wet mounts can be prepared directly from the faecal material in saline and iodine. Other useful wet mounts are buffered methylene blue (BMB) and eosin. The saline wet mount is used for the preliminary microscopic examination of faeces to detect protozoan trophozoites and cysts; and helminth larvae and eggs. A characteristic motility of a parasite can be used for its identification. Excessive cellular exudate in the faeces in the form of pus (white blood cells) or blood (red blood cells), macrophages or any other significant material such as charcot-leyden crystals can also be detected in wet mounts.</p>
<p>In the iodine wet mount, most cysts can usually be specifically identified because the iodine stains their nuclei and glycogen, if present. However, the parasites are not motile in iodine. BMB wet mount is useful only when amoebic trophozoites are seen in a saline wet mount of a fresh, unpreserved faecal specimen. It stains amoebic trophozoites, but not the cysts, and also does not the trophozoites and cysts of flagellates. Eosin wet mount is very good for the initial detection of cysts and trophozoites. Eosin does not stain them, but they can be much more easily seen against a pink-red background of eosin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="preparation-of-wet-mounts">Preparation of Wet Mounts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-wet-mounts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Saline, physiological (0.85%)</p>
<p>(ii) Lugol’s iodine</p>
<p><strong>Stock (5%) solution</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Iodine</th><th>5 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Potassium iodide</td><td>10 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>100 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Dissolve the potassium iodide in about 30 ml of water and add the iodine. Mix until dissolved and make up the volume with distilled water. Store in a brown bottle.</p>
<p><strong>Working iodine solution (1%)</strong></p>
<p>Lungol’s iodine, stock (5%) solution 5 ml</p>
<p>Saline (0.85%) 20 ml</p>
<p>Prepare fresh every two weeks.</p>
<p>(iii) <strong>Buffered</strong> <strong>Methylene</strong> <strong>Blue</strong> (<strong>BMB</strong>)</p>
<p><strong>Solution</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Solution A</th><th>Glacial acetic acid</th><th>1.2 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td></td><td>98.8 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>922 Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="solution-b">Solution B<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solution-b" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Sodium acetate (CH₃COONa) or Sodium acetate, crystalline (CH₃COONa, 3H₂O)</th><th>1.6 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>100 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="working-solution">Working Solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#working-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<p>This solution will keep indefinitely.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iv-eosin-0-5-aqueous">(iv) Eosin (0.5%), aqueous<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-eosin-0-5-aqueous" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Place a drop of saline in the centre of the left half of the slide and place a drop of iodine solution in the centre of the right half of the slide.</li>
<li>With an applicator stick, pick up a small portion (about 2 mg) of faeces from an appropriate site (see the note below), and mix it with saline on the slide to form a uniform suspension. In the same way, prepare a suspension of faeces in the iodine on the slide.</li>
<li>Cover the drop of saline suspension with a coverslip by holding it at an angle and lowering it gently on to the slide to reduce formation of air bubbles. Cover the iodine suspension with another coverslip, taking care not to mix it with the saline suspension.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="note">Note<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#note" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Strands of mucus: While preparing a wet mount, it is important to select the correct portion of the specimen. In case of formed faeces, include the material from inside as well as from the surface of the specimen. If mucus is present, prepare a separate wet mount in saline with the mucus. Trophozoites are more readily found in mucus than in other portions. In case of loose or watery stool, a direct drop of the specimen may be examined without saline or iodine.</li>
<li>BMB or Eosin mount should be prepared in the same way as saline or iodine mount.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examination">Examination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Focus on the wet mount using a low power (10X) objective.</li>
<li>Regulate the light with the substage condenser, the diaphragm and the light source. Since most of the parasites are pale or colourless, too much or too little light may not be useful. Lowering the condenser and closing the diaphragm partially can give adequate light.</li>
<li>Examine the entire coverslip in a systematic order. Focus the objective on the top left hand corner and move slide slowly up and down or backwards and forwards (Fig. 2.1). When any parasite or suspicious</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-e800db6c.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
Fig. 2.1 Preparation and examination of wet mounts</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites 923</p>
<p>material is observed, change to the high-dry objective (40X). Increase the light by opening the substage diaphragm to observe the detailed morphology. Examine each microscope field carefully, focussing up and down, before moving to the next field. When a slowly motile trophozoite is suspected, observe for at least 15 seconds to confirm the motility.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="identification-of-parasites-in-wet-mounts">IDENTIFICATION OF PARASITES IN WET MOUNTS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-parasites-in-wet-mounts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="eggs-of-helminths">Eggs of Helminths<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#eggs-of-helminths" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Eggs can be easily detected and identified in saline mounts due to their larger sizes and characteristic features in morphology. It is not necessary to stain them. Most of the eggs are large enough to be identified with the low power (10X) objective. Morphology and relative sizes of intestinal parasites is shown in Fig. 2.2. Figure 2.3 describes a scheme for identification of helminth eggs based on their morphology.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="criteria-for-identification-of-eggs-of-helminths">Criteria for identification of eggs of helminths<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#criteria-for-identification-of-eggs-of-helminths" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>There are three groups of medically important helminths: Nematodes (round worms), cestodes (tape worms) and trematodes (flukes). Usually, the diagnosis is made by the detection of eggs and larvae, and occasionally by the adult worms or their segments in faeces. In majority of cases, the eggs are used for identification. The following criteria are used for the differentiation of helminths eggs.</p>
<p>Size The length and the width of eggs are generally within a specific range.</p>
<p>Shape The eggs are rigid and each species has its own characteristic shape.</p>
<p>Thickness of the egg-shell Some species have eggs with thick shell (e.g. Ascaris) while some have thin shells (e.g. hookworm).</p>
<p>Colour Some eggs are yellow or brown (bile-stained) in colour (e.g. Ascaris, Trichuris, Taenia), some eggs are colourless (e.g. hookworm, Enterobius).</p>
<p>Stage of development In some helminths, the egg consists of a single cell (e.g. Ascaris), some may have several cells (e.g. hookworm), while some species are embryonated i.e. they contain a larva (e.g. Enterobius) when passed in faeces.</p>
<p>Presence of special characteristics Features such as operculum (lid), spine, plugs, hooklets, polar filaments, mammillated outer coat help in identification of the parasite. (Refer to Figs 2.2 and 2.3 for identification of helminth eggs).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="larvae-of-helminths">Larvae of Helminths<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#larvae-of-helminths" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>There are three species of helminths which may show presence of larvae in faeces, Strongyloides stercoralis and hookworms (Ancylostoma duodenale and Necator americanus). The larvae can occur either as rhabditiform larvae (first non-infective stage) or as filariform larvae (infective stage). The rhabditiform larvae are seen in freshly passed stool and later develop into filariform larvae after 12–14 hours.</p>
<p>The larvae are alive and actively motile in saline, and therefore it is difficult to observe the characteristic features. The larvae die in iodine mounts and the features can be clearly seen at 40x magnification. The rhabditiform larva of Strongyloides has a short mouth opening and a prominent genital primordium, while that of hookworm has a long mouth opening and a very small, almost invisible, genital primordium. The filariform larvae are longer than the rhabditiform larvae (Figs 2.4 a, b, c). Hookworm larvae are sheathed. In both the species, rhabditiform larvae have short oesophagus ending in a bulb shape whereas the oesophagus of filariform larvae is long and cylindrical.</p>
<p>Tables 2.1, 2.2 and 2.3 show important features of most common intestinal nematodes, cestodes and trematodes respectively.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="intestinal-protozoa">Intestinal Protozoa<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intestinal-protozoa" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>

<p>930 Parasitology</p>
<p>stages: vegetative or trophozoite stage and dormant or cyst stage. Both the stages may be found in the same stool specimen, but generally, trophozoites are found in loose faeces while cysts are present in formed faeces. Although trophozoites and cysts can be identified in saline and iodine mounts, sometimes permanent staining preparations may be necessary for identification of species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="saline-mounts">Saline Mounts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#saline-mounts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In saline wet mounts, trophozoites and cysts of amoebae, flagellates and ciliates may be seen. Cysts appear as round or oval refractile structures while the trophozoites are round or irregular in shape. Trophozoites of flagellates are typically pear-shaped and elongated. Motility of trophozoites may be seen only in freshly passed specimens.</p>
<p>Protozoa may be detected with the low power (10x) objective, but a high-power dry objective (40x) is necessary to identify them. It is necessary to carefully regulate the illumination of the microscope. Too much or too little light might miss the parasites in the specimen. Systematic examination of the whole area of the coverslip while focussing up and down to see all the levels or layers of the specimen will minimize the chances of missing the parasites.</p>
<p>Amoebic trophozoites in saline mounts: In freshly passed faeces, motility of amoebic trophozoites may be visible under high dry objective. <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em> is the pathogenic intestinal amoeba and needs to be distinguished from other non-pathogenic ones (Tables 2.4 and 2.5, Fig. 2.5). The trophozoite of <em>E.histolytica</em> moves quickly in one direction and forms pseudopodia rapidly. If a trophozoite shows such a movement and contains red blood cells in the cytoplasm, it is likely to be <em>E.histolytica</em>. It may be necessary to confirm its identity by BMB (buffered methylene blue) mount or by permanent stained preparation.</p>
<p>Amoebic cysts in saline mounts: Some amoebic cysts can be detected with the 40x objective as shiny, round objects with a diameter roughly equal to the size of one to three red blood cells. Nuclei are not very clearly seen in saline preparations, but are well seen in iodine mounts. The appearance of the nucleus is important in differentiating species of amoebae. Therefore, if cyst-like structures are seen in saline, iodine mounts must be examined. Chromatoid bodies are rod shaped structures, present in the cysts of <em>E.histolytica</em> and <em>E.coli</em>. and are characteristic for each species. The chromatoid bodies in <em>E.histolytica</em> have blunt rounded ends while those of <em>E.coli</em> have pointed ends (Fig. 2.5). If any cyst is detected, it should be measured by using micrometry.</p>
<p>Trophozoites and cysts of flagellates in saline mounts: Although the nuclei and flagella may not be visible in saline mounts, trophozoites of intestinal flagellates can be identified by the type of motility in saline. The trophozoites of the pathogenic species, <em>Giardia intestinalis</em> (<em>Giardia lamblia</em>) move like a falling leaf and flip back and forth. <em>Chilomastix mesnili</em> is non-pathogenic, and its trophozoites rotate as they move. <em>Trichomonas hominis</em> trophozoite has a jerky movement.</p>
<p>Out of the intestinal flagellates, <em>Dientamoeba fragilis</em> and <em>Trichomonas</em> species do not form cysts. The cysts, especially those of <em>Giardia intestinalis</em> have a distinct morphology, which is clearer in iodine mounts. Figures 2.6 and 2.7 show the key for the identification of amoebic and flagellate cysts and trophozoites.</p>
<p>Ciliates in saline mounts: <em>Balantidium coli</em> is the only ciliate parasitic to humans and the infection is not common. Its trophozoites are large (50-200μm x 40-70 μm) and very actively motile. Therefore they can be very easily seen in saline mounts under the low power objective. The cysts are 45-75 μm in diameter. The characteristics of both are shown in Tables 2.6, 2.7 and Fig. 2.8. They do not stain well with iodine or permanent stains. Therefore, saline mount should be used for detection.</p>
<p>Coccidia in saline mounts: <em>Isospora belli</em> and <em>Cryptosporidium</em> are intestinal coccidian parasites. <em>Blastocystis hominis</em> is tentatively classified as a coccidian parasite. They are rarely pathogenic</p>

<p><em>Isospora belli</em> oocysts are oval (32 × 16 μm) and contain a central individual mass of protoplasm, which may later divide into two (Fig. 2.9). <em>Blastocystis hominis</em> is round in shape, measures 6 × 40 μm and is characterized by a large mass in the centre with refractile granules around the edge.</p>
<p>Characteristics of these coccidia are shown in Table 2.8. Cryptosporidium oocysts can be identified only by special staining methods.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iodine-mounts">Iodine Mounts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iodine-mounts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Iodine mounts are useful for the identification of the cysts of amoebae and flagellates. They can be detected with the 10X objective, but the high dry objective must be used to examine them properly.</p>
<p>In the iodine wet mount, the cytoplasm of the cysts stains yellow or light brown, and nuclei dark brown. The arrangement of the peripheral chromatin in the nucleus and the position of the karyosome can also be observed. (Peripheral chromatin is present only in the cysts of <em>Entamoeba</em>). Sometimes young cysts contain glycogen which is seen as a dark brown mass in iodine. The filaments inside the flagellate cysts can also be seen in the iodine mount.</p>
<p>Iodine mounts usually enable specific identification of cysts. However, it may be necessary to use permanent stains for definite identification.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-24e30027.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg">
