<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>942 Parasitology</p>
<p>Amoebic trophozoites and cysts in iodine mounts: In an iodine mount, trophozoites of amoeba do not provide any additional information than in the saline mount. As the trophozoites die in iodine, the characteristic motility cannot be observed. The cysts stain with iodine and provide additional information for their identification.</p>
<p>To observe the cysts under the microscope, switch to the high dry (40X) objective to see the details necessary to identify the species. Cysts of <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em> measure 10-15 μm in diameter. When mature, the cysts have four nuclei with a central karyosome and uniform peripheral nuclear chromatin. The immature cysts of <em>E. histolytica</em> may have one, two or four nuclei, and generally contain a brown glycogen mass. <em>E. coli</em> cysts are larger in size, measuring 15-30 μm. The mature cysts have eight nuclei. They are usually on different planes and it is necessary to focus up and down to see all of them. The nuclei have irregular peripheral chromatin and the karyosome is not centrally placed.</p>
<p>Cysts of <em>E. hartmanni</em> are similar to those of <em>E. histolytica</em>, but smaller in size (7-9 μm). Cysts of <em>Iodamoeba butschlii</em> are irregular in shape and show a compact mass of glycogen which stains brown in iodine. They have a single nucleus. <em>Endolimax nana</em> cysts are smaller than <em>E. histolytica</em> cysts and possess four nuclei that look like punched-out holes (Figs 2.5 and 2.6).</p>
<p>Trophozoites and cysts of flagellates in iodine mounts. Trophozoites, if not identified in saline mounts, should be observed in a stained smear for identification. The cysts of <em>Giardia intestinalis</em> (<em>G. lamblia</em>) and of <em>Chilomastix mesnili</em> stain very well with iodine for easy identification (Table 2.7, Fig. 2.8). Flagellates <em>Dientamoeba fragilis</em> and <em>Trichomonas</em> species do not form cysts.</p>
<p>Trophozoites and cysts of ciliates and coccidia can be identified in saline mounts. Therefore, iodine mounts do not have a special significance in their identification.</p>
<p>Buffered methylene blue (BMB) wet mount for amoebic trophozoites: If an amoebic trophozoite is suspected to be present in the saline mount, a BMB wet mount should be prepared. BMB stains only trophozoites of amoebae. It does not stain amoebic cysts, and trophozoites and cysts of flagellates. In the amoebic trophozoites, the nucleus and the inclusions such as red blood cells or yeast cells stain dark blue while the cytoplasm stains light blue. However, the trophozoite may curl up, and no longer be motile.</p>
<p>Trophozoites of <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em> are about 12-60 μm in length. The nucleus appears as a dark blue circle of fine, peripheral chromatin granules and a dark dot, the karyosome, in the centre. The trophozoites of <em>Entamoeba coli</em> are about the same size as those of <em>E. histolytica</em>. The nucleus has peripheral chromatin which is irregular and the karyosome is not centrally placed (Table 2.4, Fig. 2.5).</p>
<p>Eosin wet mount for the detection of trophozoites and cysts: Although the eosin does not stain the living amoebae or cysts, they can be much more easily detected against the pink-red background of eosin preparation. Once detected, they can be identified by other methods.</p>
<p>Table 2.9 summarizes the characteristics of protozoa seen in various wet mounts.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-parasitic-elements">Non-parasitic Elements<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-parasitic-elements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In addition to the parasites and normal debris in faeces, the microscopic examination of the faecal specimen may show various other cellular and non-cellular structures, such as:</p>
<p>White blood cells: Polymorphonuclear leucocytes or pus cells may be present as a result of inflammatory response as in bacillary dysentery or in amoebic dysentery. If not observed carefully, they can be mistaken for amoebic cysts. The nuclei of pus cells appear as three to four rings, the cytoplasm is ragged and the cell membrane is often not visible. Amoebic cysts on the other hand, have a distinct, rigid cell wall.</p>
<p>Macrophages and epithelial cells: Macrophages are present in bacterial or parasitic infections and can be confused with amoebic trophozoites. Macrophages show a slight amoeboid movement.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites 943</p>
<p>Table 2.9 Characteristics of protozoa seen in different types of preparations (WHO reference)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Temporary preparation</th><th></th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>Saline</td><td>BMBa</td><td>Iodineb</td><td>Permanent stain (Trichrome)c</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Amocbae</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Trophozoites</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Motility</td><td>+</td><td></td><td>NA</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Cytoplasm</td><td>+</td><td>-</td><td></td><td></td><td>-</td></tr><tr><td>Inclusions</td><td>+</td><td>+</td><td></td><td></td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td>Nucleus</td><td>-</td><td>+</td><td></td><td></td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>+</td><td></td><td></td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td>Cysts</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Nuclei</td><td>-</td><td>NA</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Chromatoid bodies</td><td>+</td><td></td><td>+</td><td></td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td>Glycogen</td><td>-</td><td></td><td>+d</td><td></td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>+</td><td></td><td>-e</td></tr><tr><td>Flagellates</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Trophozoites</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Motility</td><td>+</td><td>NA</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Shape</td><td>+</td><td></td><td>-</td><td>-</td><td>-</td></tr><tr><td>Nucleus</td><td>-</td><td></td><td>+</td><td>+</td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>+</td><td>+</td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td>Cysts</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Shape</td><td>+</td><td>NA</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Nuclei</td><td>-</td><td></td><td>+</td><td>+</td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td>*Fibrils</td><td>+</td><td></td><td>+</td><td>+</td><td>+</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>aBMB stain is used only for living amoebic trophozoites.
bIodine is used to stain cysts, although flagellate trophozoites may also stain
cPermanent staining techniques are not routinely used in diagnostic laboratories, but are reserved for special circumstances (e.g. diagnosis of cryptosporidioisis).
dChromatoid bodies in amoebic cysts are better seen in saline mounts than in iodine mounts. They are best seen in permanent stained smears.
eGlcogen is dissolved during the staining process, and in permanently stained smears only a clear space (vacuole) will be seen.
*Fibrils (filaments) can sometimes be seen in saline mounts
NA: Not applicable</p>
<p>and may contain red blood cells appearing similar to trophozoites of E.histolytica.</p>
<p>Red blood cells Their presence indicates ulceration or other haemorrhagic conditions in the gastro-intestinal tract.</p>
<p>Charcot-leyden crystals They may be found in intestinal coccidiosis (e.g. Isospora belli infection), paragonimiasis, and sometimes with amoebic dysentery, hookworm infection and other parasitic infections.</p>
<p>Fat globules A large number of fat globules may be seen in malabsorption syndrome.</p>
<p>Fungi Candida species and other yeasts, and yeast-like fungi may be normally present in faeces. They are round to oval in shape and measure 4-8 μm. Large numbers of budding yeast cells in a fresh or freshly preserved stool should be reported as it may indicate a potential source for a systemic infection, particularly in an immunocompromised patient. The presence of branching pseudohyphae</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>944 Parasitology</p>
<p>in a fresh specimen may indicate pathogenic <em>Candida</em> species and should be reported.</p>
<p>Plant material Plant or vegetable fibres may be irregular in shape or may appear as long, thin spirals and could also be mistaken for nematode larvae. However, the root hairs tend to be clear and refractile, while larvae stain with iodine, revealing the internal structures. Plant cells range in size from 15–150 µm, and may also be confused with <em>Ascaris</em> eggs. However, they have thick, smooth walls and are not as symmetrical as helminth eggs. Pollen grains are thick walled, symmetrical structures that stain very darkly with iodine. They may be round or tri-lobed and 15–20 µm in diameter, resembling eggs of <em>Taenia</em> species.</p>
<p>Table 2.10 and Fig. 2.10 show the artefacts and other confusing cells in faeces.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reporting-the-microscopic-examination-of-wet-mounts">Reporting the Microscopic Examination of Wet Mounts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reporting-the-microscopic-examination-of-wet-mounts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>During the microscopic examination of wet mounts prepared from faecal specimens, count the number of each type of parasite in the entire wet mount (preparation) and report as follows:</p>
<p>1 to 3 parasites per preparation : Scanty
4 to 10 parasites per preparation : Few
11 to 20 parasites per preparation : Moderate number</p>

<p>21 to 40 parasites per preparation : Many
More than 40 parasites per preparation : Very many
Report pus cells, red cells, Charcot-Leyden crystals, fat globules, etc. in the same way as many, moderate number, or few, per high power field (hpf).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="supplementary-techniques">SUPPLEMENTARY TECHNIQUES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#supplementary-techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Other techniques have been developed to detect and identify intestinal parasites in addition to direct wet mounts. The two most commonly used techniques are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Concentration methods for recovering cysts, eggs and larvae.</li>
<li>Permanent staining techniques for identifying trophozoites and cysts of protozoa.</li>
</ol>
<p>These techniques and a few others are described in the following sections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="concentration-methods">CONCENTRATION METHODS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#concentration-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It may be necessary to use concentration methods for the detection of faecal parasites for the following reasons:</p>
<p>(i) The parasites are not detected in the direct microscopic examination but the symptoms of intestinal parasitic infection still persist.
(ii) The eggs of parasites such as <em>Taenia</em> species or <em>Schistosoma</em> species are usually few in number, and therefore, may have been missed out in the direct wet mount.
(iii) After treatment, it is necessary to check if it has been effective. Even if a very small number of eggs are still present in the faeces, a concentration method will detect them.
(iv) To investigate the prevalence and incidence of parasitic infection for epidemiological purposes.</p>
<p>There are two types of concentration methods, <em>floation techniques</em> and <em>sedimentation techniques</em>. These methods employ the use of specific gravity to separate most of the faecal debris from the parasites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-floatation-techniques">1. Floatation Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-floatation-techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A floatation technique uses a liquid with high specific gravity for the separation of protozoan cysts and certain helminthic eggs and larvae from the faecal debris. The parasites remain in the surface film while the debris remains at the bottom. However, some helminth eggs, e.g. operculated eggs or very dense eggs such as unfertilized eggs of <em>Ascaris</em>, may not float. Increasing the specific gravity of the solution may result in the distortion of eggs and cysts. It is thus advisable to examine both the surface film and the sediment, for the recovery of all the parasites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-zinc-sulphate-floatation-technique">(i) Zinc sulphate floatation technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-zinc-sulphate-floatation-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This technique is useful for the recovery of protozoan cysts and some helminth eggs. Operculated eggs get ruptured in the zinc sulphate solution. Other heavy eggs such as unfertilized <em>Ascaris</em> eggs or eggs of <em>Taenia</em> do not float at the concentration of zinc sulphate used. This procedure is not suitable for concentration of parasites in fatty faeces.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="reagent">Reagent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>33% Zinc sulphate solution Dissolve 330 g of dry zinc sulphate crystals in one litre of distilled water. The specific gravity should be 1.18. If necessary, it should be adjusted by the addition of either zinc sulphate or distilled water.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Mix about a half teaspoon of stool (about 0.5 g) with 1 to 2 ml of water in a test tube.</p>
<ul>
<li>Mix thoroughly and then fill the tube with water to within 2 to 3 mm from the top. Centrifuge at 500 g for one minute.</li>
<li>Discard the supernatant fluid.</li>
<li>Add 1 to 2 ml of the zinc sulphate solution. Tap the tube at the bottom sharply with one finger to resuspend the deposit.</li>
<li>Fill the tube with additional zinc sulphate solution. Strain the suspension through a piece of gauze into another tube.</li>
<li>Discard the gauze. Fill the tube with more zinc sulphate to fill it within 2 to 3 mm from the rim.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>948 Parasitology</p>
<ul>
<li>Centrifuge at 500 g for one minute. Allow the centrifuge to come to a halt without any disturbance.</li>
<li>Place a small drop of iodine or water on a slide.</li>
<li>Bend the loop portion of a wire loop at 90°. Without removing the tube from the centrifuge, and using a freshly flamed and cooled wire loop, remove one or two drops from the centre of the surface film in such a way that the bent portion of the loop is parallel to the surface of fluid in the tube (Fig. 2.11).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-9a2c44a3.jpeg" alt="img-49.jpeg">
Fig. 2.11 Zinc sulphate floatation technique</p>
<ul>
<li>Add the collected surface film to the drop of iodine or water on the slide.</li>
<li>Place a coverslip on, and examine the preparation systematically as in direct wet mounts. Report the number of parasites present in one gram of faeces.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Note</strong> The surface film must be examined within a few minutes after floatation is complete. Otherwise the cysts and eggs may distort or collapse. If no other concentration technique is used, both the surface film and the sediment should be examined to ensure detection of all the parasites.</p>
<p>(ii) Saturated sodium chloride floatation technique This technique is useful for concentrating hookworm or <em>Ascaris</em> eggs in field surveys.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reagent">Reagent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Saturated solution of sodium chloride Prepare a saturated solution of sodium chloride (NaCl) till some salt remains undissolved at the bottom of the container. This solution should have a specific gravity 1.2.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Emulsify about 0.5 g of faeces in 2 to 3 ml of saturated NaCl in a test tube having about 15 mm internal diameter.</li>
<li>Fill the tube with the NaCl solution. Mix well. Stand the tube in a vertical position in a rack on a flat surface.</li>
<li>Add more NaCl solution slowly, to fill the tube completely up to the brim.</li>
<li>Carefully place a clean coverslip on top of the tube so that its undersurface touches the solution.</li>
<li>Leave undisturbed for 30–45 minutes for the cysts and eggs to float through the solution.</li>
<li>Carefully lift the coverslip, where cysts and eggs will be attached, by a straight pull upwards and place it on a slide face downwards. Examine microscopically as for the wet mounts. Calculate the number of parasites per gram of faeces.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Note</strong> The egg may begin to sink, distort or collapse at the end of 60 minutes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-sedimentation-technique">2. Sedimentation Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-sedimentation-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sedimentation methods use centrifugation and can recover all the cysts, eggs and larvae in the specimen. However, the preparation shows more debris than the floatation technique.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-formalin-ether-sedimentation-technique">(i) Formalin-ether sedimentation technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-formalin-ether-sedimentation-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ol>
<li>10% formalin
Formaldehyde 10 ml
Distilled water 90 ml</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites 949</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-diethyl-ether">2. Diethyl ether<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-diethyl-ether" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Transfer about 0.5 g of stool (1/2 teaspoon) to 10 ml of 10% formalin in a 15 ml test tube.</li>
<li>Mix and let it stand for 30 minutes for adequate fixation.</li>
<li>Strain the faecal suspension through two layers of gauze in a funnel into a centrifuge tube. Add about 3 to 4 ml of diethyl ether (caution: It is highly inflammable), close the tube with a glass stopper and shake well for 30 seconds. Do not use a rubber stopper as it may get dissolved in the ether. Holding the tube in such a way that the stopper is directed away from the face, remove the stopper carefully.</li>
<li>Centrifuge the tube for two to three minutes at 500 g. Four layers are found (Fig. 2.12).</li>
<li>A small amount of sediment at the bottom of the tube containing parasites.</li>
<li>A layer of formalin.</li>
<li>A plug of faecal debris.</li>
<li>A layer of ether at the top.</li>
<li>Loosen the plug of debris from the side of the tube using an applicator stick.</li>
<li>Rapidly invert the tube to pour off the ether, the debris and the formalin.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-e7edf7e0.jpeg" alt="img-50.jpeg">
Fig. 2.12 Formalin ether sedimentation technique</p>
<ul>
<li>Transfer all the sediment on to a slide. Cover with a coverslip and examine for parasites.</li>
<li>Count the number of each type of parasite in the entire preparation and report the parasites per gram of faeces.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="permanent-stained-smears">PERMANENT STAINED SMEARS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#permanent-stained-smears" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An experienced microscopist can usually identify most protozoa in a wet preparation. Some workers prefer to confirm the identification by a permanent stained preparation. These stained smears provide a permanent record and so can be used later for reference when doubtful or unknown morphological characteristics are detected.</p>
<p>There are a number of staining methods available. For routine diagnostic work the trichrome staining method and modified iron-haematoxylin technique are adequate. Refer to Histopathology Section for the techniques.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="preparation-of-material-for-staining-fresh-specimen">Preparation of material for staining fresh specimen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-material-for-staining-fresh-specimen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Make a thin smear of the faeces on a slide. In case of a formed specimen, prepare a suspension in saline and spread it to make a thin smear.</p>
<p>While the smear is still wet (do not allow it to dry), fix it in Schaudinn’s fixative for five minutes at 50°C or for one hour at room temperature. Overnight fixation at room temperature is acceptable. If the specimen is liquid, place three to four drops of PVA on the slide and mix several drops of faecal material with the PVA. Spread the mixture and allow it to dry for several hours at 37°C or overnight at room temperature.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pva-preserved-material">PVA-preserved Material<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pva-preserved-material" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Stool specimens preserved with PVA should be allowed to fix in it for at least 30 minutes. After this period, thoroughly mix the specimen and pour it on to a paper towel in a funnel. Let it absorb the PVA.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>950 Parasitology</p>
<p>With an applicator stick, transfer a part of the specimen from the paper towel on to a slide and allow it to dry for several hours at 37°C or overnight at room temperature.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="saf-preserved-material">SAF-preserved Material<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#saf-preserved-material" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Mix the mixture of SAF and stool and sieve through gauze into a centrifuge tube. Centrifuge at 500 g for one minute and decant the supernatant.</p>
<p>Prepare a smear from the sediment, and fix by keeping in Schaudinn’s fixative for one hour at room temperature.</p>
<p>Figures 2.7 and 2.13 show identification scheme for amoebic and flagellate trophozoites in stained smears.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="identification-of-e-histolytica-cysts-by-acridine-orange-staining">Identification of E.histolytica cysts by acridine orange staining<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-e-histolytica-cysts-by-acridine-orange-staining" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Young cysts of E.histolytica contain blunt ended chromatoid bodies which differentiates them from those of E.coli which shows chromatoid bodies with rough, pointed ends, and other protozoa that do not possess chromatoid bodies.</p>
<p>The chromatoid bodies are made up of ribonucleic acid (RNA) which fluoresce yellow or yellow orange when stained with acridine orange. Either a saline suspension or a sediment from formalin-ether concentration can be used for this staining.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="acridine-orange-stock-solution">Acridine orange stock solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acridine-orange-stock-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Acridine orange</th><th>0.1 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>100 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Mix well to dissolve.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>The solution is stable for several weeks.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="acridine-orange-working-solution">Acridine orange working solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acridine-orange-working-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Prepare a working solution just before use as follows:</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Stock acridine orange solution</th><th>1 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Acetic acid, glacial</td><td>0.5 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Phosphate buffered saline, pH 6.8 to 7.2</td><td>8.5 ml</td></tr><tr><td>This working solution has pH 4.0</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Add an equal volume of working acridine orange solution to a saline suspension of faeces or sediment from formalin-ether concentration.</li>
<li>Mix and leave for at least 30 minutes at room temperature.</li>
<li>Prepare a wet mount and examine by fluorescence microscopy.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Chromatoid bodies : Yellow or yellow-orange
Nuclei of cysts : Green
Cytoplasm : Pale-green
(The fluorescence does not fade for several weeks).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-d0f27f6b.jpeg" alt="img-51.jpeg">
Fig. 2.13 Identification scheme for flagellate trophozoites in faeces in stained smears</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites 951</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="baermann-technique">BAERMANN TECHNIQUE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#baermann-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This technique depends on the migration of active larvae out of the faecal material through a wire gauze. The larvae enter the water where they settle out. The fluid can then be removed, centrifuged and the deposit examined for larvae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Attach a rubber tubing with a pinch clamp to the bottom of a funnel.</li>
<li>Fix the funnel on a stand and place a beaker or flask under the stem of the funnel (Fig. 2.14).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b5789281.jpeg" alt="img-52.jpeg">
Fig. 2.14 Baermann technique</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Fill the funnel with luke-warm (37–40°C) water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Place about 5 g of fresh faeces at the bottom of a wire gauze strainer, and keep it over the funnel in such a way that it touches the water in the funnel.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Allow the apparatus to stand undisturbed for at least two hours, so that the larvae migrate from the faeces into the water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Drain off about 10 ml of the fluid from the funnel by releasing the pinch clamp.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Centrifuge at 500g for one minute.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examine the sediment for larvae.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To differentiate between the larvae of <em>Strongyloides</em> and those of hookworms see Fig. 2.3.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Caution</strong>: The water may contain infective larvae, so adequate care should be taken.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="filter-paper-strip-culture-technique-to-differentiate-between-hookworm-species">FILTER PAPER STRIP CULTURE TECHNIQUE TO DIFFERENTIATE BETWEEN HOOKWORM SPECIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#filter-paper-strip-culture-technique-to-differentiate-between-hookworm-species" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Since the eggs of two species of hookworms, <em>Ancylostoma duodenale</em> and <em>Necator americanus</em>, are morphologically indistinguishable, the infecting species can be identified by culturing faeces in distilled water. The hatched filariform larvae can be examined after 10 days.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Smear approximately 0.5 to 1 g of faeces in the centre of a narrow strip of filter paper, about four to five inches in length.</li>
<li>Place the inoculated strip in a large test tube containing about 7 ml of water in such a way that the water level is slightly below the faecal material.</li>
<li>Seal the tube with cellophane and a rubber band.</li>
<li>Incubate at room temperature for 10 days.</li>
<li>After incubation, kill the larvae by submerging three-fourth of the tube in a water bath at 50°C for 15 minutes.</li>
<li>Using forceps, remove the filter paper strip from the tube and discard in a jar of disinfectant.</li>
<li>Transfer the liquid from the culture tube to a centrifuge tube and centrifuge at 500g for two to three minutes.</li>
<li>Examine the sediment for larvae under 10X and 40X objectives for identification (Fig. 2.3).</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Caution</strong>: The larvae are infective after incubation.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>952 Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="differentiation-of-larvae-of-hookworms">Differentiation of larvae of hookworms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#differentiation-of-larvae-of-hookworms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Ancylostoma duodenale</em> Necator americanus</p>
<ol>
<li>The oesophagus does not end in a thistle funnel shape.</li>
<li>There is no gap between the oesophagus and the intestine.</li>
<li>The head and the tail are not very pointed.</li>
</ol>
<p>There is a gap between the oesophagus and the intestine.
The head and the tail are pointed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="differentiation-of-strongyloides-stercoralis-and-hookworm-filariform-larvae">Differentiation of Strongyloides stercoralis and Hookworm Filariform Larvae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#differentiation-of-strongyloides-stercoralis-and-hookworm-filariform-larvae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Strongyloides</em> filariform larva
<em>Hookworm</em> filariform larva</p>
<ol>
<li>The larva is not sheathed.</li>
<li>The tail is either blunt or forked.</li>
<li>The oesophagus occupies half of the body length.</li>
</ol>
<p>The tail is not blunt.
The oesophagus occupies one-third of the body length.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fece2145">♦ ESTIMATION OF WORM BURDEN BY EGG COUNTS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fece2145" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In infections with parasites such as <em>Ascaris lumbricoides</em>, <em>Trichuris trichiura</em>, and hookworms, it is reasonably possible to correlate the worm burden with the egg production. Such an information can be helpful in the determination of the intensity of infection, deciding on the possible chemotherapy, and evaluating the effect of treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="egg-count-by-modified-stoll-dilution-method">Egg Count by Modified Stoll Dilution Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#egg-count-by-modified-stoll-dilution-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Add 14 ml of 0.1 N NaOH in a 15 ml centrifuge tube.</li>
<li>Add the stool sample to bring the specimen up to the 15 ml mark.</li>
<li>Mix well with a wooden applicator stick. (If the stool is hard, allow it to stand for few hours till it forms a smooth suspension). Mix again thoroughly and quickly withdraw exactly 0.15 ml of the suspension from the middle of the tube.</li>
<li>Transfer the material to the slide, cover, and count the number of eggs in the entire preparation.</li>
<li>Multiply the number of eggs per preparation by 100 to obtain the count of eggs per millilitre.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="interpretation-of-egg-counts">Interpretation of Egg-counts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#interpretation-of-egg-counts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In case of <em>Ascaris</em> infection, the presence of even one parasite is potentially dangerous because of its active migrating habits. For hookworms, an egg-count of 2,500 to 5,000 eggs/ml usually indicates a clinically significant infection. About 30,000 eggs/ml of faeces of <em>Trichuris trichiura</em> indicate the presence of several hundred worms, and can cause definite symptoms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="15fae893">♦ EXAMINATION OF OTHER SPECIMENS FROM THE INTESTINAL TRACT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#15fae893" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="cellophone-tape-preparation-for-pinworm">Cellophone Tape Preparation for Pinworm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cellophone-tape-preparation-for-pinworm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><em>Enterobius vermicularis</em> or pinworm is a nematode which is universally found among children. The adult female worm migrates out of the anus, usually at night, and deposits its eggs at the perianal area. The adult female worm may be found on the perianal skin or on the surface of the faecal specimen, measuring about 8 to 13 mm in length. Since the eggs are deposited on the perianal skin, they are not commonly found in faeces. Therefore, it is necessary to use other techniques for diagnosis. The eggs are colourless, thick-walled and oval with one side flattened. Each egg contains a larva which will be infective within a few hours after being hatched.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Intestinal Parasites 953</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="specimen-collection">Specimen Collection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimen-collection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Method
This method uses a piece of clear cellophane tape, approximately four inches in length.
Hold the tape between thumbs and forefingers with the sticky side facing outward (Fig. 2.15).
Press the sticky side of the tape against the skin across the anal opening with an even, thorough pressure. Gently place the sticky side of the tape over a glass slide.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="examination">Examination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Apply a small drop of xylene or toluene under the tape by lifting one side. Press the tape down again on the slide. The preparation will soon be cleared so that the eggs will be visible when examined with low power and low illumination.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="anal-swabs">Anal Swabs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anal-swabs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Anal swabs can also be used for the detection of pinworms. The swabs are prepared by coating</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-48c9e8a2.jpeg" alt="img-53.jpeg">
(A) Use a piece of clear (not frosted) cellophane tape approximately 4 inches (10 cm) long.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-3390cf6a.jpeg" alt="img-54.jpeg">
(B) Hold the tape between thumbs and forefingers with sticky side facing outward</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-dda82bb3.jpeg" alt="img-55.jpeg">
(C) Before the patient has arisen from bed in the morning (preferably while the child is still asleep), press the sticky side of the tape against the skin across the anal opening with even, thorough pressure.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-9ebd84da.jpeg" alt="img-56.jpeg">
(D) Gently place the sticky side of the tape down against the surface of a clear glass slide. Label the slide with the patient's name.</p>
<p>Fig. 2.15 Cellophane tape preparation for pinwarm eggs</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>954 Parasitology</p>
<p>them with molten mixture of four parts petroleum and one part paraffin. When cooled, the swab is gently rubbed over the perianal surface and folds. The swab is then placed in xylene for three to five minutes, and the preparation centrifuged at 500g for one minute. Remove the supernatant with a pipette and examine the sediment for the eggs of <em>E. vermicularis</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sigmoidoscopy-material">Sigmoidoscopy Material<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sigmoidoscopy-material" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If amoebic trophozoites or cysts are not detected in the faeces of a suspected case of amoebiasis, the material obtained by sigmoidoscopy should be examined. Material from the mucosal surface should be aspirated or scraped at least from six representative areas.</p>
<p>The material thus obtained must be processed immediately to be examined using the three components, namely the direct wet mount, concentration and a permanent stained smear for trophozoites of <em>E. histolytica</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="91e74e3b">♦ IDENTIFICATION OF ADULT WORMS IN FAECES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#91e74e3b" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The adult worms that are most commonly submitted to the laboratory for identification are <em>Ascaris lumbricoides</em> (round worm), <em>Enterobius vermicularis</em> (pinworm) or segments of tapeworms.</p>
<p><em>Ascaris lumbricoides</em> worms, when freshly expelled, are pinkish in colour with an appearance similar to earthworms. They measure 25 to 35 cm in length, male being smaller than female. The mouth is small and has three lips. The male has a curved tail and two copulatory spicules of unequal length.</p>
<p><em>Enterobius vermicularis</em> is a small yellowish white worm. The female is 0.8 to 1.3 cm in length with a thin pointed tail. The male measures less than 0.5 cm, and has a coiled tail with a spicule. The head has two wing-like expansions called cervical alae.</p>
<p>Mature tapeworm segments which are excreted in faeces contain fully developed uterine branches filled with eggs. Species identification is based on the dimensions of the segment and the number of lateral uterine branches that arise from the main uterine stem in the mature (gravid) segments (proglottids). If the uterine branches are not clearly visible, inject india ink into the central stem or the uterine pore, filling the uterine branches with ink. Press the proglottid between the two slides and count the number of branches. For differentiation, see Fig. 2.16.</p>
<p>Caution Eggs of <em>Taenia solium</em> are infective for humans.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2442f4df">♦ SPECIAL TECHNIQUES FOR COCCIDIA (FAECAL)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2442f4df" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Cryptosporidium</em> causes diarrhoea in young children and immunocompromised patients, e.g. AIDS patients. The oocysts are very difficult to identify in direct wet mounts. Since <em>Cryptosporidium</em> is moderately acid-fast, a modified acid-fast staining technique is useful for the detection of oocysts. Modified Ziehl Neelsen method using 3% acid-alcohol for one minute, Kinyoun’s method and auramine stain are employed. These techniques are described in the Bacteriology Section. But the rapid sefranin method is generally used to detect <em>Cryptosporidium</em> oocysts.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rapid-safranin-method">Rapid Safranin Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rapid-safranin-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="reagent">Reagent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Prepare a thin smear from fresh or formalin fixed faeces and dry air.</li>
</ul>

<li>Wash briefly with water.</li>
