<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sheather-s-sugar-floatation-technique">Sheather's Sugar Floatation Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sheather-s-sugar-floatation-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a concentration method for the detection of Cryptosporidium oocysts.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="reagent">Reagent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="sugar-solution">Sugar solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sugar-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Sucrose</th><th>500 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>320 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Phenol</td><td>6.5 g</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Boil the sugar solution till clear. In a fume hood, carefully add phenol and stir. Cool to room temperature before using.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Filter about 2 ml of faecal suspension in a centrifuge tube through two layers of gauze to remove mucus and large particles.</li>
<li>Add Sheather’s sugar solution until the tube is three-fourth full.</li>
<li>Stir vigorously for thorough mixing.</li>
<li>Add more sugar solution to fill up to 1–2 cm below the top of the tube.</li>
<li>Centrifuge at 100g for 5 to 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Transfer the surface layer to a microscope slide using a wire loop bent at right angle (Fig. 2.8).</li>
<li>Observe using phase contrast microscopy preferably.</li>
</ul>
<p>Results Oocysts appear as refractile bodies.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 3</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-9d4825f4.jpeg" alt="img-68.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-techniques-for-blood-parasites">Laboratory Techniques for Blood Parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-techniques-for-blood-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The parasites which can be detected in blood are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Plasmodia</li>
<li>Trypanosomes</li>
<li>Leishmania</li>
<li>Filarial worms</li>
</ol>
<p>Some parasites such as microfilariae and trypanosomes can be detected in the direct wet mount of fresh blood by their characteristic shape and motility. However, specific identification of the parasite requires a permanent stain. For permanent staining, two types of blood films can be prepared. Thick films allow a larger volume of blood to be examined, thus making it easier to detect light infections with fewer parasites, while species identification is difficult. Thin films are necessary to see the morphological characteristics of the parasites and to identify them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="collection-of-specimens-for-blood-films">COLLECTION OF SPECIMENS FOR BLOOD FILMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#collection-of-specimens-for-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Caution: Careful attention to safety technique is necessary at the time of collection of blood samples and preparation of blood films. A number of parasitological, bacterial and viral diseases can be transmitted through blood.</p>
<p>Blood films should be prepared before the commencement of any treatment. It is preferable to prepare blood films with fresh blood without anticoagulant. If it is not possible, blood anticoagulated with EDTA (10mg/5ml blood) should be used; and blood films should be prepared as soon as possible, preferably, within one hour of collection. The time of collection should be mentioned on the specimen as well as on the result sheet so that the results may be correlated with the fever pattern by the physician.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="preparation-of-blood-films">PREPARATION OF BLOOD FILMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For accurate examination of blood films, it is necessary to use absolutely clean, grease-free slides. Well washed slides cleaned with 70% ethanol are recommended.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thick-blood-films">Thick Blood Films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thick-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To make a thick film, place two or three small drops of fresh blood without anticoagulant on a clean slide. With a corner of another slide, mix the drops in a circular motion over an area about two cm in diameter. Continue mixing for about 30 seconds to prevent formation of fibrin strands that may obscure the parasites after staining. (If anticoagulated blood is used, it is not necessary to continue mixing for 30 seconds). Allow the film to dry in air at room temperature. Before staining, the thick films are laked to lyse the red blood cells and</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Blood Parasites 961</p>
<p>to remove haemoglobin so that the parasites can be easily detected. To lake the films, they are either placed in buffer solution before staining or placed directly into an aqueous stain like Giemsa stain. If thick blood films are to be stored, they should be laked before storage. Once fixed, it is difficult to lyse red cells and release haemoglobin in thick blood films.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thin-blood-films">Thin Blood Films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thin-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In thin films, the number of parasites is much less than in the thick films, but it permits specific identification of parasites.</p>
<p>The thin film is prepared in exactly the same way as the one used for a peripheral blood smear examination. (See Haematology Section.) Allow the thin blood film to air-dry. The need for fixation before staining depends on the type of stain used. Aqueous stains, such as Giemsa, need pre-fixation of thin films with methanol; while a methanol based stain, such as Leishman stain, does not require fixation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thick-and-thin-blood-film-on-the-same-slide">Thick and Thin Blood Film on the Same Slide<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thick-and-thin-blood-film-on-the-same-slide" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For routine malaria microscopy, or in field surveys, it is advantageous to prepare both the thick and the thin film on the same slide (Fig. 3.1). It must be remembered, however, that thick film requires a much longer time to dry in air; and that only the thin film must be fixed in methanol before staining.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="staining-blood-films">STAINING BLOOD FILMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#staining-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Blood films should be stained as soon as possible as delay may result in stain retention. Romanowsky stains such as Giemsa, Leishman or Field, can be used for staining of parasites in blood film. Leishman's stain has the fixative combined with the staining solution, so that fixation and staining both occur at the same time. Therefore, the thick film must be laked before staining by Leishman stain. In Giemsa and Field stains, on the other hand, the fixative and the stain are separate. Thus the thin film must be fixed in methanol before staining.</p>
<p>After staining, the smears should be air-dried. For a permanent record, these should be protected with a cover glass by mounting in a medium such as Permount or DPX.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="giemsa-staining-technique">Giemsa Staining Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#giemsa-staining-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Giemsa stain is a Romanowsky stain that requires dilution in buffered water or buffered saline before use. The stain is available commercially either as a concentrated stock solution or in a powdered form.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="giemsa-stain-stock-solution">Giemsa stain (Stock solution)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#giemsa-stain-stock-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Giemsa stain powder</th><th>0.6 g.</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Methanol, absolute (acetone-free)</td><td>50 ml.</td></tr><tr><td>Glycerol</td><td>50 ml.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Note</strong>: Giemsa stain will be adversely affected if water enters the stock solution during its preparation or storage.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-70450435.jpeg" alt="img-69.jpeg">
Fig. 3.1 Preparation of thick and thin blood films on the same slide</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>962 Parasitology</p>
<p>Dissolve the Giemsa stain in methanol in a brown bottle containing a few glass beads. Add glycerol, mix and place the bottle in a water bath at 50–60° C for two hours to dissolve the stain. Shake gently at half-hour intervals. The stain should stand at room temperature for three weeks and should be filtered before use. If kept air-tight, the stain is stable for several months.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="giemsa-stain-working-solution">Giemsa stain (working solution)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#giemsa-stain-working-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The commercial stock solution or the solution prepared as above should be diluted 1:10 with buffer for thin films and 1:50 for thick films. Phosphate buffer used for the dilution of the stain should be neutral or slightly alkaline (pH 7.0 to 7.2). If the pH of the tap water in the laboratory is satisfactory, it may be used for the entire staining procedure, even for the final rinsing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique-for-thin-films">Technique for thin films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique-for-thin-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Fix the thin blood film in absolute methanol for 1 minute. If both the thick and the thin films are on the same slide, carefully dip only the thin smear in methanol. Allow the smear to dry in air.</li>
<li>Prepare an appropriate dilution of the stock Giemsa stain using the phosphate buffer or tap water.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; Some laboratories prefer to use a higher dilution of the stain for both thick and thin films. The general rule for stain dilution and staining period says that, for 1:20 dilution, stain for 20 minutes, for 1:30 dilution for 30 minutes and so on. The best dilution and time for each batch of stock solution should be determined.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>At the end of the staining period, gently flush the stain off the slide with water. Do not tip off the stain before washing, as this will leave stain deposits over the smear.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dip the slide briefly in the buffer or rinse under gently running tap water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wipe the undersurface of the slide to remove excess stain.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Allow it to air-dry in a vertical position.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique-for-thick-films">Technique for thick films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique-for-thick-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The procedure to be followed for thick films is the same as that for thin films except that the thick film should not be fixed in methanol, but directly stained in diluted Giemsa.</p>
<p>If the slide has a thick film at one end and a thin film at the other, fix only the thin portion, and then stain both parts of the film simultaneously.</p>
<p>Caution Because thick films are not fixed, pathogens are not killed; and therefore, even after staining, smears should be handled with care to avoid infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="malarial-parasites">Malarial parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#malarial-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Chromatin of parasite</th><th>: Dark red</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Cytoplasm of parasite</td><td>: Blue</td></tr><tr><td>Schuffner’s dots</td><td>: Red</td></tr><tr><td>Maurer’s dots</td><td>: Red-mauve</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="trypanosomes">Trypanosomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trypanosomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nucleus</th><th>: Mauve-red</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Kinetoplast</td><td>: Dark red</td></tr><tr><td>Cytoplasm</td><td>: Pale mauve</td></tr><tr><td>Flagellum</td><td>: Pale mauve</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="leishmania">Leishmania<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leishmania" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nucleus</th><th>: Mauve red</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Kinetoplast</td><td>: Dark mauve-red</td></tr><tr><td>Cytoplasm</td><td>: Pale mauve.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="microfilariae">Microfilariae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#microfilariae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nuclei</th><th>: Dark purple</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Sheath of W. bancrofti</td><td>: Dark pink</td></tr><tr><td>Sheath of Loa loa</td><td>: Pale grey or unstained.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="blood-cells">Blood cells<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-cells" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Red cells</th><th>: Grey to Pale-mauve-pink</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Reticulocytes</td><td>: Grey-blue</td></tr><tr><td>Nuclei of neutrophils</td><td>: Dark purple</td></tr><tr><td>Granules of neutrophils</td><td>: Mauve purple</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Blood Parasites 963</p>
<p>Granules of eosinophils : Red
Cytoplasm of mononuclear leucocytes : Blue to blue-grey</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
In thick films, red blood cells will be lysed and therefore intracellular parasites such as malarial parasites will be seen.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="d2c3957e">Field’s Staining Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#d2c3957e" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Field’s stain is a water-based Romanowsky stain. It is composed of two solutions, A and B. Field’s solution A contains methylene blue, azure I and phosphate salts while solution B contains eosin and phosphate salts. Buffer solutions are included in the staining solution. Neither solutions need to be diluted for thick films, but solution B is diluted 1:5 before use for thin films.</p>
<p>Advantages of the Field’s stain are that it is quick, stable, isotonic with malarial parasites and trypanosomes, penetrates unfixed parasites rapidly and gives particularly good results with fresh thick blood films. However, it is not suitable for staining microfilariae in thick films.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Field’s stain A</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Methylene blue</td><td>1.6 g</td></tr><tr><td>Azur 1</td><td>1.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Disodium hydrogen phosphate (NA₂HPO₄) anhydrous</td><td>10.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Potassium dihydrogen phosphate (KH₂PO₄)</td><td>12.5 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1000 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Dissolve the phosphates in the water. Add a few glass beads and then the stain powders. Mix well and filter.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Field’s stain A (powder form)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Field’s stain A powder</td><td>5.9 g</td></tr><tr><td>Hot distilled water</td><td>600 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Mix until dissolved, cool and filter.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Field’s stain B</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Eosin (Yellow, water soluble)</td><td>2.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Disodium hydrogen phosphate (NA₂HPO₄), anhydrous</td><td>10.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>---</td><td>---</td></tr><tr><td>Potassium dihydrogen phosphate (KH₂PO₄)</td><td>12.5 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1000 ml.</td></tr><tr><td>Dissolve the phosphates in the water and add eosin. Mix to dissolve, filter.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Field’s stain B (powder form)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Field’s stain B powder</td><td>4.8 g</td></tr><tr><td>Hot distilled water</td><td>600 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Mix to dissolve, cool and filter.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique-for-thin-films">Technique for thin films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique-for-thin-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Fix the thin smear in absolute methanol for one minute. Allow the film to dry.</li>
<li>Dilute Field’s stain B 1:5 with buffered water or tap water with slightly alkaline pH.</li>
<li>Cover the film with diluted Field’s stain B.</li>
<li>Immediately add an equal volume of Field’s stain A.</li>
<li>Mix by tilting the slide and leave to stain for one minute.</li>
<li>Wash off the stain gently with water.</li>
<li>Wipe the under surface of the slide clean and air-dry.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
Field’s stain is also useful for rapid staining of the films made from aspirates and skin ulcers for Leishmania parasites; for faecal smears for flagellates of Giardia intestinalis (G. lamblia) and for blood smear for microfilariae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique-for-thick-films">Technique for thick films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique-for-thick-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Place both the stains (Field’s stains A and B) in separate staining dishes or Coplin jars.</li>
<li>Fill two jars with clean tap water.</li>
<li>Dip the thick smear into Field’s stain A for three seconds.</li>
<li>Wash gently by dipping once into clean water.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>964 Parasitology</p>
<ul>
<li>Dip into Field’s stain B for three seconds.</li>
<li>Wash again by dipping into the second jar of water.</li>
<li>Wipe the back of the side clean and air-dry.</li>
</ul>
<p>Caution If the blood-specimen is suspected to contain pathogens such as hepatitis virus, AIDS virus, haemorrhagic fever viruses etc., it is not safe to use staining jars to dip the slides. Such thick films should be stained by placing the slides on a staining rack.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The staining characteristics of various parasites and blood cells by Field’s stain are similar to those obtained by Giemsa stain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="leishman-staining-technique">Leishman Staining Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leishman-staining-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Leishman stain is a methanol-based Romanowsky stain. Therefore, thin blood films do not need fixation with methanol before staining.</p>
<ul>
<li>Add undiluted Leishman stain directly to the smear.</li>
<li>After fixation for one minute, dilute the stain on the slide 1:2 with neutral or slightly alkaline phosphate buffer (pH 7.0–7.2) and stain for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>When thick and thin blood films are prepared on the same slide, care should be taken that the stain does not reach the thick film before dilution.</li>
</ul>
<p>For preparation of Leishman stain, refer to Haematology Section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examination-of-stained-blood-films-for-parasites">EXAMINATION OF STAINED BLOOD FILMS FOR PARASITES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-stained-blood-films-for-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thick-blood-films">Thick Blood Films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thick-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In a thick blood film, the greatest concentration of blood cells occurs in the centre of the film. An area which appears mauve in colour (usually near the edges) indicates an area of good colour balance between the blue and the red stains, and will show the correct staining characteristics of parasites.</p>
<p>In thick films, most of the red blood cells are lysed, and therefore, no longer visible. The blood layer is much thicker than in a thin film, and therefore, one should focus up and down to see the parasites located at different levels. The parasites in thick films appear smaller than those in thin films, but the characteristics of species do not change. The parasites in the outer thinner edge of the smear are similar to those in thin films.</p>
<p>Scanning for microfilariae is carried out at low magnification (10 X objective) and identification confirmed with the oil immersion lens. The search for other parasites such as malarial parasites, trypanosomes and leishmania should be carried out under oil immersion lens after using one or two drops of oil. When properly stained, the thick film under the oil immersion lens should show a clean background, free from debris, with a mottled grey colour due to lysed red blood cells. The parasites and white cells should show the colour patterns as described for Giemsa stain.</p>
<p>If any parasite is detected in the thick film, the thin film should be examined for identification of species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thin-blood-films">Thin Blood Films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thin-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Initial screening of a thin film should be carried out with the low-power (10 X) objective. Generally, only a few microfilariae occur in a thin blood film preparation and it is possible to miss them if the entire film is not examined. They are commonly found near the edges of the thin film or at the thin end of the film. Once detected, microfilariae should be identified under the oil immersion lens.</p>
<p>Near the end of the film, the red cells spread out evenly into a single layer of cells. This area should be examined under the oil immersion lens for malarial parasites, trypanosomes, leishmania etc. The morphology of the red cells and that of the parasites is most clearly seen in these areas making their identification easier.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Blood Parasites 965</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="identification-of-parasites-in-blood-films">Identification of Parasites in Blood Films<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-parasites-in-blood-films" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="malarial-parasites">Malarial parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#malarial-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are four species of malarial parasites which can infect humans. They are <em>Plasmodium vivax</em>, <em>P. falciparum</em>, <em>P. malariae</em> and <em>P. ovale</em>. Three stages of their asexual life cycle occur in man, namely, trophozoite, schizont and gametocytes (male and female). In peripheral blood, the parasites are present inside the red cells. Each species can be identified on two basic parameters:</p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Appearance of infected RBC:</strong></li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li><strong>Size:</strong> The parasitized red cell may remain unchanged or may undergo variation e.g. enlargement, irregular or oval shape.</li>
<li><strong>Stippling:</strong> The red cell may show pink-red dots called Schuffner's dots while some red cells often show irregular large red-mauve dots called Maurer's dots.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li><strong>Appearance of the parasite:</strong></li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li><strong>Early trophozoite (ring form):</strong> Size of the ring, position of the ring, the number of chromatin dots and the number of rings in one red cell are considered.</li>
<li><strong>Late trophozoite:</strong> The late trophozoites may have an irregular outline or a smooth outline; or may appear as bands across the parasitized red cell.</li>
<li><strong>Colour of the pigment:</strong> The colour of the pigment in the parasite may be light brown or dark brown and it may be fine or coarse.</li>
<li><strong>Schizont:</strong> The number and arrangement of merozoites in the schizont varies from one species to another.</li>
<li><strong>Gametocyte:</strong> Gametocytes may be crescent shaped or round.</li>
</ul>
<p>A chart for identification of malarial parasites (Fig. 3.2) and Table 3.1 for differentiating characteristics of <em>Plasmodium</em> species are given below. For morphology, see Fig. 3.3.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="trypanosomes">Trypanosomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trypanosomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Trypanosomes may be identified in both thick and thin blood films. They are extracellular parasites found free in blood (Fig. 3.4). Like malarial parasites, the cytoplasm stains blue while the nucleus and the kinetoplast stain red or purple. Sometimes, in thick films, they may get distorted and difficult to differentiate from cellular debris.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="leishmania">Leishmania<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leishmania" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Amastigote forms of <em>Leishmania</em> species are small, round or oval bodies measuring about 2–4 μm. The nucleus and the rod shaped kinetoplast in each parasite stain dark reddish mauve (Fig. 3.5). Cytoplasm stains palely and is often difficult to see. The amastigotes can be seen in groups inside mononuclear phagocytic cells or lying free between cells. Except for a little variation in size, amastigotes of <em>Leishmania</em> species are similar in structure.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; Although amastigotes can occasionally be detected in mononuclear leucocytes in the peripheral blood, they are more easily detected by microscopic examination of bone marrow, lymph node and splenic aspirates. Concentration methods for <em>Leishmania</em> in blood may yield better results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="microfilariae">Microfilariae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#microfilariae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For identification of microfilariae in stained blood films, the following characteristics are looked for:</p>
<p>(i) Presence or absence of a sheath.
(ii) Presence or absence of nuclei at the tip of the tail.
(iii) Size of microfilaria.
(iv) Size of cephalic space in sheathed microfilaria.</p>
<p>Figure 3.6 shows a scheme for the identification of microfilariae.</p>
<p>Morphology of some microfilariae is shown in Fig. 3.7.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>966 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-30cef0b1.jpeg" alt="img-70.jpeg"></p>
<p>Fig. 3.2 Identification of material parasites in thick and thin blood films</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="special-techniques-for-blood-parasites">SPECIAL TECHNIQUES FOR BLOOD PARASITES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#special-techniques-for-blood-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="estimation-of-the-number-of-malarial-parasites">Estimation of the Number of Malarial Parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#estimation-of-the-number-of-malarial-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>In severe malaria caused by <em>P. falciparum</em>, it is important to know the degree of parasitaemia so that the patient can be appropriately treated. It is also helpful in cases of suspected drug resistance to estimate the parasitic density during treatment.</p>
<p>There are two methods to estimate the number of parasites:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="counting-the-percentage-of-parasitized-cells">Counting the percentage of parasitized cells<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#counting-the-percentage-of-parasitized-cells" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>This technique is similar to that used for counting reticulocytes (refer to Haematology Section).</p>
<p>Count at least 500 red cells and at the same time note the number of red cells that contain malarial parasites. Do not count the gametocytes as they do not take part in the asexual life cycle.</p>
<p>To calculate the percentage of parasitized cells, divide the number of parasites in 500 red cells by five. Five per cent or higher parasitaemia indicates a serious infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="counting-the-number-of-parasites-against-white-cells">Counting the number of parasites against white cells<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#counting-the-number-of-parasites-against-white-cells" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>This technique can be followed in both thick and thin films because the white cells remain intact even in thick films. It is necessary that the film should be evenly spread so that the white cells and parasites are evenly distributed.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<ol start="196">
<li>Sunnarpoog my sonnquy Annooy</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="concentration-by-buffy-coat-smears">Concentration by Buffy Coat Smears<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#concentration-by-buffy-coat-smears" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This technique is employed when the number of parasites in blood is low. Buffy coat is a layer of white cells between the red cells at the bottom and plasma at the top resulting from centrifugation of whole citrated blood.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Blood Parasites 971</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-7983a819.jpeg" alt="img-78.jpeg">
Fig. 3.7 Microfilariae of various species</p>
<p>The buffy coat gives excellent concentration of <em>P. vivax</em> parasitized red cells just below the white cells and platelets. Other species of malarial parasites do not concentrate so well.</p>
<p>This technique is also useful for the concentration of <em>Leishmania</em> species, <em>Trypanosoma</em> species and microfilariae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Using a capillary pipette, fill the Wintrobe tube with EDTA, citrated or oxalated blood.</li>
<li>Centrifuge at 100 g for 30 minutes.</li>
<li>After centrifugation, the tube will show three layers with packed red cells at the bottom, plasma at the top, and a small layer of white cells (buffy coat) between them.</li>
<li>For the malarial parasites, remove the cells from the buffy coat and red cells immediately below it to the depth of 1 mm, using a capillary pipette. Prepare thin smears with these cells. Stain by Giemsa or Field's method. Identify the malarial parasites present.</li>
</ul>
<p>Trypanosomes and microfilariae are more concentrated in the plasma immediately above the buffy coat. They can be detected in a thin film in the same way as malarial parasites, or by preparing a wet film. To prepare a wet film, place a drop of the plasma and buffy coat layer on a slide, place a cover slip and observe under the microscope. Microfilariae can be easily detected by their motility under low power (10X) objective whereas trypanosomes are better seen with 40 X objective.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="concentration-of-trypanosomes-and-microfilariae-by-microhaematocrit-method">Concentration of Trypanosomes and Microfilariae by Microhaematocrit Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#concentration-of-trypanosomes-and-microfilariae-by-microhaematocrit-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This method has a few advantages over the buffy coat smears. If venepuncture cannot be performed, a small quantity of blood can be collected in a capillary tube by finger-prick; and it is not necessary to stain the film. The parasites can be observed directly in the capillary tube under the microscope. A special centrifuge for microhaematocrit tubes is required.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>972 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b16251ec.jpeg" alt="img-79.jpeg">
Fig. 3.8 Concentration of parasites by microhaematocrit method</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Fill a capillary tube three-fourth full with anticoagulated venous blood. If venous blood is not available, obtain two drops of blood by finger-prick in a heparanised capillary tube.</li>
<li>Seal the open end of the capillary tube either by heating or with plasticine.</li>
<li>Centrifuge in a microhaematocrit centrifuge for two minutes for microfilariae and four minutes for trypanosomes.</li>
<li>Secure the ends of the capillary tube on a microscope slide with adhesive tape (Fig. 3.8).</li>
<li>Using 10X and 40X objective, examine the area between the buffy coat and the plasma.</li>
<li>Motile microfilariae and trypanosomes can be easily detected.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="knott-concentration-method-for-microfilariae">Knott Concentration Method for Microfilariae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#knott-concentration-method-for-microfilariae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When a light infection is suspected, the microfilariae in peripheral blood can also be detected by haemolysing the red cells with formalin and using the centrifuged deposit for direct examination and to prepare thick and thin smears.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Mix 1ml of venous blood with 9ml of 2% formalin. Allow it to stand for five minutes for the red cells to haemolyse.</li>
<li>Centrifuge at 500g for two minutes.</li>
<li>Pour off the supernatant fluid.</li>
<li>Examine a small portion of the sediment as a wet mount; and prepare thick and thin films from the remaining sediment. Air-dry, fix and stain the film by Giemsa stain, and examine under oil immersion lens to detect and identify microfilariae.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="delafield-s-haematoxylin-stain-for-microfilariae">Delafield's Haematoxylin Stain for Microfilariae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#delafield-s-haematoxylin-stain-for-microfilariae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a rapid staining method for staining nuclei and sheaths of microfilariae which helps in the identification of species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="field-s-stains-a-and-b">Field's stains A and B<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#field-s-stains-a-and-b" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Phosphate buffer (pH 7.1 to 7.2)</p>
<p>Delafield's haematoxylin</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Haematoxylin</th><th>4.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Ammonium alum</td><td>8.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Potassium permanganate</td><td>0.2 g</td></tr><tr><td>Ethanol, absolute</td><td>125 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>400 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Dissolve haematoxylin in absolute ethanol. Dissolve the ammonium alum in 400ml warm water (40–50° C).</p>
<p>Filter the haematoxylin solution and mix it with the solution of ammonium alum. Dissolve the potassium permanganate in about 10ml of water and add it to the stain. This will 'ripen' the stain so that it can be used immediately. The stain is stable for several months.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques for Blood Parasites 973</p>
<p>The Delafield's stain is also commercially available.</p>
<p><strong>Technique</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Prepare a thin smear from fresh blood or from the blood sample concentrated by one of the methods described. Allow it to dry in air.</li>
<li>Fix the smear in methanol for one minute and wash with water. Cover the smear with approximately 1ml of diluted Field's stain B.</li>
<li>Immediately add an equal volume of Field's stain A and mix well either with a pasteur pipette or by tilting the slide. Allow the mixture to stain for one minute and wash with water.</li>
<li>Dilute Delafield's haematoxylin stain 1:10 with distilled water and stain the smear with it for five minutes. Wash with buffered water, pH 7.1 to 7.2.</li>
<li>Dry and examine microscopically. Use the 10 X objective to detect microfilariae, and 40 X and oil immersion objectives for identification of species.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Results</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nuclei of microfilariae</th><th>Blue</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Sheath of Wuchereria bancrofti microfilariae</td><td>Grey</td></tr><tr><td>Sheath of Brugia malayi microfilariae</td><td>Dark grey</td></tr><tr><td>Sheath of Loa loa microfilariae</td><td>Pale grey</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
This method can also be adapted for staining thick films: Follow the technique for staining of thick films by Field's method followed by staining with Delafield's stain for 5 minutes. The results obtained are similar.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="acridine-orange-fluorescence-technique-for-trypanosomes">Acridine Orange Fluorescence Technique for Trypanosomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acridine-orange-fluorescence-technique-for-trypanosomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Acridine orange is a fluorochrome that causes deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) to fluoresce green and ribonucleic acid (RNA) to fluoresce orange red. Trypanosomes contain RNA and therefore fluoresce orange red in ultraviolet light. They can be rapidly detected using 20X or 40X objective.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="acridine-orange-acid-stain">Acridine orange acid stain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acridine-orange-acid-stain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Acridine orange</th><th>0.13 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Acetic acid, glacial</td><td>10 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>490 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Mix the glacial acetic acid with the distilled water. Add this acidified water to acridine orange and mix until dissolved. The stain is stable for several weeks.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="alcohol-saline-solution">Alcohol-saline solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#alcohol-saline-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Ethanol (or methanol), absolute</th><th>5 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Sodium chloride 0.85% (w/v)</td><td>245 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Sodium chloride 0.85% (w/v) (Physiological saline)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Prepare a smear either directly from blood or from a blood sample concentrated by one of the methods described. Allow to dry in air (Do not fix as the stain contains a fixative).</li>
<li>Cover the smear with acridine orange acid stain for 5–10 seconds. Wash off the stain and decolorize with alcohol-saline for 5–10 seconds. Rinse the smear with the physiological saline.</li>
<li>Mount in saline or distilled water and examine by fluorescence microscopy.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Trypanosomes fluoresce orange-red against a dark background.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>974 Parasitology</p>
<p>Table 3.2 Blood collection times for suspected filariasis patients (WHO reference)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Species</th><th>When to take a specimen</th><th>Part of World</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Wuchereria bancrofti</td><td>At night (between 22h and 24h)</td><td>Tropical Africa, Asia, Central and South America, Indian Ocean</td></tr><tr><td>Wuchereria bancrofti (var. pacifica)</td><td>Any time</td><td>Pacific Ocean</td></tr><tr><td>Brugia malayi</td><td>Mainly at night (between 22h and 24h)</td><td>Southern China, Southern India</td></tr><tr><td>Loa loa</td><td>During the day (between 10h and 12h)</td><td>West and Central Africa</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><em>These periods are not invariable</em></p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 4</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="examination-of-urine-aspirates-body-fluids-and-tissue-biopsies">Examination of Urine, Aspirates, Body Fluids and Tissue Biopsies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-urine-aspirates-body-fluids-and-tissue-biopsies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="parasites-in-urine">PARASITES IN URINE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#parasites-in-urine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Parasites which can be detected in urine are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Eggs of schistosoma haematobium</li>
<li>Microfilariae of Wuchereria bancrofti</li>
<li>Microfilariae of Onchocerca volvulus.</li>
<li>Trophozoites of Trichomonas vaginalis.</li>
</ol>
<p>If present in sufficient numbers, these parasites can be detected while examining centrifuged deposits of urine. In areas where schistosomiasis is endemic, the first indication of infection is haematuria which can be detected either by chemical test or microscopically. A heavy infection with Schistosoma may lead to gross haematuria which is seen visually. A milky urine may show microfilariae of Wuchereria bancrofti or Onchocerca volvulus in a centrifuged deposit of urine. Trichomonas vaginalis is a parasite of the genital system but may appear in the urinary deposit as a contaminant from genital organs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis-of-schistosoma">Diagnosis of Schistosoma<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis-of-schistosoma" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="collection-of-urine">Collection of urine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#collection-of-urine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A special care is needed for the collection of urine for suspected schistosomiasis because the number of ova excreted in urine varies throughout the day. It is highest in the terminal portion of the urine between 10.00 am to 2.00 pm. The last few drops of urine contain the maximum number of eggs. Therefore, the specimen should be collected between these times and should be terminal urine at least 10 ml in volume. Alternatively, a 24 hour specimen may be collected with formalin (1 ml per 100 ml of urine) as a preservative for the eggs. If it is not possible to examine the fresh specimen within one hour of collection, this too, should be preserved with formalin using the same proportion. It is advisable to examine large volumes of urine because the ova are very scanty and can be easily missed. If not preserved with formalin, the eggs may hatch to release miracidia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="examination-of-urine-for-the-eggs-of-schistosoma-haematobium">Examination of urine for the eggs of Schistosoma haematobium<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-urine-for-the-eggs-of-schistosoma-haematobium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>There are two methods in use for the detection of Schistosoma eggs in urine.</p>
<ol>
<li>The sedimentation technique is less sensitive, but cheaper and simpler to perform.</li>
<li>The filtration technique is used when quantitative information is needed, e.g. in a public health laboratory.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="sedimentation-technique">Sedimentation technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sedimentation-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Collect a 24 hour or terminal urine sample as explained above, and check for haematuria.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>976 Parasitology</p>
<ul>
<li>Shake the urine well and allow it to sediment for 1 hour.</li>
<li>At the end of 1 hour, carefully withdraw the supernatant without disturbing the sediment.</li>
<li>Transfer the sediment to a centrifuge tube and centrifuge at 500g for 5 minutes.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
Do not exceed the centrifugation time and speed as this may rupture the ova and release miracidia.</p>
<ul>
<li>Discard the supernatant and examine the sediment as a wet preparation, using the 10x objective to search for the ova of <em>Schistosoma haematobium</em>.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="filtration-technique">Filtration technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#filtration-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Requirements</strong> 10 ml syringe and a syringe filter holder, 13 mm in diameter.
Nuclepore polycarbonate membrane filter, 13 mm in diameter and 12 to 14 µm pore size. This is a clear filter which can be disinfected in domestic bleach (1% hypochlorite solution) and reused several times if used carefully.</p>
<p><strong>OR</strong></p>
<p>Nytrel filter, 12 mm in diameter and 20 µm pore size, can be used if polycarbonate filter is not available. This filter is not reusable. Alternatively, paper filters such as Whatman No. 1 or No. 541 can be used.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
Low priced kits are available from organisations such as PATH or Tropical Health Technology.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Collect a fresh or a 24 hour urine sample, and check for the presence of proteins and blood.</li>
<li>With blunt forceps, place a polycarbonate, nytrel or paper filter in the filter holder.</li>
</ul>
<p>Close and attach it to a 10 ml plastic syringe.</p>
<ul>
<li>Remove the plunger and fill the syringe with the well mixed urine sample up to the 10 ml mark.</li>
<li>Replace the plunger. Hold the syringe over a suitable container such as a beaker.</li>
<li>Press the urine through the filter into the container.</li>
<li>Remove the filter from its holder carefully and place it on a slide with face upwards so that the eggs are on the surface.</li>
<li>Add a drop of physiological saline or Lugol’s iodine and cover with a coverslip.</li>
<li>Examine under 10x objective with reduced light, the entire area of the filter for <em>Schistosoma</em> eggs.</li>
<li>Count the number of eggs and note the number per 10 ml of urine.</li>
<li>More than 50 eggs/10 ml indicates a heavy infection.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Notes</strong>
Polycarbonate filters can be reused after soaking it overnight in a 1% hypochlorite solution (domestic bleach). Next day, wash the filter thoroughly with detergent solution and then for several times with clean water. Check the filter microscopically that it is free of parasites before reusing it.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="identification-of-schistosoma-haematobium-ova">Identification of <em>Schistosoma haematobium</em> ova<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-schistosoma-haematobium-ova" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The ovum of <em>Schistosoma haematobium</em> is large, measuring 145 × 55 µm in size. It is oval in shape and pale-brown in colour. It has a characteristic small, terminal spine at one end. The ovum contains a fully developed embryo, the miracidium. If the egg is viable, the excretory flame cells of the embryo show rapid ciliary movements under reduced light at 40X objective.</p>
<p>Viable eggs can be seen only in fresh, unpreserved specimens. Viability can be tested by adding a drop of 1% (w/v) trypan blue to the preparation.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Examination of Urine, Aspirates, Body Fluids and Tissue Biopsies 977</p>
<p>ration. Keep it for 30 minutes in a moist chamber. The non-viable eggs take up the blue stain whereas viable eggs remain unstained with trypan blue.</p>
<p>Viability test is useful in assessing the severity of infection or effectiveness of treatment.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
<em>Schistosoma haematobium eggs can occasionally be found in faeces.</em></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="special-techniques-for-detecting-microfilariae-in-urine">Special Techniques for Detecting Microfilariae in Urine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#special-techniques-for-detecting-microfilariae-in-urine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Microfilariae most commonly found in urine are those of <em>Wuchereria bancrofti</em>. A chronic infection with <em>W. bancrofti</em> may lead to chyluria. Urine containing chyle appears creamy white or milky. The chyle consists of lymph and fat particles which gives the urine its characteristic appearance.
