The fat is soluble in ether, therefore, urine becomes clear when mixed with a small volume of ether. Other microfilariae, such as those of <em>Onchocerca volvulus</em> and <em>Brugia malayi</em>, may also be found in urine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sedimentation-technique">Sedimentation technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sedimentation-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Collect the first early morning urine sample after waking.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the urine is milky in appearance, add about 2 ml of ether and shake till it becomes clear.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Centrifuge 10 ml of the specimen at 500g for 5 minutes. A very high speed is not advisable as the microfilariae may lose their sheath.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Discard the supernatant and examine the sediment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the sediment shows the presence of blood, add one or two drops of water to lyse the red cells.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Centrifuge again, discard the supernatant, and examine the sediment after covering it with a coverslip.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Motile microfilariae can be observed under 10x objective in reduced light.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If microfilariae are detected, prepare thin or thick smears from the specimen and stain them by Field’s stain followed by Delafield haematoxylin. The microfilariae can be identified as shown in Figs 3.6 and 3.7 on the basis of the presence or absence of sheath and arrangement of the nuclei in the tail.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="triple-concentration-technique">Triple concentration technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#triple-concentration-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Collect the first morning specimen of urine and measure the volume.</li>
<li>Add 1 ml of thiomersal per 100 ml of urine.</li>
<li>Place the specimen in a funnel fitted with a tubing and a clamp.</li>
<li>Allow the urine to settle in the funnel overnight.</li>
<li>Next day, withdraw 10 to 20 ml of urine through the tubing and centrifuge at 500g for 5 minutes.</li>
<li>Discard the supernatant and resuspend the sediment in saline.</li>
<li>Centrifuge again as above and examine the sediment of microfilariae using 10x objective.</li>
<li>Identify by staining thick or thin film of the sediment.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="filtration-technique-for-microfilariae">Filtration technique for microfilariae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#filtration-technique-for-microfilariae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This technique is useful in detecting infections of low density or in field surveys. The filtration technique is performed in the same way as for <em>Schistosoma</em> eggs, except that the pore size of the membrane used for microfilariae is 5 μm instead of 15-20 μm for <em>Schistosoma</em> eggs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="trichomonas-species-in-urine">Trichomonas Species in Urine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trichomonas-species-in-urine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em> is a flagellate protozoan which is a common cause of vaginitis in women and usually asymptomatic urethritis in men. It may</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>978 Parasitology</p>
<p>contaminate the urine and can be seen as a motile trophozoite in urinary sediments.</p>
<p>T.vaginalis measures about 15-25 µm in diameter and exhibits a characteristic jerky motility in fresh specimens. The undulating membrane extends along two-thirds of the body. It has three to five anterior flagella, a single nucleus and an axostyle which extends beyond the body (4.1a).</p>
<p>The urine may also be contaminated with Trichomonas hominis from faeces. T. hominis is smaller in size and its undulating membrane extends beyond the body as a free flagellum (Fig. 4.1b). Both the parasites do not have a cyst stage.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-3478a62a.jpeg" alt="img-80.jpeg">
Fig. 4.1 (a) Trichomonas vaginalis (b) Trichomonas hominis</p>
<p>The identification can be confirmed by staining a thin film from the sediment with Giemsa, Field's or acridine orange stain, but the parasites may get distorted while staining.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> Trichomoniasis is a sexually transmitted disease.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="parasites-in-aspirates">PARASITES IN ASPIRATES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#parasites-in-aspirates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Examination of aspirated material or tissue biopsies may help in the diagnosis of parasitic infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aspirate-from-lung-or-liver-abscess">Aspirate from Lung or Liver Abscess<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aspirate-from-lung-or-liver-abscess" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Examination of aspirates from lung or liver abscess may reveal trophozoites of Entamoeba histolytica. The parasites are trapped in the viscous pus and tissue debris at the periphery of the abscess. To see the typical motility, the aspirate should be treated with a proteolytic enzyme which will allow the parasite to move.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Add 1 ml of sputasol or 3% KOH to about 1 ml of pus, and incubate at 37°C for 30 minutes, shaking in between.</li>
<li>Centrifuge at 500xg for 5 minutes.</li>
<li>Examine the sediment microscopically as a wet mount and a permanent stained smear.</li>
<li>For identification characteristics of E. histolytica trophozoites, see Table 2.4, Fig. 2.5.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aspirate-from-hydatid-cyst">Aspirate from Hydatid Cyst<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aspirate-from-hydatid-cyst" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hydatid cyst is formed when the embryos of the parasitic tapeworm, Echinococcus granulosus migrate through the intestinal wall into the blood stream and are carried through the blood to the liver, lungs and other organs in the host. The hydatid cyst consists of an outer, thick, laminated cyst wall and an inner, thin, nucleated germinal layer. The brood capsules (Fig. 4.2) are produced in the lumen of the cyst from the germinal layer. Each brood capsule contains a number of protoscolices. When mature, the brood capsule may detach from the germinal layer and remain suspended in the cyst fluid. Such daughter cysts and individual scolices released from the wall of the cyst settle at the bottom and are called hydatid sand.</p>
<p>The diagnosis of hydatid cyst depends on the examination of the material aspirated from the cyst. However, the cyst fluid should be aspirated only after the surgical removal of the cyst. Leakage of the cyst fluid into the body can cause massive</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Examination of Urine, Aspirates, Body Fluids and Tissue Biopsies 979</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b07e0c59.jpeg" alt="img-81.jpeg">
Fig. 4.2 Hydatid cyst (schematic)</p>
<p>trinfection and allergic reaction. When old, the cyst may get calcified, and the daughter cysts disintegrated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>If the cyst material is fluid, centrifuge at 500g for 1 to 3 minutes.</li>
<li>Examine the sediment carefully using 10x and 40x objectives.</li>
<li>If the cyst material is viscous or solid, mix either with saline or 10% KOH before centrifuging as above.</li>
<li>Examine the sediment. Protoscolex of <em>E. granulosus</em> is colourless and round to oval in shape.</li>
<li>The characteristic hooks of the invaginated scolex can be easily seen with 40x objective.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>NOTE</strong>: Some cysts are sterile and contain no scolices or daughter cysts. Histological examination of the cyst wall can confirm the diagnosis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lung-biopsy-or-tracheo-bronchial-aspirate-for-pneumocystis-carinii">Lung Biopsy or Tracheo-Bronchial Aspirate for Pneumocystis carinii<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lung-biopsy-or-tracheo-bronchial-aspirate-for-pneumocystis-carinii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Pneumocystis carinii</em> can cause a very rapidly progressing pulmonary infection, especially in immunocompromised patients. It is a protozoan parasite which can be detected in the stained impression smears from a lung biopsy or in the sediment of tracheo-bronchial aspirate or occasionally in the sputum. Various staining techniques are available for staining the parasite. One of them is described here.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rapid-methenamine-silver-stain">Rapid methenamine silver stain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rapid-methenamine-silver-stain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The parasite is stained by deposition of acidified hot metal ions on the cell wall.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>5% chromic acid (w/v)</li>
<li>3% Methenamine
Hexamethylenetetramine 3 g
Distilled water 100 ml</li>
<li>1% sodium bisulphite (w/v)</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>980 Parasitology</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>2% sodium thiosulphate, (w/v)</li>
<li>Light green
Stock solution
Light green, S.F. (yellow) 0.2 g
Distilled water 100 ml
Acetic acid, glacial 0.2 ml
Working solution
Light green stock solution 10 ml
Distilled water 40 ml</li>
<li>5% Silver nitrate (w/v)</li>
<li>5% Borax (w/v)</li>
<li>0.2% Gold chloride
Stock solution
Prepare 1% solution as directed on the ampoule
Working solution
Gold Chloride stock solution 10 ml
Distilled water 40 ml</li>
<li>Methenamine silver nitrate solution
Stock solution
5% Silver nitrate (Solution 6) 5 ml
3% Methenamine (Solution 2) 100 ml
A white precipitate is formed as soon as the solutions are mixed, it dissolves subsequently on shaking. Store at 2–8°C. It is stable for one month.
Working solution
5% Borax solution (Solution 7) 2 ml
Distilled water 25 ml
Mix and add methenamine
Silver nitrate stock solution 25 ml</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
The working solution should be prepared fresh with every batch of staining, and should not be reused.</p>
<p>Technique</p>
<ul>
<li>The tissue from lung biopsy should be ground and then smeared on the slide.</li>
<li>Tracheo-bronchial aspirate should be centrifuged, and the smears prepared from the sediment.</li>
<li>Air-dry and fix the smears in methyl alcohol for five minutes.</li>
</ul>
<p>Control: A known positive smear for Pneumocystis carinii must be stained along with patient smears.</p>
<ul>
<li>Place 5% chromic acid and working methenamine silver stain in two separate Coplin jars and place them in a water bath at 80°C.</li>
<li>Keep the smears in 5% chromic acid at 80°C for two minutes for oxidation.</li>
<li>Wash the slides quickly in running tap water.</li>
<li>Keep the slides in 1% sodium bisulphite for 30 seconds.</li>
<li>Rinse the slides in three changes of distilled water.</li>
<li>Place the slides in the working methenamine silver nitrate solution at 80°C for 4 ½ minutes.</li>
<li>Rinse again with three changes of distilled water. Remove excess stain by wiping the back of the slide with a filter paper.</li>
<li>Tone in 0.2% gold chloride for 30 seconds.</li>
<li>Rinse in three changes of distilled water.</li>
<li>Place the smears in 2% sodium thiosulphate for 30 seconds.</li>
<li>Rinse in three changes of distilled water.</li>
<li>Counterstain in working light green solution for 30 seconds.</li>
<li>Rinse in three changes of distilled water.</li>
<li>Dehydrate in two changes each of 95% and absolute ethanol for 30 seconds.</li>
<li>Clear in two changes of xylene for 30 seconds.</li>
<li>Mount in Permount or DPX.</li>
</ul>
<p>Results
Pneumocystis Carinii The parasite shows a delicately stained wall, grayish or brownish in colour. The interior is transparent and may show black structures called parentheses.
RBC Mucin: Rose to dark gray.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Examination of Urine, Aspirates, Body Fluids and Tissue Biopsies 981</p>
<p>Background: Pale green</p>
<p>The methenamine silver nitrate stain can also detect fungi in tissues. Fungi stain gray to black in colour.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examination-of-lymph-nodes-spleen-liver-aspirates-bone-marrow-spinal-fluid-and-eye-specimens">EXAMINATION OF LYMPH NODES, SPLEEN, LIVER ASPIRATES; BONE MARROW; SPINAL FLUID AND EYE SPECIMENS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-lymph-nodes-spleen-liver-aspirates-bone-marrow-spinal-fluid-and-eye-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A specimen, either tissue or fluid, obtained from these sites should be processed as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>A portion of the fluid material should be examined as a wet mount, under low power (10X) and high power (40X) objectives for the detection of motile parasites. If necessary, the fluid should be centrifuged and the sediment examined.</li>
<li>Prepare impression smears from the tissues by pressing a small portion of the tissue between the two slides, and then pulling them apart by sliding in opposite directions. Allow the smear to air-dry and stain using appropriate stain, e.g. Giemsa as for a thin blood film.</li>
<li>Send a portion of the material from a tissue to a Histopathology laboratory for section cutting and staining. The parasites expected to be present in the above mentioned specimens are listed in Table 1.2. Identification of parasites such as trypanosomes, leishmania, microfilaria, amoebae, etc. has been described in previous pages. Identification of some more parasites is discussed below.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="identification-of-naegleria-fowleri">Identification of Naegleria fowleri<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-naegleria-fowleri" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Naegleria fowleri is an amoebic parasite which can be found in spinal fluid, exudate or tissue fragments.</p>
<p>In a direct wet mount or centrifuged deposit of the specimen, Naegleria fowleri appears as an elongated, amoeboid, actively motile trophozoite, measuring <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mo>−</mo><mn>22</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>7</mn><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10-22 \times 7 \mu \mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">22</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">7</span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>. It has a broad anterior and tapered posterior end (Fig. 4.3a). The nucleus has a large central karyosome and peripheral nuclear chromatin is absent. The cytoplasm is granular, and contains vacuoles, but no ingested red blood cells. Unlike Entamoeba histolytica, which cannot remain motile for a long time outside the host, Naegleria trophozoites can show motility for several hours at room temperature, and for up to 24 hours at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>37</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">7</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>.</p>
<p>To confirm the identity of <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>N</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">N</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">N</span></span></span></span></span>. fowleri, the amoebae may be transferred to water and incubated at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>27</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">27^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">7</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> to <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>37</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">7</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>. Within two to twenty hours, the amoeboid trophozoite transforms into a flagellate. The flagellate formed is pearshaped, with two flagella at the broad end, and show a typical jerky motility (Fig. 4.3b).</p>
<p>Cyst forms (Fig. 4.3c) do not appear in the body of the host, but may be seen in culture or in nature.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-8a900cb9.jpeg" alt="img-82.jpeg">
Fig. 4.3 Naegleria fowleri (a) trophozoite (b) flagellate and (c) cyst forms</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> If not observed carefully, <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>N</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">N</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">N</span></span></span></span></span>. fowleri in cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) may be mistaken for polymorphs and vice versa.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>982 Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="identification-of-acanthamoeba-species">Identification of Acanthamoeba Species<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-acanthamoeba-species" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The trophozoites of <em>Acanthamoeba</em> have spine-like pseudopodia, and the progressive movement is very slow. It is larger in size than <em>N. fowleri</em> (15–25 μm in diameter). The nucleus with a large karyosome is very clearly visible in unstained wet preparations (Fig. 4.4a).</p>
<p>Unlike <em>Naegleria fowleri</em> the cysts may be seen in the clinical specimens. The cyst is round with a large nucleus and a prominent nucleolus. The cyst wall is double, with a slightly wrinkled outer wall (Fig. 4.4b).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-c856f6f7.jpeg" alt="img-83.jpeg">
Fig. 4.4 Acanthamoeba species; (a) trophozoite, (b) cyst forms</p>
<p>Acanthamoeba species do not exist in flagellate form.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="examination-of-corneal-scrapings-for-acanthamoeba-cysts">Examination of corneal scrapings for Acanthamoeba cysts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-corneal-scrapings-for-acanthamoeba-cysts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Smear the corneal scrapings on the slide and dry in air.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Fix in absolute methanol for three-five minutes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Prepare the staining solution as follows:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Calcafluor white (CFW) 0.1 g</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Evans blue 0.1 g</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Distilled water 100 ml</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dissolve the two stains separately in distilled water, mix and make the volume up to 100 ml.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Stain the scrapings with the staining solution for five minutes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Drain the slide and allow the excess stain to run off. Do not wash.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Add coverslip and examine with fluorescent microscope for apple-green fluorescence of amoebic cysts.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Note</strong> The CFW does not stain the trophozoites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="identification-of-toxoplasma-gondii">Identification of Toxoplasma gondii<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-toxoplasma-gondii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Following ingestion of the infective oocysts, toxoplasma parasites become intracellular and multiply in the lymph glands, liver, muscle, central nervous system and other internal organs.</p>
<p><em>Toxoplasma gondii</em> is found in two forms in humans. In the early, more acute phases of infection, the actively proliferating trophozoites, called tachyzoites are seen intracellularly in phagocytes. The tachyzoites are crescent shaped measuring 2–3 × 4–8 μm. One end is more rounded than the other (Fig. 4.5a). The parasite can be seen with Giemsa stain. The cytoplasm stains blue and the nucleus, situated at the broader end, appears red.</p>
<p>The cysts are formed in chronic infections. They are PAS (periodic acid-Schiff) positive (Histopathology section). The parasites within the cysts are called bradyzoites, which multiply to form the cyst (Fig. 4.5b).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-fa020e15.jpeg" alt="img-84.jpeg">
Fig. 4.5 Toxoplasma gondii: (a) tachyzoites, (b) cyst form</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Examination of Urine, Aspirates, Body Fluids and Tissue Biopsies 983</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
Most Toxoplasma infections in adults are asymptomatic. Severe and often fatal infections can occur in immunocompromised patients. Intrauterine congenital infection in a foetus may result in abortion or still-birth, or an infant with mental defects.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examination-of-muscle-for-larvae-of-trichinella-spiralis">Examination of Muscle for Larvae of Trichinella spiralis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-muscle-for-larvae-of-trichinella-spiralis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The adult worm produces larvae in the small intestine which migrate to the skeletal muscles via the blood stream and the lymphatics. The larvae encyst in the muscles and often become calcified. The suitable sites for muscle biopsy are the shoulder, the outer thigh and calf of the leg. There are two techniques for the detection of Trichinella larvae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="direct-slide-technique">Direct slide technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#direct-slide-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Cut the muscle biopsy into small pieces.</li>
<li>Press these between two slides, and examine using 10x objective with reduced light.</li>
<li>The larvae of Trichinella are seen coiled inside cysts that lie longitudinally along the muscle fibres and measure about 0.5 × 0.2 mm. Some larvae may calcify and appear black.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="digestion-technique">Digestion technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#digestion-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Prepare the digestive fluid as follows:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pepsin 5 g</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hydrochloric Acid (conc.) 7 ml</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Distilled water 1000 ml</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dissolve pepsin in the water and add conc. HCl.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cut the tissue into small pieces (2–3mm) and add to about 20 ml of digestive fluid. (1 part tissue for 20 parts fluid).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Mix and incubate at 37°C overnight.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Next morning centrifuge at 500g for two-three minutes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Discard the supernatant carefully and resuspend the sediment in 10% formol saline.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Centrifuge again as above, discard the supernatant and examine the sediment under 10x objective for larvae.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
The muscle can also be stained by haematoxylin and eosin stain (refer to Histopathology section).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="larval-stages-of-cestodes-in-tissues">Larval Stages of Cestodes in Tissues<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#larval-stages-of-cestodes-in-tissues" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cestodes other than Echinococcus granulosus, the causative agent of Hydatid disease, can also form cysts in various tissues of the body. For example, the larva of Taenia solium causes Cysticercosis; the larva of Multiceps can cause Coenurosis. The diagnosis depends on the surgical removal of the cyst and identification of the larva.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="skin-snips-for-microfilariae">Skin Snips for Microfilariae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#skin-snips-for-microfilariae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A bloodless skin snip should be collected by using a sterile needle and a razor blade.</li>
<li>Clean the skin with an alcohol swab and dry.</li>
<li>Insert the needle almost horizontally into the skin, and lift it so that a small cone, about 2 mm in size, is formed.</li>
<li>Cut off the piece with a sterile razor blade.</li>
<li>Immediately place the skin snip in a centrifuge tube containing about 2 ml of saline.</li>
<li>Incubate at room temperature overnight.</li>
<li>Remove the skin snip with forceps, place on a slide and cover with a cover slip.</li>
<li>Centrifuge the tube at 500g for five minutes.</li>
<li>Examine the sediment microscopically under 10x objective.</li>
<li>The microfilariae may be identified as shown in Figs 3.6 and 3.7.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="examination-of-sputum-for-eggs-of-paragonimus-westermani">Examination of Sputum for Eggs of Paragonimus westermani<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-sputum-for-eggs-of-paragonimus-westermani" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Collect a deep-sputum specimen from the lower respiratory passages, and not the saliva. In paragonimus infection, the sputum may be tinged with brown flecks or 'iron filings' which are clusters of eggs, and may be streaked with blood.</p>
<p>Examine the specimen directly as a wet mount. If the sputum is too thick, digest it with an equal volume of 3% sodium hydroxide (w/v) solution and leave for 10 minutes to dissolve the mucus. Mix and centrifuge at 500g for five minutes. Examine the sediment as a wet mount.</p>
<p>Eggs of <em>P. westermani</em> are yellow-brown to brown in colour, measuring 70–100 × 50–65 μm, slightly flattened on one side. The operculum is flat and has shoulders. The egg shell is thicker at the end opposite the operculum. The egg of <em>P. westermani</em> may be mistaken for that of <em>D. latum</em> due to their striking resemblance (Fig. 4.6).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-a5833ec7.jpeg" alt="img-85.jpeg">
Fig. 4.6 Eggs of (a) Diphyllobothrium latum and (b) Paragonimus westermani</p>
<p>Other parasites that may be found in sputum are larvae of <em>Ascaris lumbricoides</em>, <em>Strongyloides stercoralis</em> and hookworm; <em>Echinococcus granulosus</em> scolices, <em>Pneumocystis carinii</em> and trophozoites of <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="examination-of-rectal-or-bladder-biopsy-for-schistosoma-eggs">Examination of Rectal or Bladder Biopsy for Schistosoma Eggs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-rectal-or-bladder-biopsy-for-schistosoma-eggs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In a chronic infection with <em>Schistosoma mansoni</em> or <em>Schistosoma japonicum</em>, the eggs may not be seen in the stool. However, the eggs may be detected by an examination of rectal mucosa. <em>Schistosoma haematobium</em> eggs may be recovered in the bladder biopsy.</p>
<p><strong>Direct examination</strong> Compress small pieces of fresh tissue between two microscope slides and examine using the 10x objective.</p>
<p><strong>Notes</strong> As the treatment of schistosomiasis depends on the viability of eggs, the eggs should be carefully observed using 40x objective for the flickering motion of the flame cells of the miracidium (Fig. 4.7).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-483b040d.jpeg" alt="img-86.jpeg">
Fig. 4.7 Egg of Schistosoma mansoni</p>
<p><strong>Examination after digestion</strong> Digest small pieces of tissue in 4% potassium hydroxide or 4% sodium hydroxide at 60 to 80°C for two to three hours. The digested material may then be concentrated by centrifugation and examined.</p>
<p><strong>Identification of schistosoma eggs</strong> Eggs of <em>Schistosoma haematobium</em> and <em>S. mansoni</em> are</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Examination of Urine, Aspirates, Body Fluids and Tissue Biopsies 985</p>
<p>pale and yellow-brown, large (145 × 55 μm) and oval in shape. They have a characteristic terminal (S. haematobium) or lateral (S. mansoni) spine.</p>
<p>Eggs of S. japonicum are small in size (70 × 50 μm) and have a minute lateral spine. Each egg contains a fully developed miracidium (Fig. 4.8).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b2e7b597.jpeg" alt="img-87.jpeg">
Fig. 4.8 Differentiation of Schistosoma eggs</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 5</p>
<p>Serology and Culture of Parasites</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="serological-diagnosis">SEROLOGICAL DIAGNOSIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#serological-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Serological investigation is useful in the diagnosis of parasitic diseases and in the epidemiology and control of parasitic infections. The serological techniques are directed either to the detection of parasitic antigens or detection of antibodies specific for antigens of parasites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="antigens-of-parasites">Antigens of parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antigens-of-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The antigens that evoke antibody response are somatic components of parasites and the metabolic products of their secretion and excretion. These antigens may be found in the specimens such as serum, urine, cerebrospinal fluid and faeces. Positive tests based on the antigen detection provide the evidence of current infection and therefore, are of great value in diagnosis. The development of useful antigen detection tests is recent and reagents for only a few tests are available commercially.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="antibodies-to-parasites">Antibodies to parasites<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antibodies-to-parasites" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Depending on the antigenic nature of the parasite and its location in the body, a host reaction is initiated involving various subpopulations of cells such as T- and B-lymphocytes, macrophages and inflammatory cells. The levels and types of specific antibodies produced will depend upon the fate of the parasite in the body of the host. The sensitivity and specificity of tests for antibody detection in parasitic disease vary greatly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="considerations-in-serodiagnosis-of-parasitic-infections">Considerations in Serodiagnosis of Parasitic Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#considerations-in-serodiagnosis-of-parasitic-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Human parasites can be divided into two broad categories</p>
<ol>
<li>Parasites that multiply within the host, e.g. protozoa.</li>
<li>Parasites that mature within the host but never multiply, e.g. Schistosomes.</li>
</ol>
<p>Protozoan parasites which undergo multiplication in the human host provide a continuous antigenic stimulus to the immune system of the host as the infection progresses. In such cases, there is usually good correlation between the clinical symptoms and the serological results.</p>
<p>Helminths, on the other hand, migrate through the body and pass through various developmental stages before maturing into adult forms. Such infections, generally, are difficult to confirm serologically for two reasons. The antigenic stimulus may be limited, and it may not be possible to use an appropriate antigen in the test system. Because of the complexity of the structure of parasites, most antigens used in serodiagnosis are heterogeneous mixtures which are not well defined. The use of such antigen leads to cross-reactivity.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Serology and Culture of Parasites 987</p>
<p>and low sensitivity. Even though the parasites and their products are immunogenic for the host, the immune response is generally not protective. The developed immunity may be specific only for a stage, strain or species.</p>
<p>Antibodies of various subclasses of immunoglobulins can be generated. The initial response is IgM antibodies, later shifting to IgG. IgE response is more likely to be provoked by helminths that invade the tissues than by protozoa. Some antigens even provoke cellular reactivity as shown by skin tests.</p>
<p>Interpretation of test results may also present problems, particularly for patients from endemic areas where the baseline titres may be higher than non-endemic areas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-serological-tests-in-use">Types of Serological Tests in Use<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-serological-tests-in-use" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In spite of the problems in sensitivity, specificity and interpretation, some serological tests that have proved useful are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Complement fixation (CF)</li>
<li>Indirect hemagglutination (IHA)</li>
<li>Indirect fluorescent antibody (IFA)</li>
<li>Soluble antigen fluorescent antibody (SAFA)</li>
<li>Bentonite flocculation (BF)</li>
<li>Latex agglutination (LA)</li>
<li>Double diffusion (DD)</li>
<li>Counterelectrophoresis (CEP)</li>
<li>Immunoelectrophoresis (IE)</li>
<li>Radioimmunoassay</li>
<li>Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assays (ELISA).</li>
</ol>
<p>Table 5.1 shows various serological tests performed in CDC, Atlanta for the diagnosis of parasitic infections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="culture-techniques">CULTURE TECHNIQUES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#culture-techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For routine diagnosis, very few parasites are cultured. The methods for culture are often complex, while quality control is difficult. For these reasons, very few laboratories practise specific culture techniques for parasites. The culture methods, however, are very useful for research purposes. Parasites which can be cultured in the laboratory include <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em>, <em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em>, <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em>, <em>Leishmania</em> species, <em>Toxoplasma gondii</em>, <em>Naegleria fowleri</em> and <em>Acanthamoeba</em> species.</p>
<p><em>E. histolytica</em> grows readily under partial anaerobic conditions in a nutrient medium containing bacteria and rice flour. On such a non-cellular medium, other intestinal amoebae may grow for only a few generations. An example of a medium for <em>E. histolytica</em> is Dobell and Laidlaw’s medium.</p>
<p>Novy, MacNeal, Nicolle (NNN) medium is a diphasic medium used for culturing <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em> and <em>Leishmania</em>. The medium contains 30% defibrinated rabbit blood along with antibiotics such as penicillin and streptomycin or gentamicin to prevent bacterial contamination.</p>
<p>Cell culture media can be used for the isolation of <em>Toxoplasma gondii</em> and <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em>. <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em> can be grown in human red cells. However, this technique is less sensitive than detection of the parasite in the direct blood smear.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="animal-inoculation">Animal Inoculation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#animal-inoculation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In some suspected parasitic infections, animal inoculation may be requested and can be helpful in reaching a diagnosis. Animal inoculation can not be preferred to other more routine procedures for the diagnosis of parasitic infections. Sometimes a detectable infection in the laboratory animal may develop very slowly and may take several months before a diagnosis can be made. Moreover, parasites already present in the animal may produce misleading results. Many parasites exhibit host specificity, thus limiting the types of animals available for the diagnosis. <em>Leishmania</em> species can be recovered by animal inoculation in young (2–4</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>988 Parasitology</p>
<p>Table 5.1 Serological tests for parasitic infections (CDC reference)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Disease</th><th>Test</th><th>Diagnostic Titres</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>African trypanosomiasis</td><td>IFA</td><td>≥ 16</td></tr><tr><td>Amoebiasis</td><td>IHA</td><td>≥ 256</td></tr><tr><td>Ascariasis</td><td>ELISA</td><td>≥ 32</td></tr><tr><td>Chagas' disease</td><td>CF, DAT, IHA</td><td>≥ 8, ≥ 128, ≥ 64</td></tr><tr><td>Cysticercosis</td><td>IHA</td><td>≥ 64</td></tr><tr><td>Echinococcosis</td><td>IHA</td><td>≥ 256</td></tr><tr><td>Fascioliasis</td><td>IHA</td><td>≥ 128</td></tr><tr><td>Filariasis</td><td>IHA</td><td>≥ 128</td></tr><tr><td>Leishmaniasis</td><td>DAT, IIF</td><td>≥ 64,≥ 16</td></tr><tr><td>Malaria</td><td>IIF</td><td>≥ 64</td></tr><tr><td>Paragonimiasis</td><td>CF</td><td>≥ 8</td></tr><tr><td>Pneumocystosis</td><td>IIF</td><td>≥ 16</td></tr><tr><td>Schistosomiasis</td><td>IIF</td><td>≥ 16</td></tr><tr><td>Strongyloidiasis</td><td>IHA</td><td>≥ 64</td></tr><tr><td>Toxocariasis</td><td>ELISA</td><td>≥ 32</td></tr><tr><td>Toxoplasmosis</td><td>IIF, IgM-IIF</td><td>≥ 256, ≥ 16, ≥ 256</td></tr><tr><td>Trichinosis</td><td>BFT</td><td>≥ 15</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p>months) hamsters whereas <em>Trypanosoma</em> and <em>Toxoplasma</em> can infect many laboratory animals.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="xenodiagnosis">Xenodiagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#xenodiagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a technique in which an arthropod host is used as an indicator of infection. For example, uninfected reduviid bugs are allowed to feed on the blood of a patient suspected to be suffering from <em>Trypanosoma</em> infection (Chaga’s disease). After 30 to 60 days, the faeces from the bugs are examined for the presence of the parasites.</p>
<p>This technique can also be used to diagnose an infection with <em>Trichinella spiralis</em>. A muscle tissue from the patient is fed to uninfected rats. After an appropriate period, the rats are checked for the presence of larvae. However, this procedure is not in common use.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 6</p>
<p>Intestinal Protozoan Parasites</p>
<p>Note</p>
<p>For all the laboratory techniques in this chapter, refer to Chapter 2.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="i-intestinal-amoebae">I. INTESTINAL AMOEBAE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-intestinal-amoebae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="entamoeba-histolytica">ENTAMOEBA HISTOLYTICA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#entamoeba-histolytica" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>E. histolytica is distributed worldwide and is endemic in many parts of tropical and subtropical countries. Infection with E. histolytica is called amoebiasis. The main cause of infection is inadequate sanitation and poor personal hygiene.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infection with E. histolytica occurs by ingesting infective cysts in water contaminated with faeces or food contaminated by flies or food handlers.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-868988b1.jpeg" alt="img-88.jpeg">
Fig. 6.1 Life cycle of Entamoeba histolytica</p>
<p>Following ingestion of the cyst, excystation of the parasite occurs in the slightly alkaline environment of the intestine and it develops into a trophozoite. In the large intestine the trophozoite starts multiplying by binary fission.</p>
<p>Some trophozoites condense into a round mass to form a cyst. The reason for the formation of cysts is not clearly understood. The immature cyst with a single nucleus matures to form a cyst with four nuclei. This is the infective stage of the parasite. Once formed, the cyst does not excyst in the same host. Trophozoites and cysts are excreted in faeces. The cysts survive under the unfavourable environmental conditions for a considerable length of time until they are ingested by another host. Trophozoites, on the other hand, are not infective to other hosts. They die quickly outside the human body.</p>
<p>From the intestine, the trophozoites may invade the blood stream. They may be carried to the liver, lungs, brain or other body tissues where they can form amoebic abscesses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Trophozoite: Living trophozoites vary in size from <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mo>−</mo><mn>60</mn><mtext> </mtext><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10 - 60\,\mu\mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">60</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>. The motility is described as rapid and unidirectional, with pseudopods forming quickly in response to the environment. Trophozoites can be seen only in loose, fresh stool.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>990 Parasitology</p>
<p>In the trichrome or iron-haematoxylin stain, the nucleus shows evenly arranged chromatin on the nuclear membrane and a small centrally located karyosome. The cytoplasm is finely granular and may show vacuoles and ingested red blood cells (Fig. 2.5)</p>
<p>Cyst The trophozoites may condense into a round mass to form a precyst or immature cyst under unfavourable conditions. The precyst is also seen in formed stools. It contains a glycogen mass and highly refractile chromatoid bars with smooth ends. The cyst slowly matures and contains up to four nuclei. The glycogen completely disappears and chromatoid bars may also disappear. A mature cyst may measure 10–20 μm in diameter.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> Cysts of <em>E. hartmani</em> are similar in morphology to <em>E. histolytica</em>, but usually measure less than 10 μm. They are non-pathogenic.</p>
<p>The morphology of trophozoites and cysts of various amoebae are shown in Tables 2.4 and 2.5 and Fig. 2.5. All the other amoebae described except <em>E. histolytica</em> are non-pathogenic, and therefore, should not be mistaken for it. <em>E. coli</em> is the most commonly encountered species of non-pathogenic amoebae. <em>Dientamoeba fragilis</em>, if found in large numbers in an immunocompromised host, is considered pathogenic.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The disease, amoebiasis or amoebic dysentry, is caused by pathogenic strains of <em>E. histolytica</em> invading the walls of the large intestine. They can cause acute attacks of dysentry with blood and mucus, accompanied by low abdominal pain. Amoebic dysentry can be differentiated from bacillary dysentry by the features shown in Table 6.1.</p>
<p>Amoebae produce typical flask shaped ulcers in the intestinal mucosa. Sometimes, the amoebae may invade the blood stream through the intestinal wall and invade other organs such as the lung, liver, brain and other tissues. However, 90% of infections with <em>E. histolytica</em> are asymptomatic.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis of intestinal amoebiasis is by demonstration of trophozoites or cysts of <em>E. histolytica</em> in faeces or rectal scrapings.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> Presence of <em>E. histolytica</em> trophozoites with ingested red cells indicates an active infection.</p>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis of extraintestinal amoebiasis is by:</p>
<ol>
<li>Demonstration of <em>E. histolytica</em> trophozoites in the aspirate from the liver, lung or tissue abscess.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Note</strong> Aspiration is not recommended for diagnostic purpose. If aspiration has been done as part of treatment, it can be sent to the laboratory for investigation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="table-6-1-characteristics-of-fresh-faeces-in-bacillary-and-amoebic-dysentry">Table 6.1 Characteristics of fresh faeces in bacillary and amoebic dysentry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#table-6-1-characteristics-of-fresh-faeces-in-bacillary-and-amoebic-dysentry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Bacillary dysentry</th><th>Amoebic dysentry</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Odour</td><td>Odourless</td><td>Offensive odour</td></tr><tr><td>Consistency</td><td>Often watery, with blood</td><td>Loose, with blood and mucus</td></tr><tr><td>pH</td><td>Alkaline</td><td>Acidic</td></tr><tr><td>Pus cells</td><td>Many</td><td>Fewer</td></tr><tr><td>Motile trophozoites of</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>E. histolytica</td><td>Absent</td><td>Present</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Intestinal Protozoan Parasites 991</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Demonstration of antibodies to <em>E. histolytica</em> in diagnostic titres.</li>
