<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="blood-and-tissue-protozoa">Blood and Tissue Protozoa<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-and-tissue-protozoa" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>For laboratory techniques in this chapter, refer to Chapters 3 and 4.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-amoebae">I. AMOEBAE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-amoebae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="naegleria-fowleri">NAEGLERIA FOWLERI<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#naegleria-fowleri" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Naegleria fowleri is reported worldwide, but it is mainly in temperate regions, as a cause of primary amoebic meningoencephalitis (PAM) in children and young adults. Naegleria fowleri is a free-living amoeba, which normally inhabits soil and stagnant fresh water.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The amoeba <em>N. fowleri</em> occurs both as a trophozoite and as a cyst, the stage primarily depending on the environmental conditions. The trophozoite has two forms, amoeboid and flagellate, which is a unique feature. The flagellate form is found in water or moist soil, whereas the amoeboid form is seen in humans.</p>
<p>The infection is transmitted by bathing in contaminated stagnant water. The amoebae enter through the nose and invade the brain through the olfactory nerve. In the tissue, they multiply by binary fission.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The amoeboid form is elongated showing narrow posterior and broad anterior ends, and measures about 7–20 μm. The karyosome is large and centrally placed, and there is no peripheral chromatin on the nuclear membrane. The granular cytoplasm may contain vacuoles.</p>
<p>The amoeboid form may change to the flagellate form by incubating in water at 27°C to 37°C. The change may occur within 2 to 20 hours. The flagellate form is not capable of reproduction.</p>
<p>The cyst is about 7–10 μm in diameter, has a thick wall and a nucleus similar to that of the amoebic form.</p>
<p>Fig. 4.3 shows the two forms of the trophozoite, amoebic and flagellate, and the cyst of Naegleria fowleri.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Primary amoebic encephalitis (PAM) is an acute, suppurative infection of brain and meninges. It can be rapidly fatal. Early symptoms are upper respiratory distress, headache, lethargy and olfactory dysfunction. It may progress to fever, vomiting and stiffness of the neck, mental confusion and coma about three to five days prior to death. Mainly children and young adults are affected.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis of PAM by <em>N. fowleri</em> is by</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Parasitology</p>
<ul>
<li>Detection of motile amoebae in Cerebrospinal Fluid (CSF)</li>
<li>Confirmation of <em>N. fowleri</em> by its ability to transform to a flagellate.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimen-cerebrospinal-fluid-csf">Specimen Cerebrospinal fluid (CSF)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimen-cerebrospinal-fluid-csf" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques-1-direct-detection-of-n-fowleri-in-csf">Techniques 1. Direct detection of <em>N. fowleri</em> in CSF<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques-1-direct-detection-of-n-fowleri-in-csf" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><em>Naegleria fowleri</em> can be detected in a wet preparation of uncentrifuged purulent CSF or of centrifuged sediment. The amoebae are actively motile and remain so for up to 24 hours at 35°C. A cyst form is never seen in CSF.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> The CSF in PAM appears similar as in bacterial meningitis, but the Gram stain does not show any bacteria.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Giemsa stained smear of the sediment will show the typical amoebae (Fig. 4.3).</li>
<li>The amoebae develop flagella in distilled water at 27°–37°C within 2–20 hours.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="acanthamoeba-species">ACANTHAMOEBA SPECIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acanthamoeba-species" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is another free-living amoeba which can cause meningoencephalitis and can also cause eye infections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The life cycle is similar to that of <em>N. fowleri</em>, except that it does not have a flagellate form.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Trophozoites are about 30 μm in diameter and have spine-like pseudopodia, which give it the name, <em>Acanthamoeba</em>. The motility is very slow.</p>
<p>Cysts are double-walled with somewhat wrinkled outer wall (Fig. 4.4). The nucleus has a central, large karyosome.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Acanthamoeba</em> causes a more chronic form of meningoencephalitis and is usually associated with trauma or other underlying disease.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The laboratory diagnosis is similar to that of <em>N. fowleri</em> PAM, i.e. by detection of amoebae and cyst in CSF and is by permanent stained smear. There is no flagellate form.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ii-flagellates">II. FLAGELLATES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-flagellates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="trichomonas-vaginalis">TRICHOMONAS VAGINALIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trichomonas-vaginalis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Urogenital infection caused by <em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em> has a worldwide occurrence.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><em>T. vaginalis</em> infection is a sexually transmitted disease. The flagellates live and multiply by binary fission in the urogenital tract of both men and women. The normal body sites are prostate and vagina. The parasite exists only as a trophozoite, there is no known cystic stage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The trophozoite of <em>T. vaginalis</em> is round or oval in shape measuring 10–15 μm × 5–15 μm. The axostyle is very prominent and extends beyond the posterior end. The undulating membrane stops about 2/3rd along the body-length. There are three–five anterior flagella (Fig. 4.1). It has jerky motility.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><em>T. vaginalis</em> is site specific, and usually cannot survive outside the urogenital system. There is inflam</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Blood and Tissue Protozoa 999</p>
<p>mation of the vagina with yellowish-green, frothy, foul smelling discharge which may be accompanied by itching. The infection in males is usually asymptomatic.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis is by detection of <em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em> in the vaginal or urethral specimens.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimen">Specimen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Vaginal swab.</li>
<li>Urethral swab.</li>
<li>Urethral discharge after prostatic massage.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Direct detection of <em>T. vaginalis</em> in saline wet mounts: It can be identified by its jerky motility and morphology.</li>
<li>Permanent thin smear, stained by
(a) Giemsa stain
(b) Acridine orange stain.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li><em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em> may also be seen in urine specimens contaminated with vaginal or prostatic discharge.</li>
<li>Occasionally, <em>Trichomonas hominis</em>, a non-pathogen from intestinal tract, may contaminate the urine or vaginal discharge. It can be differentiated from <em>T. vaginalis</em> on the basis of its morphology (Fig. 4.1).</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iii-haemoflagellates">III HAEMOFLAGELLATES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-haemoflagellates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Haemoflagellates are actively motile flagellated protozoa that live mainly in the blood and lymph. <em>Leishmania</em> and <em>Trypanosoma</em> are two genera of haemoflagellates which are pathogenic to humans. In addition to blood and lymph, some species of these parasites also parasitize reticuloendothelial cells of the haemopoietic organs, muscle, nervous tissue and skin.</p>
<p>The haemoflagellates of humans exist in four morphological forms (Fig. 7.1).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-88b9e78f.jpeg" alt="img-93.jpeg">
Fig. 7.1 Stages of development of trypanosomes</p>
<ol>
<li>Amastigote form The amastigote form is an intracellular parasite living in the cells of reticuloendothelial (RE) system. It is oval in shape, measuring about <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1.5</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>5</mn><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1.5 \times 5 \mu \mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">1.5</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span> and contains a nucleus and a kinetoplast.</li>
<li>Promastigote form The parasite in this stage is a slender (10–15 μm in length), motile organism with a single anterior flagellum, kinetoplast and a central nucleus. It develops in the insect host and is the infective form of <em>Leishmania</em> species for humans.</li>
<li>Epimastigote form In the insect hosts, <em>Trypanosomes</em> develop into flagellated forms with Kinetoplast placed anteriorly, close to the central nucleus. A flagellum originates at the kinetoplast, and is attached to the cell wall except at the anterior tip, where it terminates with a free end. Since</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1000 Parasitology</p>
<p>the flagellum is constantly moving, it pulls the cell wall into irregular extensions, the feature being known as undulating membrane. The epimastigote form has a short undulating membrane.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Trypomastigote form This is a flagellated form of the parasite which lives extracellularly in the blood and the lymph of man. The kinetoplast is situated posterior to the nucleus. The flagellum originates from the kinetoplast and runs along the length of the body, forming a long undulating membrane. The flagellum extends anteriorly as a free flagellum. In some species of Trypanosoma, there may be a variation in the appearance of trypomastigotes, some being long and slender while others are short and stumpy. Giemsa stain may show dark blue volutin granules in the cytoplasm.</li>
</ol>
<p>Table 7.1 shows the major pathogenic species and developmental forms of haemoflagellates.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="trypanosomes">TRYPANOSOMES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trypanosomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The major pathogens in the genus Trypanosoma include T. gambiense, T. rhodesiense and T. cruzi. T. gambiense and T. rhodesiense are closely related and belong to the Trypanosoma brucei group which causes African trypanosomiasis or sleeping sickness. T. cruzi causes American trypanosomiasis or Chaga's disease. Life cycles of both the groups are shown in Figures 7.2 and 7.3. They involve alternate existence in man and in insect host. There is a cyclic development in a blood sucking insect such as a tsetse fly or reduviid bug. It becomes infective to man as a trypomastigote form.</p>
<p>African trypanosomiasis or sleeping sickness is a wasting disease which can be fatal if not treated in time. The disease starts as a painful swelling (chancre) at the site of the bite by a tsetse fly. The chancre contains trypomastigotes multiplying by longitudinal binary fission. The organisms then enter the blood stream and the reticuloendothelial system. This results in the enlargement of lymph nodes, spleen and liver. The patient has intermittent fever with night sweats, headache, malaise and anorexia. In the late stages of the disease, the trypanosomes invade the central nervous system showing symptoms such as tremors, progressive mental dullness and excessive sleeping. There is rapid weight loss, and death is usually due to secondary infection.</p>
<p>American trypanosomiasis or Chaga's disease is transmitted when infective trypomastigotes in the faeces of reduviid bug are rubbed over the site of the insect bite or on exposed mucous membranes. The trypanosomes are engulfed by histiocytes at the site of inoculation where they multiply intracellularly as amastigotes. The amastigotes develop into trypomastigotes, and when the host cell ruptures, are carried by the blood stream and can infect virtually every organ or tissue in the body.</p>
<p>At the site of inoculation, a painful, erythematous, subcutaneous nodule, called chagoma, develops. This may contain the parasite for a few days. As the infection spreads, the regional lymph nodes also become enlarged.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b5d15fb8.jpeg" alt="img-94.jpeg">
Fig. 7.2 Life cycle of Trypanosoma gambiense and T. rhodesiense</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>

Salivary glands and gut of insects</td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>

<li>Intestine and salivary glands of insects | Tsetse flies (Glossina)  |
|  <em>T. rhodesiense</em> | None | None | 1. Salivary glands and gut of insects</li>

<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1002 Parasitology</p>
<p>Trypomastigotes appear in the blood about 10 days after the infection and remain there throughout the acute phase. The acute phase presents as high fevers, hepatosplenomegaly, erythematous rash, acute myocarditis, lymphadenopathy and oedema of face, legs and feet. The central nervous system may also be involved. After the acute phase, the patient may die, or recover and enter into a chronic phase. Very few parasites may be detected in blood in the chronic stage. Congenital transmission can occur in both acute and chronic forms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis-of-african-trypanosomiasis-sleeping-sickness">Laboratory Diagnosis of African Trypanosomiasis (Sleeping Sickness)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis-of-african-trypanosomiasis-sleeping-sickness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis depends on</p>
<ol>
<li>Demonstration of <em>T. gambiense</em> or <em>T. rhodesiense</em> in blood, lymph node aspirates or in CSF. Both the trypanosomes are morphologically identical.</li>
<li>Demonstration of specific antibodies in serum.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Blood, whole with anticoagulant, preferably during febrile period, when trypanosomes are maximum in number.</li>
<li>Lymph node aspirates.</li>
<li>CSF</li>
<li>Serum.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Examination of blood for trypanosomes by
(i) Thick or thin blood film stained by Field’s or Giemsa’s or acridine orange method (Fig. 3.4).
(ii) Concentration of trypanosomes by buffy coat technique or by micro haematocrit method.</li>
<li>Smears of lymph node aspirate or CSF prepared directly or from the sediment, and stained by methods in (i) above.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-84560461.jpeg" alt="img-95.jpeg">
Fig. 7.3 Life cycle of <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em></p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Blood and Tissue Protozoa 1003</p>
<p>The CSF may also show</p>
<ul>
<li>increased protein level</li>
<li>elevated IgM</li>
<li>lymphocytosis</li>
<li>Morula cells: Morula cells are altered plasma cells whose cytoplasm is filled with proteinaceous droplets, appearing as vacuoles. Morula cells in CSF usually indicate trypanosomal infection of the central nervous system (Fig. 7.4).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-7025b08d.jpeg" alt="img-96.jpeg">
Fig. 7.4 Morula cell in CSF</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Demonstration of specific antibodies in serum:</li>
</ol>
<p>The tests used should detect IgM antibody against the trypanosomes because the population in endemic areas may have high IgG levels of the antibody. IgM antibodies in CSF are diagnostic of the disease.</p>
<p>The techniques used include
(i) Indirect immunofluorescence test (IFAT)
(ii) Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA)
(iii) Indirect haemagglutination test (IHA)</p>
<p>Laboratory Diagnosis of Chaga's Disease (T. cruzi Infection)</p>
<p>Diagnosis of T. cruzi infection depends on:</p>
<ol>
<li>Demonstration of trypomastigotes in blood.</li>
<li>Demonstration of amastigotes in tissues.</li>
<li>Detection of specific antibodies in serum.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Blood, whole with anticoagulant, collected during acute phase of the disease</li>
<li>Aspirates from chagomas or enlarged lymph nodes.</li>
<li>Serum.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Demonstration of trypomastigotes of T. cruzi in blood by</li>
</ol>
<p>(i) Examination of stained thick or thin blood film:
Blood smears stained by Field’s or Giemsa stain should be examined for ‘C’ or ‘U’ shaped trypomastigotes. They have a prominent, dark red staining kinetoplast at the posterior end and a central nucleus (Fig. 3.4). The trypomastigotes of T. cruzi are fragile and are easily damaged in blood films. Moreover, they are generally very few in number in the peripheral blood except in the febrile period.</p>
<p>(ii) Examination of wet film with a coverslip for motile trypanosomes.
(iii) Concentration of parasites by microhaematocrit or test tube method (Buffy-coat concentration).
(iv) Isolation of the parasites from blood (or tissues) by culture technique in NNN medium. Incubate the medium at 25°C for up to 30 days for epimastigote forms showing kinetoplast anterior to the nucleus (Fig. 7.1).
(v) Xenodiagnosis: Light infections can be detected by allowing bugs, which are free of trypanosomes, to feed on patient’s blood. The bug’s intestinal contents will show epimastigotes or trypomastigotes within 25–30 days.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1004 Parasitology</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Aspirates from chagoma, enlarged lymph nodes or muscle biopsy can be examined in the same way as blood for amastigote forms which are non-motile, round to oval (2–6 μm) with a large nucleus and rod shaped kinetoplast (Fig. 7.1).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Serological tests for the diagnosis of <em>T. cruzi</em> infection include
(i) Indirect fluorescent antibody test (IFAT)
(ii) Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA)
(iii) Indirect haemagglutination test (IHA)
(iv) Complement fixation test (CFT)</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The antibodies can be detected about one month after the infection, and persist even after treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="leishmania-species">LEISHMANIA SPECIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#leishmania-species" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The genus <em>Leishmania</em> includes flagellates that occur as intracellular amastigotes in vertebrate hosts and as flagellar promastigotes in invertebrate hosts and in cultures (Fig. 3.5). At least four major groups of the parasite, similar in morphology, but differing in clinical manifestations, geographical distribution and cultural characteristics cause disease in human beings. The insect vector is sandfly. The major groups are:</p>
<ol>
<li><em>Leishmania donovani</em> complex, causative agent of kala-azar (Black sickness) or visceral leishmaniasis.</li>
<li><em>Leishmania tropica</em> complex, causing old world cutaneous leishmaniasis or oriental sore.</li>
<li><em>Leishmania braziliensis</em> complex of cutaneous and muco-cutaneous disease in the Americas.</li>
<li><em>Leishmania mexicana</em> complex associated mainly with cutaneous lesions in the Americas.</li>
</ol>
<p>Within each of the above groups, there are strains and subspecies with their own distinctive characteristics.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The life cycle involves an alternate existence in a vertebrate host and an insect host (Fig. 7.5). Besides humans, the natural reservoir hosts include domestic dog and a variety of wild mammals such as rodents. The invertebrate insect hosts are sandflies of the genus <em>Phlebotomus</em> (<em>Lutzomyia</em> in the Americas).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-6d91b89d.jpeg" alt="img-97.jpeg">
Fig. 7.5 Life cycle of <em>Leishmania</em> species</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Blood and Tissue Protozoa 1005</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The amastigote form of the typical leishmanial parasite in the vertebrate host is a small, oval intracellular organism measuring 2-4 μm. It is found mainly within the macrophages. There is no flagellum, the nucleus is large and there is a prominent rod shaped kinetoplast (Fig. 3.5).</p>
<p>In the sandfly, or in culture, the promastigote with a long, delicate, anterior flagellum, an anterior kinetoplast and central nucleus is seen. It is about 14-20 μm long and 1.5-4.0 μm wide.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-diseases">Clinical Diseases<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-diseases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Kala-azar or visceral leishmaniasis caused by <em>L. donovani</em> Common symptoms include fever, anorexia, malaise, weight loss and diarrhoea. There is marked enlargement of liver, spleen and lymph nodes. The organisms parasitize the reticuloendothelial (RE) system and are seen intracellularly in phagocytes in these organs. Darkening of the skin of the face, hands, feet and abdominal skin occurs, giving the disease its name Black Sickness. Cell mediated immunity is suppressed. Death may occur in a few weeks in acute cases or after 2-3 years in chronic cases.</p>
<p>Other species of <em>Leishmania</em> cause mainly cutaneous lesions, some of them, e.g. <em>L. braziliensis</em> group cause mucocutaneous lesions. In the cutaneous form, called the oriental sore, a small papule develops at the site of the insect bite. The papule slowly forms a crater-like sore with raised margin which contains a large number of parasites. The sore enlarges up to 2cm or more in diameter. Secondary papules may appear at the periphery. This oriental sore usually heals spontaneously within one or two years.</p>
<p><em>L. braziliensis</em> causes a more severe form of mucocutaneous leishmaniasis. The lesions are initially similar to the oriental sore. The parasites migrate to nasal and oral mucosa, nasopharynx and palate with a continuous erosion of these tissues and thus cause disfiguration.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>All species of <em>Leishmania</em> are morphologically similar. Their differentiation depends on their geographical distribution, the nature and site of lesions and the symptoms.</p>
<p>Laboratory confirmation of leishmaniasis is by</p>
<ol>
<li>Finding amastigotes in</li>
</ol>
<p>(a) aspirates from the spleen, bone marrow, lymph node, or peripheral blood monocytes (Kala-azar or visceral leishmaniasis) or
(b) aspirate from infected ulcers or nodules (cutaneous leishmaniasis) or
(c) smears taken from infected mucocutaneous lesions (mucocutaneous leishmaniasis)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Culturing the specimens to obtain promastigote forms.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Detection of antibodies to <em>Leishmania</em> in serum.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Aspirates from the spleen, bone marrow, lymph nodes, elevated margin of ulcers, infected nodules.</li>
<li>Swabs from mucocutaneous lesions.</li>
<li>Whole blood, anticoagulated.</li>
<li>Serum.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Examination of aspirates and swabs for amastigotes:</li>
</ol>
<p>Prepare thin smears from the aspirate and stain by Field's or Giemsa method. Examine for intracellular amastigote forms (Fig. 3.5) in the large phagocytic cells. Some parasites may be extracellular. Amastigotes of <em>L. donovani</em> are called L.D. bodies.</p>
<p>&gt; Note
&gt; The spleenic aspirate has the highest chances of detecting the parasites in visceral leishmaniasis.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1006 Parasitology</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Examination of blood for amastigotes
The amastigotes can be detected in blood only in visceral leishmaniasis and their number is very low. Therefore, it is necessary to concentrate the parasites by buffy coat technique either in a microhaematocrit capillary or in a test tube. The smears from the buffy coat should be stained by Field’s or Giemsa technique. The amastigote forms can be seen in the mononuclear cells. However, this technique may not be very effective.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cultivation of amastigotes from aspirates and blood to obtain promastigotes
Aspirates from spleen, bone marrow, lymph nodes, and ulcers; and blood can be cultured in NNN-medium. The cultures should be incubated at 24°C in the dark for up to two weeks.
Examine daily for motile promastigotes by aseptically removing a loopful of the culture. If negative after three to five days incubation, subculture into a fresh medium and examine for 10 more days.
The culture technique is also useful to determine the effect of treatment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Serodiagnosis
Serodiagnosis is not useful in cutaneous leishmaniasis due to poor antibody response. However, it helps in the diagnosis of visceral and mucocutaneous types of leishmaniasis. In the visceral leishmaniasis, there is a large increase in gamma-globulins, both of IgG and IgM types. This is the basis for the aldehyde or formol gel test to screen the patients with active disease in endemic areas.
Serological techniques used include:</p>
</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Indirect fluorescent antibody test (IFAT)</li>
<li>Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA)</li>
<li>Counter immunoelectrophoresis (CIEP).</li>
</ul>
<p>Formol-gel or aldehyde test This is a non-specific screening test for visceral leishmaniasis. It detects a non-specific polyclonal globulin in patient’s serum in this disease.</p>
<p>Technique
To about 1ml of patient’s serum, add two drops of formalin (40% w/v) solution. Mix and allow to stand for about 20 minutes.</p>
<p>Results
If the test is positive, the serum becomes whitish and then coagulates like the white of a hard boiled egg within five minutes.
If weakly positive, the serum may show a milky white colour without any coagulation.
A negative test is indicated by no change in appearance in 20 minutes, or if the serum coagulates without whitening.</p>
<p>IV. CILIATES
There are no pathogenic ciliates known to invade the blood or tissues of humans.</p>
<p>V. COCCIDIA
◆ MALARIAL PARASITES (GENUS PLASMODIUM)
Malarial parasites are protozoan parasites belonging to the subclass Coccidia, genus Plasmodium. The genus contains many species which cause malaria in mammals and birds. The asexual cycle of these parasites called schizogony takes place in the red blood cells of vertebrates (intermediate hosts) and the sexual cycle, sporogony, occurs in mosquitoes (definitive host). There are four species of Plasmodium which can cause disease in man. They are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Plasmodium vivax which has the widest distribution, extending through the tropics, subtropics and temperate zones.</li>
<li>Plasmodium falciparum is also widely distributed, mainly in the tropics.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Blood and Tissue Protozoa 1007</p>
<ol start="3">
<li><em>Plasmodium malariae</em> occurs sporadically in different parts of the world.</li>
<li><em>Plasmodium ovale</em> is mainly confined to Central West Africa and some South Pacific islands.</li>
</ol>
<p>P. vivax and P. falciparum together account for about 95% of human infections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology-and-life-cycle">Morphology and Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology-and-life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Transmission of the disease to humans occurs when an infected female anopheline mosquito injects the sporozoites while taking its blood meal. The disease can also be transmitted through blood transfusions, contaminated hypodermic needles and as a congenital infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>In the peripheral blood of the human host, the malarial parasite has three different forms, namely the trophozoite, the schizont and the gametocytes (male and female).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="trophozoite">Trophozoite<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trophozoite" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A merozoite released by the rupture of a mature schizont enters a red blood cell and develops into a trophozoite. Young trophozoites are concave discs which appear as ring forms in stained preparations because the disc is very thin in the centre, and does not take up the stain (Fig. 3.3). The nucleus appears as one or two chromatin dots attached to the ring. This is an early trophozoite. The trophozoite feeds on haemoglobin in the red cell forming malarial pigment as an end product. This accumulates in the centre of the red cell as brown black granules.</p>
<p>The early trophozoite grows and becomes globular or irregular in shape, due to an active amoeboid movement within the red cell. This is a late or amoeboid trophozoite.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="schizont">Schizont<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#schizont" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>When the trophozoite is fully developed, its nucleus begins to divide, followed by the division of cytoplasm. This process, called schizogony, results in the formation of a schizont. Each division of nucleus surrounded by cytoplasm is called a merozoite. The number of merozoites in mature schizonts varies with the species of <em>Plasmodium</em>. When the schizont matures, it ruptures the red cell, releasing merozoites into the blood along with the malarial pigment and toxins. This causes typical malarial fever with chills. The merozoites invade new red cells and start developing as trophozoites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="gametocytes">Gametocytes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#gametocytes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>After several schizogenic cycles, some of the merozoites enter red cells and develop into gametocytes instead of schizonts. The gametocytes are either male (microgematocytes) or female (macrogametocytes). One red cell contains only one gametocyte.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="asexual-life-cycle-in-man">Asexual life cycle in man<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#asexual-life-cycle-in-man" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The asexual life cycle of malarial parasites in man has four phases:</p>
<ol>
<li><strong>Pre-erythrocytic or primary exo-erythrocytic schizogony</strong></li>
</ol>
<p>When the insect vector female anopheline mosquito takes a blood meal, the sporozoites from the salivary glands of the mosquito are discharged into the puncture wound. Within one hour the sporozoites are carried to the liver via the blood stream. The sporozoites are slender, motile organisms measuring 11 µm in length. In the liver, the sporozoites enter the liver parenchyma cells or hepatocytes, become rounded and start growing as trophozoites and eventually form schizonts. These schizonts are much larger than those in the red cells and contain 10,000 to 30,000 merozoites. When mature, the schizont ruptures releasing the merozoites which enter the blood stream. They must enter a red cell within a few minutes for their survival. In <em>P. vivax</em> and <em>P. ovale</em> infections some merozoites remain dormant or quiescent in the liver until a later time. These are called hypnozoites.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li><strong>Erythrocytic schizogony</strong></li>
</ol>
<p>This is the second phase of the cycle in the human host when the merozoites enter the red cells, grow into a trophozoite which eventually forms a mature schizont. The schizont ruptures to release the</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1008 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-69abccfa.jpeg" alt="img-98.jpeg">
Fig. 7.6 Life cycle of malarial parasites</p>
<p>merozoites. They parasitise new red cells and continue the erythrocytic cycle.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Gametogony After several erythrocytic generations some of the merozoites instead of producing schizonts begin to undergo development into male and female gametocytes. These gametocytes form a part of the sexual cycle taking place in the mosquito. They remain circulating in the peripheral blood for some time, awaiting to be taken up by an anopheline mosquito with its blood meal. In the mosquito they initiate the sexual cycle or sporogony.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Exoerythrocytic schizogony The resting merozoites in the liver cells, the hypnozoites, may sometimes revive and start developing into schizonts and then release merozoites in the blood stream to initiate a new erythrocytic schizogony. This new invasion of the red cells from the liver merozoites is called recurrence or true relapse. This occurs only in infections with <em>P. vivax</em> and <em>P. ovale</em>.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Recrudescence is a term used mainly in <em>P. falciparum</em> malaria. Sometimes the red cell infection is not completely eliminated either by the immune system or by antimalarial therapy. The symptoms subside temporarily, but the parasite becomes active again showing signs and symptoms of the disease. This is called recrudescence. All species may have recrudescence, but it is more common in <em>P. falciparum</em> malaria. A recrudescence is different from recurrence or true relapse due to delayed development of hypnozoites in the liver. The hypnozoite stage is absent in the life cycle of <em>P. falciparum</em>.</p>
<p><strong>Sexual life cycle in mosquito</strong> If gametocytes from human blood are ingested by the mosquito during its blood meal, they migrate to its gut where they mature into gametes. The male gametocyte</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Blood and Tissue Protozoa 1009</p>
<p>rapidly undergoes division to form a number of male gametes, each with a flagellum. This process is called exflagellation. These gametes become free, actively motile and penetrate the macrogamete to form a zygote by the fusion of male and female nuclei.</p>
<p>The zygote becomes elongated and motile, developing into an ookinete. The ookinete migrates to the midgut of the mosquito and forms an oocyst with a thin wall around it. Within a few days the oocyst matures and contains a large number of sporozoites. The oocysts ruptures releasing the sporozoites which disperse throughout the body, particularly to the salivary glands. When the mosquito takes its next blood meal, the sporozoites are injected into the human blood with saliva.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="variations-in-the-life-cycles-and-morphological-appearance-of-plasmodium-species">Variations in the Life Cycles and Morphological Appearance of Plasmodium species<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#variations-in-the-life-cycles-and-morphological-appearance-of-plasmodium-species" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Duration of the cycle Schizogony in red cells takes 48 hours in case of <em>P. vivax</em> and <em>P. ovale</em>, 72 hours in <em>P. malariae</em> and 38–48 hours in <em>P. falciparum</em>. The duration of the cycle decides periodicity of the characteristic chills and fever which coincide with the liberation of merozoites from red cells.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Age of the red cells affected <em>Plasmodium vivax</em> and <em>P. ovale</em> tend to infect young red cells while <em>P. malariae</em> infects old cells. <em>P. falciparum</em> can attack any red cell regardless of age, resulting in the highest parasitaemia (30–40% red cells can be parasitized).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Changes in the parasitized red cell In <em>P. vivax</em> infection, the red cell harbouring the parasite is enlarged and may contain Schuffner’s dots which stain pink with Giemsa stain. <em>Plasmodium ovale</em> also enlarges the red cell which shows fimbriated edges (Fig. 3.3). In <em>P. malariae</em> and <em>P. falciparum</em> infections, the red cell remains unchanged but <em>P. falciparum</em> may show few large dots called Maurer’s dots.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Early trophozoite Early trophozoite, the ring form of <em>P. vivax</em>, appears as a delicate ring with one chromatin dot, occupying one-third of the red cell. <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em> rings are smaller, more delicate and may contain more than one chromatin dot (Fig. 3.3), and more than one ring in a single red cell is seen very frequently. The ring forms of <em>P. ovale</em> are smaller than <em>P. vivax</em> while those of <em>P. malariae</em> are thicker with prominent chromatin.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Late trophozoite <em>Plasmodium vivax</em> shows irregularly shaped actively amoeboid trophozoite, <em>P. ovale</em> trophozoite is less amoeboid, while the trophozoite of <em>P. malariae</em> tends to form a band across the red cell. <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em> does not change into amoeboid form. The ring tend to be close to the periphery of red cell and are called appliqué or accole forms.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Schizont The schizonts of <em>P. vivax</em>, when mature, contain 12–24 merozoites, while those of <em>P. malariae</em> have 6–12 merozoites irregularly arranged with central brown pigment. <em>Plasmodium ovale</em> schizont has 8–12 merozoites arranged like a rosettes.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>In case of <em>P. falciparum</em>, as the parasite grows, the membrane of the host red cell becomes sticky and the cells tend to adhere to the endothelial lining of the capillaries of the internal organs. Thus only ring forms and gametocytes normally appear in the peripheral blood.</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>Gametocytes The gametocytes of <em>P. vivax</em> and <em>P. malariae</em> are round, those of <em>P. ovale</em> are oval while those of <em>P. falciparum</em> are sausage or banana shaped. As a general rule, microgametocytes (male) have dispersed chromatin while in macrogametocytes (female) the chromatin is more compact.</li>
</ol>
<p>Table 3.1 shows the differentiating features of malarial parasites in Giemsa stained thin blood films, and Fig. 3.3 shows their morphological features.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1010 Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The patient remains asymptomatic for a week or more from the time of original mosquito bite. The typical malarial symptoms (paroxysms or chills) appear when merozoites and the metabolic waste by-products are released into the blood stream at the end of the first schizogony in red cells.</p>
<p>Clinical symptoms include anaemia, splenomegaly, the paroxysm, fever and sweats. Anaemia may be due to various reasons such as direct lysis of red cells by parasites, accelerated removal of red cells by the spleen, decreased production of red cells due to bone marrow suppression or autoimmune lysis.</p>
<p>Malaria caused by <em>P. vivax</em> may be mild to severe and recurrence or true relapse may occur due to hypnozoites in the liver cells which may get activated at a later period. Although complications are rare, cerebral involvement and death may occur.</p>
<p><em>Plasmodium ovale</em> infections are similar to but milder than <em>P. vivax</em> infections; and chances of relapse less frequent. <em>Plasmodium malarae</em> also causes a mild form of malaria. The parasitaemia is generally low as it invades only old red cells. However, renal complications may occur due to chronic infections. Recrudescence is also possible.</p>
<p><em>Plasmodium falciparum</em> generally causes the most severe type of malaria. It can infect red cells of all ages resulting in 30–40% parasitaemia. The blood flow to an organ may be affected due to plugging of vessels by parasites, and this may lead from severe to fatal complications. The symptoms depend on the organ involved. Cerebral malaria is most often seen with <em>P. falciparum</em> infections. Involvement of the liver is indicated by abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting with bile or blood in it. Diarrhoea or dysentery may be seen with or without liver involvement. Black water fever is a <em>P. falciparum</em> complication with dark reddish black urine with a high methaemoglobin content. This may be due to the parasite itself, quinine sensitivity of the host or due to autoantibodies to self, red cell antigens. Acute renal failure may also occur with black water fever. Recrudescence is fairly common. Pulmonary oedema is a rare but often fatal complication.</p>
<p><em>Plasmodium falciparum</em> infection in pregnancy may result in abortion, premature labour and occasionally still birth. It has been observed that individuals with haemoglobin S gene (sickle cell anaemia), thalassaemia genes, ovalocytosis genes or glucose 6 phosphate dehydrogenase (G6PD) deficiency genes are naturally protected against malaria.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-diagnosis-of-malaria">Laboratory Diagnosis of Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis-of-malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis of malaria is made by:</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of parasites in blood</li>
<li>Serodiagnosis</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Blood, fresh and directly spread on the slides to make thick and thin blood films; or anticoagulated with EDTA.</li>
<li>Serum</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-detection-of-parasites-in-blood">1. Detection of parasites in blood<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-detection-of-parasites-in-blood" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) In thick and thin blood films Prepare thick and thin blood films and stain by Field’s or Giemsa method.</p>
<p>Examine the thick and thin film under the oil immersion lens, look for various forms of malarial parasites in blood and identify the species (Fig. 3.3, Table 3.1).</p>
<p>(ii) By concentration using buffy coat preparation Prepare thick and thin blood films from the buffy coat. Stain and examine microscopically. Estimate the parasitaemia.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> It is necessary to examine at least 200–300 oil immersion fields before reporting a negative result.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Blood and Tissue Protozoa 1011</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-serology">2. Serology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-serology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Serological tests have a limited use in the diagnosis of malaria because the antibody tests are unable to differentiate between the past and current infections. They are helpful in epidemiological surveys for the control of malaria.</p>
<p>The tests used include</p>
<p>(a) Indirect fluorescent antibody test (IFAT)
(b) ELISA
(c) Radioimmunoassay (RIA).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="genus-babesia">GENUS BABESIA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genus-babesia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The <em>Babesia</em> are sporozoan parasites of red blood cells that cause infections in humans. Cases of babesiosis have been documented world wide. The disease is transmitted by ticks. Although there are many species of <em>Babesia</em>, <em>B. microti</em> is the cause of most infections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="life-cycle-and-morphology">Life Cycle and Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle-and-morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Like malarial parasites, <em>Babesia</em> has two hosts, man as the intermediate host in which asexual cycle takes place and tick as a definitive host in which sexual multiplication occurs. The tick transmits the infection to man during its blood meal. The parasites infect the red cells and appear as intracellular, pleomorphic, ringlike structures, resembling early ring forms of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em>. It has a darkly staining nucleus and very little cytoplasm. The morphology of the ring varies with species, e.g. some may be pear shaped. Merozoites are formed in the red cell by asexual division which are released to invade new cells. <em>Babesia microti</em> is seen very frequently in fours and so, it is called Maltese cross (Fig. 7.7).</p>
<p>When taken up by a tick there is a complex sexual multiplication cycle, resulting in the presence of parasites in the salivary glands.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-60c5b551.jpeg" alt="img-99.jpeg">
Fig. 7.7 Babesia microti, maltese cross in red cells (↑)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The symptoms of babesiosis resemble those of malaria. There is general malaise, fever, chills, profuse sweating, fatigue and weakness. Hepatosplenomegaly may be seen. Parasitaemia is generally very low. The disease can also be transmitted through blood transfusion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis depends on the demonstration of parasites in blood.</p>
<p>Serological tests are not very useful yet and there are cross reactions with malarial parasites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="specimen">Specimen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Blood, preferably fresh and quickly spread on the slides to prepare thick and thin films, or anticoagulated with EDTA.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The parasites can be demonstrated by</p>
<ol>
<li>Staining of thick and thin blood smears by Fields or Giemsa’s method.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1012 Parasitology</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Animal inoculation</li>
</ol>
<p>In case of very low parasitaemia the blood is inoculated intraperitoneally into a hamster. Within a few days, the parasites can be demonstrated in the blood of the hamster showing the same morphology as in man.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="toxoplasma-gondii">TOXOPLASMA GONDII<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#toxoplasma-gondii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Toxoplasma gondii is a coccidian parasite closely related to other coccidians such as Plasmodium species. The parasite can infect many vertebrates including humans. The vertebrates are the intermediate hosts. The definitive hosts are the domestic cat and other members of the cat family. The parasite has a world-wide distribution.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Humans can acquire the infection in two ways:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>From the definitive host to intermediate host Human beings can ingest oocysts shed in cat faeces either directly or through food or water contaminated with infective cat's faeces. Oocyst contains two sporocysts each with four sporozoites.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>From one intermediate host to another Infection can also be acquired by ingestion of raw or uncooked meat of an intermediate host such as chickens, lambs, pigs, etc. which contain the trophozoites (tachyzoites) or pseudocysts of T.gondii.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Following ingestion of oocysts from cat's faeces or pseudocysts from another intermediate host, sporozoites are liberated. The sporozoites invade the intestinal epithelium and become intracellular trophozoites, called tachyzoites. In the acute phase they divide actively within the host cells. The multiplication can occur in many internal organs such as liver, muscle, central nervous system and lymph glands. In the chronic phase, the parasite multiplies slowly and is then called a bradyzoite. Bradyzoites can fill up a host cell, develop a parasitic membrane and become a cyst which is called a pseudocyst. Pseudocysts or the resting forms are found primarily in the muscle and brain. When uncooked tissue containing tachyzoites or pseudocysts is ingested by another intermediate host the parasite begins the asexual cycle in the new host.</p>
<p>When ingested by a member of the cat family, the parasite undergoes a sexual cycle resulting in the formation of oocyst which is shed in the faeces.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-fd48f453.jpeg" alt="img-100.jpeg">
Fig. 7.8 Life cycle of Toxoplasma gondii</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
