<p>The oocysts shed in cat's faeces are <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>13</mn><mtext> </mtext><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10 \times 13\,\mu\mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">13</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span> in size. The trophozoites (tachyzoites, bradyzoites) are <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>3</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>6</mn><mtext> </mtext><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">3 \times 6\,\mu\mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>, pyriform in shape, with one end more rounded than the other. The nucleus is situated towards the broad end of the parasite (Fig. 4.5). The parasites may be present in a cell or in a pseudocyst. A pseudocyst may contain 50 to several thousand bradyzoites and measure from 10 to <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>100</mn><mtext> </mtext><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">100\,\mu\mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">100</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span> in diameter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Majority of Toxoplasma infections are asymptomatic. The most severe symptoms are seen with congenital infections or those in immunocompromised</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Blood and Tissue Protozoa 1013</p>
<p>mised patients, e.g. after transplant surgeries, AIDS etc.</p>
<p>Congenital toxoplasmosis This occurs in 0.1 to 0.5% of pregnancies. Unlike that in adults, it is often severe and even fatal to the foetus if the mother acquires infection in the first or second trimester of pregnancy. It may lead to stillbirth or abortion; or the symptoms may develop in the infant after two to three months of birth. These show involvement of the central nervous system with evidence of mental defects.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Toxoplasmosis is diagnosed by</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of antibodies, especially IgM antibodies, in patient's serum</li>
<li>Demonstration of parasites in tissues, aspirates or body fluids.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Serum</li>
<li>Sputum, aspirates from lymph nodes, bone marrow, cerebrospinal fluid, pleural fluid, peritoneal fluid etc.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Serological tests: These include:</li>
</ol>
<p>(i) Indirect fluorescent antibody test (IFAT)
(ii) Enzyme-linked immunoabsorbant assay (ELISA)
(iii) Indirect haemagglutination test (IHA)
(iv) Complement fixation test (CFT)</p>
<p>The tests used are specific for Toxoplasma antigens. Detection of IgM antibodies is indicative of current or recent infection, which is significant in case of pregnant women and immunocompromised patients.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Detection of parasites in aspirates or tissues: A smear should be prepared from a sediment and stained by Field's or Giemsa technique. The intracellular trophozoites or pseudocysts can be detected microscopically (Fig. 4.5).</li>
</ol>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> Detection of parasites in tissues may not necessarily be the causative agent responsible for the symptoms. Most infections are asymptomatic.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pneumocystis-carinii">PNEUMOCYSTIS CARINII<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pneumocystis-carinii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pneumocystis carinii, an extracellular parasite which causes interstitial plasma cell pneumonia is presently classed as a coccidian. However, its taxonomic status is still uncertain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The life cycle of the parasite is not fully understood. They are abundant in the pulmonary exudate of the infected person, therefore, the transmission is likely to be by droplet infection. Pneumocystis carinii are seen in humans in two forms, the trophozoite and the cyst, both being present extracellularly in the lung tissue. The trophozoites continue to multiply by binary fission, eventually forming precysts, and the mature cysts contain eight trophozites (Fig. 7.9).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-22195ad4.jpeg" alt="img-101.jpeg">
Fig. 7.9 Pneumocystis carinii</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The trophozoites measures about 5μm in diameter and is amoeboid in shape. The nucleus is visible either with Giemsa, haematoxylin or silver</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1014 Parasitology</p>
<p>methanamine stain (Refer to Chapter 4). The cysts also measure 5 µm and may contain one to eight trophozoites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Pneumocystis carinii</em> causes an interstitial plasma cell pneumonia in premature or immunocompromised infants within the first three months of life. <em>P. carinii</em> is recognised as a cause of pneumonia in immunocompromised adults, especially those with acquired immuno deficiency syndrome (AIDS). Dyspnoea, fever, non-productive cough, cyanosis and hepatosplenomegaly are the most common clinical signs and symptoms. The disease may be fulminant and rapidly fatal.</p>
<p>Histologically there is a heavy interstitial infiltrate with plasma cells. The alveoli are filled with fat laden cells, parasites and foamy vacuolated material giving a honeycomb appearance.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis depends on the demonstration of the parasite in the material from the lung.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Lung biopsy - This procedure should not be carried out unless there is no other alternative as it carries the risk of causing haemorrhage and pneumothorax.</li>
<li>Transbronchial biopsy</li>
<li>Bronchoalveolar lavage.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Various staining procedures can be used to demonstrate the parasites in the specimen, e.g. modified acid-fast, acridine orange, or toluidine blue, but Gomori’s methenamine silver stain gives the best results. The cyst wall is clearly stained blue-black and may show trophozoites inside it. The parasites have a tendency to group together.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; Satisfactory serological tests for Pneumocystis carinii are not yet commercially available, due to the non availability of purified antigen.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="microsporodia">MICROSPORODIA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#microsporodia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Microsporidia</em> are obligate intracellular parasites causing infections of the gastrointestinal tract in animals and humans. Compromised patients are susceptible hosts. The symptoms include diarrhoea, vomiting, respiratory distress and leukopenia. It can infect almost any organ in the body via the gastrointestinal tract.</p>
<p>The life cycle is not well documented. The diagnosis depends on the detection of spores in biopsy or autopsy specimens. They produce resistant spores which are less than 10 µm in diameter. It contains a granule at the anterior end which is periodic-acid Schiff (PAS) positive. The spores are seen within epithelial cells.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 8</p>
<p>Common Intestinal Helminths of Humans</p>
<p>For laboratory techniques in this chapter, refer to Chapter 2.</p>
<p>General characteristics of helminths and their broad classification is described in Chapter 1. The common parasites belonging to the three groups, cestodes, trematodes and nematodes, and which are transmitted through the alimentary tract, are briefly discussed here.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intestinal-cestodes-tapeworms">INTESTINAL CESTODES (TAPEWORMS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intestinal-cestodes-tapeworms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The commonly occurring human cestodes are shown in Table 2.2.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diphylobothriium-latum">DIPHYLOBOTHRIIUM LATUM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diphylobothriium-latum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Distribution:</strong> Worldwide</p>
<p><strong>Life cycle</strong> Figure 8.1 shows the life cycle of the parasite. There are two intermediate hosts; cyclops which ingest the larvae from the egg, and fish which ingest the cyclops. The infection is transmitted to man by eating raw or undercooked freshwater fish. The definitive hosts are man and many other mammals.</p>
<p><strong>Morphology</strong> <em>Adult worm</em> It is one of the longest tapeworms of humans, three to ten metres long, with about 3000 proglottids. It lives in the small intestine. The head (scolex) is elongated or</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-162a5028.jpeg" alt="img-102.jpeg">
Fig 8.1 Life cycle of Diphyllobothrium latum</p>
<p>almond shaped with two deep grooves with which it attaches to the intestinal mucosa. The mature segments are broader than long (3 × 11 mm) and have rosette-shaped uterus.</p>
<p><strong>Egg</strong> It is pale yellowish brown in colour, oval, measuring 70 × 45 μm. It has an operculum which is not very easily seen. The egg contains a mass of granulated yolk cells surrounding an undeveloped ovum (Fig. 4.6a).</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> The worm can live in the human host for many years. It may cause</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1016 Parasitology</p>
<p>gastrointestinal symptoms with weight loss. If present at the jejunum, it may consume vitamin B₁₂ leading to megaloblastic anaemia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is made by the detection of eggs or segments in faeces.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Microscopic examination of stool by
(a) Wet preparation using saline or iodine
(b) Examination of faeces after concentration</li>
</ol>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; Concentration is generally not required in D.latum infection because the worm produces a large number of eggs which can be easily detected if present.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Microscopic Examination of a gravid segment to identify the parasite.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="taenia-saginta-beef-tapeworm">TAENIA SAGINTA (Beef Tapeworm)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#taenia-saginta-beef-tapeworm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="distribution-worldwide">Distribution: Worldwide<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#distribution-worldwide" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Human infection with the adult worm is initiated by ingestion of raw or improperly cooked beef containing encysted larvae of T. saginata. The larva evaginates in the small intestine and attaches with the suckers to the mucosa. It matures in five to twelve weeks to reach a length of up to 25 metres. The gravid segments pass out in the faeces which when consumed by cattle will continue the life cycle in the cattle (Fig. 8.2).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology-adult-worm">Morphology Adult worm:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology-adult-worm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The pyriform scolex, 1–2 mm in diameter, has four prominent hemispherical suckers but no hooks (Fig. 2.16). The mature worm can be as long as 25 metres.</p>
<p>A gravid segment measures 20 mm long and 6 mm wide. The uterus has a central stem with more than 13 lateral branches.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="egg">Egg<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#egg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>It is round to oval in shape, measuring 33 × 43 μm in diameter. The embryo is surrounded by a thick, brown, radially striated wall. The embryo contains three pairs of hooklets (hexacanth) which can be seen by focusing the microscope up and down (Figs 2.2, 2.16).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="clinical-disease">Clinical disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Generally very few symptoms are associated with the presence of T. saginata adult worm. The symptoms may include weight loss, loss of appetite, diarrhoea, intestinal obstruction, and occasionally appendicitis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis of T.saginata infection is by:</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of eggs in faeces.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-f1fa0ca8.jpeg" alt="img-103.jpeg"></p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Intestinal Helminths of Humans 1017</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="egg-of-t-saginata-is-morphologically-indistinguishable-from-that-of-t-solium">Egg of T.saginata is morphologically indistinguishable from that of T.solium.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#egg-of-t-saginata-is-morphologically-indistinguishable-from-that-of-t-solium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol start="2">
<li>Detection and identification of a gravid segment and scolex of <em>T. saginata</em> in faeces.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Microscopic examination of stool by
(a) Saline and iodine wet preparation
(b) Concentration method</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Microscopic examination of a gravid segment: The segment should be examined microscopically for uterus with more than 13 main lateral branches. The segments are narrow (6 mm) and long (20 mm)</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>: The segments are passed singly in faeces.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Identification of scolex if it is detected in faeces. The scolex of <em>T.saginata</em> has no hooklets.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="taenia-solium-pork-tapeworm">TAENIA SOLIUM (Pork Tapeworm)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#taenia-solium-pork-tapeworm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="distribution">Distribution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#distribution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>It is worldwide, but is less common than <em>T.saginata</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The life cycle of <em>T.solium</em> is similar to that of <em>T.saginata</em>, except that the infection is caused by eating insufficiently cooked pork, instead of beef (Fig. 8.2). After ingestion, the larva emerges from the meat, the head evaginates in the small intestine and attaches to the mucosa with its hooks and suckers. In about three months, it grows into an adult worm, two—seven metres in length. The life span is about 25 years. When mature, the eggs pass out in chains of segments, and are ingested by pigs. In the pig the embryos enter the muscles where they are encysted till eaten by man. Cysticercus of <em>T. solium</em> in man: Sometimes after hatching, the released embryos penetrate the intestinal wall and are carried by the blood stream throughout the body. They filter out in muscles and other tissues such as brain and eye, and develop into cysticerci. Each cysticercus is an ovoid, milky white bladder with the head invaginated into the bladder. It measures 5 × 10 μm. It may become calcified later. Cysticercus can also form due to regurgitation of gravid segments back into the intestines.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Adult worm</strong>: The mature adult worm is two-seven metres long and may contain 800–1000 segments. The globular scolex is about 1 mm in diameter. It has four cup shaped suckers, a low cushioned rostellum (mouth) surrounded by a double row of hooklets (Fig. 2.16). The mature segment is greyish blue, about 12 mm long and six mm wide (shorter than that of <em>T. saginata</em> which is 20 × 6 mm). The uterus has a central stem with about 13 or less lateral branches on each side.</p>
<p><strong>Egg</strong>: The egg of <em>T.solium</em> is morphologically identical to that of <em>T.saginata</em> (Figs 2.2, 2.16).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="clinical-disease">Clinical disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><em>T.solium</em> infection can lead to two types of diseases. The first is due to the adult worm in the intestine while the other is due to the larval form, cysticercus.</p>
<p>Adult worm usually causes no problems other than slight irritation at the site of attachment and vague abdominal symptoms.</p>
<p><strong>Cysticercosis</strong>: The cysticercosis caused by the larval stage can lead to symptoms depending on its site or the tissue involved. The sites commonly affected are muscle, brain or eye.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis of <em>T.solium</em> infection is by:</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of eggs in faeces</li>
<li>Detection of gravid segments in faeces</li>
<li>Microscopic examination of cysticercus after its removal by surgery.</li>
<li>Serodiagnosis of cysticercosis</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Detection of eggs in faeces by:
(a) Direct microscopic examination by saline wet mount</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1018 Parasitology</p>
<p>(b) Microscopic examination after concentration</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Detection and examination of gravid segment. It is shorter than that of <em>T.saginata</em> and the uterus has less than 13 lateral branches (Fig. 2.16).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examination of cysticercus: The site is detected by x-ray or CT scan. After extraction the cysticercus should be examined for the typical scolex of <em>T. solium</em> (Fig. 2.16).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Serodiagnosis: It may be helpful in some cases but generally there may be cross reactivity with other antigens, especially that of <em>Echinococcus granulosus</em>.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; The gravid segments and cysticercus should be handled with care as the eggs are directly infective.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hymenolepis-nana">HYMENOLEPIS NANA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hymenolepis-nana" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Distribution</strong> <em>Hymenolepis nana</em> is the smallest tapeworm of humans and is called the dwarf tapeworm. It has a worldwide distribution.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-f6aab144.jpeg" alt="img-104.jpeg"></p>
<p><strong>Life cycle</strong> The infection with <em>H. nana</em> can occur in two ways, by ingestion of eggs in food, drink or through contaminated hands; and by internal auto infection. Intermediate host is not required though some insects can act as intermediate hosts. Generally, man acts both as intermediate and definitive host.</p>
<p>After ingestion, the eggs hatch in the stomach or small intestine and liberate larvae. The larvae enter the villi of small intestine and develop into infective cysticercoid stage. When mature, the cysticercoides rupture the villi, enter the intestinal lumen and attach to other villi to grow into adult worms. The eggs produced in the gravid segments are released by the disintegration of segments. Some eggs pass out in the faeces to the external environment to be reingested, while others remain in the same host and hatch again to release larvae which invade the villi to start the autoinfection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Adult worm:</strong> It is a very small tapeworm unlike the <em>Taenia</em> species. It measures about 4 cm. The scolex has four suckers and a short rostellum with hooks.</p>
<p>The strobila (body) has about 100–200 segments which are about four times broader than long (Fig. 2.16). The uterus is not branched.</p>
<p><strong>Egg:</strong> The egg is round or oval, colourless. 30–45 µm in diameter. It has two membranes enclosing a hexacanth embryo with three pairs of hooklets. The inner membrane shows two polar thickenings, from each of which arise 4 to 8 slender polar filaments (Figs 2.2, 2.16).</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> The infection is more common among children and they may be asymptomatic even with a heavy worm burden. Some patients may complain of abdominal pain, diarrhoea, headache and anorexia.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Intestinal Helminths of Humans 1019</p>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis H. nana infection is confirmed by detection of eggs in the stool.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> The eggs are infective when they are passed.</p>
<p>Techniques Detection of H. nana eggs in faeces by
(a) Saline wet mounts
(b) Concentration method.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> H. nana eggs should be differentiated from H. diminuta and Dipylidium caninum eggs which are similar to H. nana. Hymenolepis diminuta and Dipylidium caninum are cestodes of animals but may cause infection in man. Eggs of H. diminuta are similar to those of H. nana, but are larger and do not contain polar filaments in the egg shell. Eggs of D. caninum are discharged in faeces in groups or packets with hexacanth embryos.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8941f3e4">♦ INTESTINAL AND LIVER TREMATODES (FLUKES)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8941f3e4" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Intestinal trematodes can be broadly divided into two groups—those which reside in the intestine, e.g. Fasciolopsis buski and those that inhabit the bile ducts in the liver, e.g. Fasciola hepatica. Trematodes are parasites of vertebrates, hermaphroditic (both sexes in the same parasite) and usually require two intermediate hosts of specific species or aquatic plants. For example, fresh water snail is required by all trematodes as an intermediate host. The adult worms vary in size from barely visible (Metagonimus yokogawai) to the very large (Fasciolopsis buski). The life cycle of all the intestinal trematodes is similar. Each passes through various stages of larval forms in the intermediate hosts through the medium of fresh water. The common trematodes are described in Table 2.3. Figure 8.5 outlines their life cycles.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Adult worms:</strong> They differ in shape and morphology and can be readily differentiated from one another. Figure 8.4 shows a diagram of the general structure of intestinal and liver trematodes.</p>
<p><strong>Eggs:</strong> Some species of trematodes produce eggs which look alike and cannot be differentiated morphologically.</p>
<p>Eggs of Fasciolopsis buski, Fasciola hepatica and Echinostoma ilocanum are large and measure about 140 × 85 μm. They are oval in shape, pale brown in colour and have a small operculum which is difficult to see. Each egg contains an unsegmented ovum surrounded by many yolk cells (Fig. 8.6(a)).</p>
<p>The egg of Opisthorchis (Clonorchis) sinensis is small (30 × 16 μm) yellow brown and shaped like an electric light bulb. It has an operculum which rests on a rim giving an appearance of a shoulder. At the opposite end, the egg shell shows a small projection. The shell also has an indistinct outer covering (Fig. 8.6(b)).</p>
<p>The egg of Heterophyes heterophyes is more oval than that of O. sinensis, and the operculum is not clearly visible. The shoulder is not prominent and the outer indistinct coat is absent.</p>
<p>The egg of Metagonimus yokogawai is similar to that of H. heterophyes except that it is more elongated and the shoulder is absent.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infection by intestinal and liver trematodes can be diagnosed by detecting eggs in faeces or in duodenal aspirate. Adult worms may also be detected in the faeces in very heavy infections or when the patient is undergoing treatment.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1020 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-d558cdc0.jpeg" alt="img-105.jpeg">

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Detection of eggs in faeces by
(a) Saline wet mount
(b) Formal-ether concentration method.</li>
</ol>
<p>&gt; Note
&gt; Zinc sulphate flotation technique is not recommended for concentration of trematode eggs because the egg shell is very fragile and may break at the high concentration of the salt. Some eggs may also sink at the bottom due to their high density.</p>

<li>Detection of eggs in duodenal aspirate by
(a) Saline wet mount
(b) Examination of the sediment.</li>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intestinal-nematodes">INTESTINAL NEMATODES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intestinal-nematodes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nematodes are cylindrical, unsegmented helminths with bilateral symmetry. They have a fully developed digestive tract and vary in size from a few millimetres to one metre. Nematodes which infect</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Intestinal Helminths of Humans 1021</p>
<p>humans have separate sexes and the male is usually smaller than the female. The infection is transmitted either by ingesting infective eggs or by infective larvae penetrating the skin. The rhabditiform larva is the non-infective first stage larva that hatches from the egg either in the intestine (Stronglyloides stercoralis) or in the soil like hookworms. The filariform larva is the infective stage of the worm which causes infection by penetrating the skin. Table 2.1 shows the most common intestinal nematodes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ascaris-lumbricoides-round-worm">ASCARIS LUMBRICOIDES (Round Worm)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ascaris-lumbricoides-round-worm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Distribution</strong> Ascaris lumbricoides has a worldwide distribution. It is prevalent in moist, warm climates and in areas with poor sanitation. It is one of the commonest human parasites.</p>
<p><strong>Life cycle</strong> Infection in humans is acquired through ingestion of embryonated egg from faecally polluted soil. The eggs hatch in the stomach or the duodenum. The larvae penetrate the intestinal wall, enter the blood stream, and are carried to the heart via the portal circulation. From the heart, they are carried to the lung where they are filtered out by the capillaries. The larvae undergo development for about 10 days in the lung, start migrating through the trachea and the pharynx, from where they are swallowed. The larvae mature into male and female worms in the intestine (Fig. 8.7). The eggs produced by the female are passed out in the faeces. The entire cycle takes 8–12 weeks. The female produces a large number of eggs (about 200,000 every day).</p>
<p><strong>Unfertilised eggs</strong> Unfertilised eggs of A. lumbricoides are found in about two-fifths of infections because repeated copulation is necessary for the continuous production of fertilised eggs. The unfertilised eggs are also seen in absence of male worms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Adult worms</strong> A. lumbricoides worms are pink or yellow-white in colour. The female is larger (20–35 × 0.3–0.6 cm) than the male (15–30 × 0.2–0.4 cm). The tail of the male is curved and has two rod-like projections (spicules). A small mouth is surrounded by three lips (Fig. 2.16).</p>
<p><strong>Fertilised egg</strong> (Fig. 2.2): It is round, oval, measures 60 × 40 μm, yellow-brown in colour and is covered by an outer coarsely mammillated thick albuminous covering. The egg contains a mass of unsegmented embryo, densely impregnated with lecithin granules. Sometimes the albuminous coat of the egg may be absent and it may appear pale yellow with a smooth shell. It is described as ‘decorticated’.</p>
<p><strong>Unfertilised egg</strong> It is usually more oval (90 × 45 μm) and darker in colour. It has a thinner shell with an irregular coating of albumin and is completely filled with an amorphous mass of protoplasm with large refractile granules.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> The symptoms of the disease may be either due to migrating larvae or adult worms. The worms in the intestine during and after maturity can cause abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting and diarrhoea. A heavy infection can also lead to malnutrition due to consumption of proteins and vitamins by the worms. A heavy infection can cause intestinal obstruction or perforation which can be fatal. Instead of passing through the anus, the adult worms can sometimes migrate upwards and are excreted through the mouth or nose. During the migration of the larvae through the heart and the lung, hypersensitivity and inflammatory responses can lead to attacks of coughing, bronchial asthma or pneumonia-like symptoms. Eosinophilia and urticaria (skin rash) is common.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1022 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-5d61e0f8.jpeg" alt="img-108.jpeg">
Fig. 8.7 Life cycle of Ascaris lumbricoides</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ascariasis is diagnosed by</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of eggs in faeces</li>
<li>Identification of the adult worm passed out from the anus or the mouth/nose.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Direct microscopic examination of stool by
(a) Saline wet preparation
(b) Concentration by a sedimentation method (unfertilised eggs do not float on NaCl or zinc sulphate).</li>
</ol>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; Concentration method is generally not necessary due to the large number of eggs present as the female releases about 200,000 eggs per day.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>The adult worm can be identified by its morphology as described above.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="enterobius-vermicularis-pinworm-or-thread-worm">ENTEROBIUS VERMICULARIS (Pinworm or Thread Worm)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#enterobius-vermicularis-pinworm-or-thread-worm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="distribution">Distribution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#distribution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Enterobius vermicularis, commonly known as pinworm or threadworm, is one of the most common parasitic agents of human infections in the world. The infection is more common among children in areas where the tendency is to bathe less often.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Humans are infected by ingestion of infective eggs. They hatch in the large intestine and develop into adult worms in about 1 month. The male worms fertilise the females, die and are usually passed out in the faeces. In gravid females, almost the entire body is filled with eggs. The female further migrates to the anus, and deposits the eggs on the perianal skin. The eggs are passed out in faeces. They remain viable on clothing or other inanimate objects. They become infective within a few hours. Sometimes retroinfection can occur when the eggs hatch on the perianal skin. The larvae migrate back into the intestine and grow into adult worms. There is no intermediate host required to complete the life cycle (Fig. 8.8).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="adult-worms">Adult worms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adult-worms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>The small yellowish white female worm (8–13 mm × 0.4 mm) has a winglike cuticular expansion called alae near the mouth, a prominent oesophageal bulb and a long pointed tail (Fig. 2.16). The male (2–5 mm × 0.4 mm) with a curved tail and a single spicule, is rarely seen.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="egg">Egg<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#egg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>It is colourless and measures 55 × 40 μm. The shape is oval and planoconvex (usually flattened on one side, (Fig. 2.2). Each egg contains a coiled larva.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="clinical-disease">Clinical disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>The most striking symptoms of E. vermicularis infection is intense irritation around the anus due to the migration of the female worm to the anus to deposit eggs. In heavily infected females, infection of the urinary or genital tract may occur due to the migration of the worm.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>E. vermicularis infection is diagnosed by detection of eggs or female worms either in the stool or more commonly on the perianal skin.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Intestinal Helminths of Humans 1023</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-d74476b7.jpeg" alt="img-109.jpeg">
Fig. 8.8 Life cycle of Enterobius vermicularis</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Direct examination of faeces by
(a) Saline wet mount
(b) concentration method</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Note</strong> The eggs are infective within 6 hours of deposition on the skin and should be handled with care.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Detection of eggs in scotch tape preparation</li>
<li>Identification of female adult worms: The female <em>E. vermicularis</em> appears like a piece of a small thread. Microscopically, a long pointed tail and wing-like alae near the mouth are diagnostic.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="trichuris-trichuira-whip-worm">TRICHURIS TRICHUIRA (Whip Worm)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trichuris-trichuira-whip-worm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="distribution">Distribution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#distribution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><em>T. trichiura</em> or whipworm infection is common in warm, moist climates. It is often associated with ascariasis.</p>
<p><strong>Life cycle</strong> Human trichiuriasis is acquired by ingestion of fully embryonated eggs from the soil. The eggs hatch in the small intestine, mature in three months and eventually attach to the mucosa in the large intestine. The eggs are passed out in faeces. They become infective in about two weeks in moist soil. Ingestion of these eggs continues the cycle. (Fig. 8.9)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Adult worms</strong> The female worm is 35–50 mm long while the male worm is 30–45 mm. The tail end of the male is coiled and has a single spicule. The common name whipworm comes from the characteristic shape, being narrow at the anterior end and wide, like a whip handle, at the posterior end (Fig. 2.16).</p>
<p><strong>Egg</strong> The eggs of <em>T. trichiura</em> are brown in colour, 50 × 25 μm, typically barrel-shaped with clear protruding mucoid polar plugs at each end (Fig. 2.2). They contain a central irregular mass of unsegmented embryo.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Light infections by <em>T. trichiura</em> are symptomless. Heavy infection can cause abdominal pain, diarrhoea with blood in the faeces, anaemia and weight loss. The worms embed themselves in the mucosa of the large intestine with their anterior ends, their tails being free in the lumen. A heavy burden can therefore, lead to bleeding due to damaged mucosa. Whipworm dysentery is more chronic than <em>E. histolytica</em> dysentery; and</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1024 Parasitology</p>
<p>is associated with malnutrition, and sometimes rectal prolapse.</p>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis Trichiuriasis is diagnosed by detection of eggs in faeces.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
The adult worms are anchored in the rectal mucosa and rarely passed out in faeces. They can be seen in the rectum by proctoscopy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Direct microscopic examination for detection of characteristic eggs by</p>
<p>(a) Saline wet mount
(b) Concentration technique: Generally concentration is not required as a large number of eggs are present.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
It is necessary to quantitate or grade the number of ova as rare, few, moderate or many, since light infections may not require treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hookworms-ancylostoma-duodenale-and-necator-americanus">Hookworms (ANCYLOSTOMA DUODENALE and NECATOR AMERICANUS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hookworms-ancylostoma-duodenale-and-necator-americanus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ancylostoma duodenale or the old world hookworm and Necator americanus or the new world hookworm are prevalent in moist, warm climates. The eggs of both hookworms are identical. The differentiation between the two worms is established by examining the adult worms and larvae.</p>
<p>Life cycle Hookworm infection is acquired through penetration of the skin by filariform larva from the soil, usually between the toes or the dorsum of the foot. The larvae enter the lymphatics or venules and are carried in the blood through the heart to the lungs. Due to their size, they are unable to pass through the capillaries and therefore they break out into the alveoli. Like Ascaris larvae they ascend the bronchi and trachea. They migrate through the pharynx and finally reach the intestine. During this period from skin penetration, the larvae undergo moulting 4 times and develop into adult worms. They attach to the intestinal mucosa by their teeth or cutting plates. The eggs are produced about 5-6 weeks after infection, and are passed out in the faeces (Fig. 8.10).</p>
<p>In the soil, the eggs hatch to liberate rhabditiform larvae. The rhabditiform larvae grow into longer, more slender, infective filariform larvae (Fig. 2.4), which infect humans by skin penetration.</p>
<p>Occasionally, infection may occur by ingesting infective larvae through contaminated food. In this case, the larvae mature into adult worms without passing through the lung.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Adult worms Hookworms are small, cylindrical nematodes, greyish-white in colour. The females measure 8-13 mm in length while the males measure 6-10 mm. The male has a broad, translucent membranous bursa, with riblike rays at the posterior end, which is used for the attachment to the female during copulation.</p>
<p>The chief morphological differences between the two hookworms are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Size and shape: N. americanus adult worm is slightly smaller than that of A. duodenale and is characteristically bent anteriorly in the direction opposite to the body curve (Fig. 2.16).</li>
<li>Buccal cavity: Ancylostoma duodenale has two pairs of teeth on the ventral surface and a smaller, single pair of teeth on the dorsal surface. Necator americanus has no teeth but cutting plates, one pair each on its dorsal and ventral surfaces (Fig. 8.11).</li>
<li>Copulatory bursa: The posterior end of the male hookworm has a broad, translucent membranous bursa with rib-like rays. The dorsal ray of N. americanus</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Intestinal Helminths of Humans 1025</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-4574ba82.jpeg" alt="img-111.jpeg">
Fig. 8.10 Life cycle of Hookworms (A. duodenale, N. americanus)</p>
<p>divides into two from the root while that of A. duodenale is undivided. (Fig. 8.11)</p>
<p>Egg The eggs of both hookworms look similar. They are colourless and oval with bluntly rounded ends measuring <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>60</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>40</mn><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">60 \times 40 \mu \mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">60</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">40</span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>. The egg shell is thin, transparent and contains an embryo. The embryo may be unsegmented or divided into two to eight blastomeres. There is a clear space between the developing embryo and the thin egg shell (Fig. 2.2).</p>
<p>Larvae The eggs hatch in soil to release larvae. The larvae of hookworms can be differentiated from each other and from those of Strongyloides by their morphology (Fig. 2.4).</p>
<p>Clinical disease The initial symptom of hookworm infection is the skin reaction at the site of penetration, known as ground itch. It may be more severe in subsequent infections. Mild respiratory symptoms and eosinophilia may be evident during migration of larvae through heart and lung.</p>
<p>Major symptoms of the infection are due to the adult worms in the intestine. In case of acute infec</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-cffcc5e9.jpeg" alt="img-112.jpeg">
Fig. 8.11 Differentiating features of the two hookworms</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1026 Parasitology</p>
<p>tion there may be fatigue, nausea, vomiting, abdominal pain, diarrhoea with black or red stools due to blood loss in the intestine. It has been estimated that one hookworm can consume about 0.15–0.3 ml blood everyday. Chronic infection may lead to iron deficiency anaemia.</p>
<p><strong>Laboratory diagnosis</strong> Hookworm infection is diagnosed by detecting eggs in faeces, preferably fresh. The infecting species may be identified by examination of larval stages or adult worms.</p>
<p><strong>Techniques</strong> Examination of faeces by</p>
<p>(a) Saline wet mount
(b) Concentration method.</p>
<p>It is advisable to indicate the degree of severity of infection by mentioning the egg load (scanty, few, moderate, many) in the specimen.</p>
<p>The differentiation between the two species on the basis of larval morphology is discussed in Chapter 2.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; If the stool specimen is not fresh, the eggs may hatch to produce larvae. If larvae are seen in the specimen, they should be identified and differentiated from those of Strongyloides (Fig. 2.4).</p>
<p>Also, Trichostrongylus eggs may be mistaken for hookworm eggs. Trichostrongylus eggs are longer and thinner (90 × 40 μm) than hook-worm eggs and are more pointed at one or both ends.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="strongyloides-stercoralis">STRONGYLOIDES STERCORALIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#strongyloides-stercoralis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Stongyloides stercoralis has worldwide distribution. It is most commonly found in warm regions but can survive in other climates as well.</p>
<p><strong>Life cycle</strong> Strogyloides stercoralis has a complex life cycle. The worm is capable of a free-living existence by continuing its non-infective life cycle in a warm, moist soil. It can also exist in a parasitic form in humans and other animals and can cause autoinfection in the same host.</p>
<p>Normally the infection is acquired by skin penetration by infective filariform larvae from the soil. Following penetration, they are carried by the blood stream to the lungs, passing through the heart. Being larger than the capillaries, they break out into the alveoli and migrate through the bronchi and trachea in the same way as Ascaris and hookworm larvae, to be swallowed to reach the intestine. They grow into adult worms in the duodenum. The female worms become embedded in the wall of the intestine and lay eggs. The eggs are similar to those of hookworm but are smaller. As soon as they are laid, the eggs hatch to liberate rhabditiform larvae. These larvae can develop further in three different ways.</p>
<ol>
<li>Free living existence (indirect cycle) The rhabditiform larvae are passed out in faeces, enter the soil and start a free-living existence. Under favourable conditions they develop directly into adult worms which lay eggs and continue the cycle in soil. Some of the rhabditiform larvae may develop into filariform larvae to initiate human infection.</li>
<li>Direct cycle The rhabditiform larva may grow directly into a filariform larva in soil and penetrate human skin to continue parasitic cycle in another individual.</li>
<li>Autoinfection The rhabditiform larva may become filariform larva in the intestine itself, and instead of passing out in faeces, penetrate the intestinal mucosa to enter the bloodstream to continue the cycle in the same host (Fig. 8.12).</li>
</ol>
<p>Autoinfection or reinfection may also occur when the rhabditiform larva is converted to the filariform larva while on the perianal skin. It migrates upwards to the intestine and penetrates the mucosa to reinfect the same individual.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Intestinal Helminths of Humans 1027</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><strong>Adult worms</strong> The female worm is very small (2 mm) colourless, semitransparent with a short buccal cavity and a long slender oesophagus. No male form has been identified in the parasitic stage. The female is said to reproduce by parthenogenesis, that is, a form of sexual reproduction in which the organism develops without fertilisation by the male gamete.</p>
