<p><strong>Egg</strong> The eggs are oval, colourless <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>50</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>30</mn><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">50 \times 30 \mu \mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">50</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">30</span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>; similar to and slightly smaller than hookworm eggs.</p>
<p>The eggs hatch almost immediately after they are laid, and therefore the female worm is said to be ovoviviparous.</p>
<p><strong>Larvae</strong> Rhabditiform Larva: It measures <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>300</mn><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mo>×</mo><mn>20</mn><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">300\mu \mathrm{m} \times 20\mu \mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">300</span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">20</span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span> and has a typical bulbed oesophagus showing club-shaped anterior bulb, a constriction and a posterior bulb (Fig. 2.4).</p>
<p>Filariform Larva: The filariform larva is long and slender (<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>600</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>15</mn><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">600 \times 15 \mu \mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">600</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">15</span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>) with a long cylindrical oesophagus.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> The symptoms of strongyloidiasis vary greatly both in severity and areas of the body involved.</p>
<p>Cutaneous rash or itching may occur during the skin penetration by the larva. Pulmonary symptoms may appear during the passage of larvae through the lungs. The severity depends on the parasitic load and host's immune response. The symptoms include cough, breathlessness, fever, pneumonia. These larvae may sometimes be found in sputum.</p>
<p>Intestinal mucosa may get damaged in heavy infections, showing symptoms similar to peptic ulcer. Heavy infection may lead to malabsorption, steatorrhoea and dehydration. Repeated autoinfection can be fatal, especially in immunosuppressed patients</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-2a12cfa6.jpeg" alt="img-113.jpeg">
Fig. 8.12 Life cycle of Strongyloides stercoralis</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1028-parasitology">1028 Parasitology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1028-parasitology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis Strongyloidiasis is diagnosed by detection of larvae in faeces, duodenal aspirate or in any other body fluid.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Microscopic examination of faeces or duodenal aspirate or other body fluids by</p>
<p>(a) Saline wet mount for motile larvae
(b) Iodine wet mount: Larvae are immobilised and can be studied to identify (rhabditiform or filariform) or differentiate from hookworm larvae (Fig. 2.4).
(c) Concentration techniques: Either formal-ether or Baerman technique (Chapter 2) should be used.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; For quick differentiation, rhabditiform larvae are shorter and plumpier than filariaform larvae and have a bulbed oesophagus. Strongyloides larvae have a shorter buccal cavity and notched tail as compared to hookworm larvae.</p>
<p>Chapter 9</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="common-blood-and-tissue-helminths">Common Blood and Tissue Helminths<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#common-blood-and-tissue-helminths" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>For laboratory techniques in this chapter, refer to Chapters 3 and 4</p>
<p>Some helminth parasites infecting humans inhabit body sites other than intestine. Such parasites causing extraintestinal infections may be cestodes (e.g. <em>Echinococcus granulosus</em>), trematodes (e.g. <em>Schistosomes</em>) or nematodes (e.g. <em>Trichinella spiralis</em> or <em>Wuchereria bancrofti</em>).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-extraintestinal-cestodes-tape-worms">I. EXTRAINTESTINAL CESTODES (TAPE WORMS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-extraintestinal-cestodes-tape-worms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="echinococcus-granulosus">ECHINOCOCCUS GRANULOSUS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#echinococcus-granulosus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The infection caused by <em>E. granulosus</em> is called hydatid disease or echinococcosis. It occurs in many parts of the world where animals like dog and sheep are in close contact with humans.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Echinococcosis is a disease of animals transmissible to man (<em>zoonosis</em>). Dog and other members of the canine family are the definitive hosts where as the intermediate hosts are animals such as sheep, pigs, cattle, goats and camels. Man is an accidental intermediate host and the life cycle may not continue after infecting a human being.</p>
<p>The cycle starts when the eggs of the parasite are ingested by an intermediate host through food or hands contaminated by dog’s faeces. The eggs release the embryos in the duodenum, which migrate through the intestinal wall into the blood stream. From there they are carried to other organs such as the liver, lungs, brain etc. In these organs the embryos develop to form hydatid cysts.</p>
<p>The natural cycle is completed when the infected tissue from the intermediate host, e.g. sheep, is eaten by a member of canine family which serves as a definitive host. The scolices from the cyst, evaginate, attach to the wall of the small intestine and grow into mature worm. Eggs are released when a gravid segment ruptures (Fig. 9.1).</p>
<p>In man, the cycle remains incomplete because human flesh is usually not eaten. The hydatid cyst is eventually surrounded by a fibrous cyst wall and later may get calcified.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Adult worm</strong> It is the smallest tapeworm (3 to 8 mm) of medical importance. The scolex is globular with a prominent rostellum having a double row of hooks and four suckers. The body consists of head, neck and three proglottids. The first proglottid is immature, the second with fully developed reproductive organs and the third gravid segment shows uterus with 12 to 15 branches filled with about 500 eggs (Fig. 2.16). The gravid segment ruptures to release the eggs.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1030 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-a7abbc2d.jpeg" alt="img-114.jpeg">
Fig. 9.1 Life cycle of Echinococcus granulosus</p>
<p>Egg The egg measures 30–40 μm, and resembles that of Taenia species. It is brown in colour with a radially striated wall and contains a hexacanth (six-hooked) embryo.</p>
<p>Hydatid cyst Hydatid cysts in humans are of three types; unilocular, coenurus and multilocular, formed by different parasites (Fig. 9.2).</p>
<p>The unilocular cyst produced by E. granulosus is the most common form in humans. It grows slowly measuring up to 7 cm when fully developed. The cyst consists of</p>
<p>(i) An external, laminated, non nucleated thick layer
(ii) An inner, nucleated germinal layer</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-feca9d22.jpeg" alt="img-115.jpeg">
A</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-e0497f01.jpeg" alt="img-116.jpeg">
B</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-94e08605.jpeg" alt="img-117.jpeg">
C</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-ad7b4dd3.jpeg" alt="img-118.jpeg">
D</p>
<p>A: Cysticercus—One cavity, one scolex (e.g. Taenia solium)
B: Coenurus—One cavity, multiple scolices (e.g. Multiceps species)
C: Hydatid—One cavity, brood capsules each with multiple scolices (e.g. Echinococcus granulosus).
D: Multilocular hydatid—Multiple cavities, each having brood capsules with multiple scolices (e.g. Echinococcus multilocularis)</p>
<p>Fig. 9.2 Various forms of cysts produced by cestodes</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Blood and Tissue Helminths 1031</p>
<p>(iii) Colourless or light yellow sterile fluid that causes distension of these two layers.</p>
<p>(iv) Brood capsules or daughter cysts which bud off from the inner germinal layer and may remain attached or float free in the fluid. Individual scolices (heads) bud off from the inner wall of the daughter cysts (Fig. 4.2).</p>
<p>Hydatid sand When the brood capsule ruptures within the hydatid cyst, the scolices escape into the hydatid fluid. These protoscolices and free daughter cysts are called hydatid sand.</p>
<p>An average fertile cyst contains about 2 million protoscolices which when eaten by a definitive host can produce innumerable adult worms in about seven weeks.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hydatid disease is potentially dangerous, severity depending on the site and size of the cyst. Obstruction or pressure on a vital organ can be fatal. Leakage or rupture of the cyst can lead to a severe anaphylactic shock.</p>
<p>Liver is the most common site of hydatid cyst which may lead to obstructive jaundice. If present in the bone, it can cause bone fractures. Lung is also a common site. Other sites include the brain, kidneys and spleen. Release of cyst in a tissue may cause abscess formation, emboli and the development of more young cyst at other sites. Some cysts may grow only for a short time, then die and calcify.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hydatid disease is diagnosed in the laboratory by</p>
<p>(i) Examination of the cyst fluid after its removal from the body.
(ii) Serodiagnosis</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong>
Rupture of the cyst for aspiration of the fluid is not advised because leakage of the fluid can cause severe anaphylactic shock and massive reinfection.</p>
<p>(iii) Detection of hydatid fragments, brood capsules or scolices in sputum or urine from a ruptured cyst.</p>
<p><strong>Specimens</strong> Hydatid cyst, serum, sputum, urine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Examination of the cyst fluid or other specimens containing fragments: The sediment of the cyst fluid after centrifugation should be examined microscopically for protoscolices. The protoscolex is colourless and shows invaginated scolex with hooks.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wall of the cyst can be examined histologically.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Serodiagnosis:</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>(i) <strong>Casoni skin test</strong> It is an intradermal skin test for the indirect evidence of infection. Intradermal injection of hydatid cyst fluid evokes an immune response in the presence of infection. However, the specific antigens are not available and cross reactions occur in other parasitic infections.</p>
<p>(ii) Indirect hemagglutination test for detection of antibodies.</p>
<p>(iii) Complement fixation test (CFT) The test is useful after surgical removal of the cyst because the complement fixing antibodies disappear after one year.</p>
<p>(iv) Indirect immunofluorescence test (IFT) Same as CFT.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="echinococcus-multilocularis-and-multiceps-multiceps">ECHINOCOCCUS MULTILOCULARIS AND MULTICEPS MULTICEPS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#echinococcus-multilocularis-and-multiceps-multiceps" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Man can be occasionally infected by ingesting eggs of <em>E. multilocularis</em> and <em>M. multicieps</em> in food contaminated with dog faeces. The infections are usually fatal.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1032 Parasitology</p>
<p>Echinococcus multilocularis has a life cycle similar to that of E. granulosus. It forms a multilocular hydatid cyst (Fig. 9.2) which is composed of many cavities with no fluid or free scolices, is not encapsulated and spreads like a malignant tumour. The liver is most commonly affected. Multiceps multiceps produces a coenurus cyst, primarily in the central nervous system. The structure of a coenurus cyst is intermediate between that of a hydatid cyst with multiple scolices and daughter cysts; and that of a cysticercus with a single scolex. A single coenurus cyst has multiple scolices and there are no daughter cysts.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ii-blood-and-tissue-trematodes">II. BLOOD AND TISSUE TREMATODES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-blood-and-tissue-trematodes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="schistosomes">SCHISTOSOMES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#schistosomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Schistosomes are trematodes or flukes which are different from other trematodes in many respects. (i) They are not hermaphrodite like other flukes but are dioecious, i.e. the sexes are separate. (ii) Their life cycle varies from the other flukes because the infection occurs through skin penetration by the larval form, cercaria, not by its ingestion. (iii) The adult worms live in the blood vessels, not in the gastro-intestinal tract.</p>
<p>There are three species of schistosomes which are important agents of human disease: Schistosoma haematobium, S.mansoni and S.japonicum. Basic life cycles of schistosomes are similar, but there are many species differences (Table 9.1) that allows differentiation among them on the basis of morphology, intermediate host species and pathology.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The infection with schistosome larvae, the cercariae, occurs when human skin comes in contact with them in fresh water. The cercariae have bifurcated tail which is lost when they penetrate the skin. Inside the human host, a cercaria without a tail is called a schistosomulum. It migrates</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-19769484.jpeg" alt="img-119.jpeg">
Fig. 9.3 Life cycle of Schistosomes</p>
<p>through the tissue and finally enters a blood vessel. Through blood, it is carried to the heart, the lung and then to the liver. The worm begins to grow and mature into an adult within the liver sinusoids. Eventually they migrate against the portal blood flow into the mesenteric, vesical or pelvic venules.</p>
<p>The delicate adult worms reside in the venules in pairs, the female lying in the gynecophoric canal of male. Depending on the species, eggs are passed daily into the venules (e.g. about 300 by S.mansoni, 3500 by S.japonicum). Egg deposition occurs in the small venules of the intestine and the rectum (S. japonicum and S.mansoni) or the venules of the bladder (S. haematobium). The eggs are carried to the lumen of the bladder or urethra. (S. haematobium) or into the intestine (S.mansoni and S.japonicum) and are ultimately released from the body in the urine or faeces. The eggs are immature when first laid and contain a mature miracidium by the time they are released in the urine or faeces.</p>
<p>When the egg comes in contact with water, the mature miracidium is liberated. It swims actively in the water till it finds a suitable snail host which varies from species to species. The miracidium penetrates the snail and develops through the stages of a mother sporocyst which produces</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Blood and Tissue Helminths 1033</p>
<p>daughter sporocysts that finally develop into cercariae with a forked tail. These cercariae are released by the snail into water to infect man through skin penetration.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Adult worms The schistosomes are not flat, leaf like worms like other trematodes. They have narrow, elongated bodies and have separate sexes.</p>
<p>The three pathogenic species show morphological variations as shown in Figure 2.16. The larger, thicker male has a cylindrical anterior end. The body surface may be smooth, as in S. japonicum, heavily tuberculated like S. mansoni or may show fewer, smaller tubercules as on S. haematobium. The length of the male ranges from 0.6 to 2.5cm. The body of the male is folded to form a long ventral gynecophoric canal in which the darker, slender and longer female is held during copulation.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-96f619e4.jpeg" alt="img-120.jpeg">
Fig. 9.4 Schistosomes in copula</p>
<p>Eggs Figure 4.8 and Table 9.1 show morphological differences among the three species of the eggs of the schistosomes. The size, shape of the egg and the position of the spine help in differentiation. When passed out in urine or faeces, the egg contains a fully developed miracidium which is liberated on contact with water. A viable miracidium is indicated by the flickering of flame cells, the excretory system (Fig. 4.7).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The clinical symptoms of schistosomiasis may be divided into three phases.</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Developmental phase It includes the period from the penetration of the skin to the development of mature worms. Mild cutaneous lesions may develop at the site of entry and there may be tissue reactions due to the presence of immature worms, both inside and outside the blood vessels.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Production and discharge of eggs As soon as the eggs are produced, initially they are trapped in the fine venules. The eggs liberate a number of enzymes which facilitate their passage to the lumen of the bladder or the intestine. In S. haematobium infections, the spine on the egg may cause haematuria at the end of micturition. S. manosoni and S. japonicum infections may lead to blood stained faeces.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Immunological response As the eggs are deposited in tissues, their antigens invoke an immune response in the host, leading to the formation of granulomas around the eggs in tissues. The wall of the intestine or the bladder becomes inflamed and thickened causing mechanical obstruction. Some eggs are swept into blood circulation and deposited in the liver and other organs in the body. Cerebral symptoms are commonly seen in S. japonicum infections while pulmonary involvement is more common with S. haematobium infections.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Schistosomiasis is diagnosed in the laboratory by</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of eggs in urine, faeces; in rectal or bladder biopsy</li>
<li>Serological diagnosis</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1034 Parasitology</p>
<p>Table 9.1 Characteristics of Blood Trematodes</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Species</th><th>Geographic Areas</th><th>Diagnostic Specimen</th><th>Egg</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>Size</td><td>Shape</td></tr><tr><td>Schistosoma mansoni</td><td>Africa, Malagasy, West Indies, Surinam, Brazil, Venezuela</td><td>Stool, rectal biopsy, serum</td><td>114–180 × 45–73 μm</td><td>Elongate, prominent lateral spine</td></tr><tr><td>Schistosoma japonicum</td><td>China, Indonesia, Japan, Philippines</td><td>Stool, rectal biopsy, serum</td><td>55–85 × 40–60 μm</td><td>Oval, minute lateral spine</td></tr><tr><td>Schistosoma haematobium</td><td>Africa, Middle East, India, Portugal</td><td>Urine, stool (some cases), serum</td><td>112–170 × 40–70 μm</td><td>Elongate, terminal spine.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Blood and Tissue Helminths 1035</p>
<p><strong>Specimens</strong> Urine, stool, biopsy from the rectum or bladder, or serum.</p>
<p><strong>Techniques</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of characteristic eggs in urine or faeces by
(i) Saline wet preparation
(ii) Concentration methods such as sedimentation and filtration. Zinc sulphate floatation may rupture the eggs. Simple sedimentation by centrifugation is useful for eggs in urine. The species identification depends on the morphology of the ova. Occasionally eggs of <em>S.mansoni</em> may be found in urine and those of <em>S.haematobium</em> in faeces.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Detection of viable Schistosoma eggs:</strong> For estimating the active infection and the effect of treatment, it is necessary to count the number of viable eggs present in the fresh specimen. The methods are described in Chapter 4.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Examination of rectal or bladder biopsies:
(i) The biopsy tissue can be crushed between two slides and examined microscopically to identify the species. If the preparation is too thick, add a drop of 4% KOH to clear the tissue.
(iii) Digest the tissue in 4% KOH at 37°C for 6 to 18 hours. Centrifuge and examine the sediment for ova.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Serodiagnosis: It is not very useful because of cross reactions with other helminths, slow immunological response of the host and persistent elevated titres even after successful treatment.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>PARAGONIMUS WESTERMANI</strong>
<em>(Lung Fluke)</em></p>
<p>Infection with <em>P.westermani</em> is common in the Far East, parts of South-east Asia and Indonesia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Humans are infected by this parasite by ingesting uncooked crabs or crayfish containing metacercariae of <em>P.westermani</em>. The metacercariae excyst in the duodenum and migrate through the intestinal wall, abdominal cavity and diaphragm into the pleural cavity and the lungs.</p>
<p>In the lungs, the larvae mature into adults and discharge their eggs into the bronchial secretions. The eggs may be either coughed up in the sputum or swallowed and excreted in the faeces. After contact with water, the eggs hatch and liberate miracidium to infect a snail. The miracidia undergo development in the snail and become cercariae. On leaving the snail they infect crabs or crayfish and develop into metacercariae. The life cycle is completed when humans ingest crab/cray fish containing the metacercariae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Adult worm</strong> It is reddish brown in colour, measuring about 12 × 6 × 5 mm. The body is covered with short spines. It is a hermophrodite.</p>
<p><strong>Egg</strong> It is oval (90 × 60 μm), yellow brown with a flat operculum. The operculum has a raised rim or ‘shoulder’ around it. The shell opposite the operculum is slightly thickened. The egg contains unsegmented embryo and a mass of yolk cells. (Fig. 4.6b).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The adult worms in the lungs induce a tissue reaction resulting in pulmonary infection, which in severe cases may resemble pulmonary tuberculosis. The worms lie in a fibrous capsule. The brownish-yellow eggs, when coughed out in sputum, give an appearance of ‘iron-filings’. The eggs and larvae may be carried to other organs directly or through the blood stream, and get encysted.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Paragonimiasis is confirmed by</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of eggs in sputum or faeces</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1036 Parasitology</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Serological tests.</li>
</ol>
<p>Specimens Sputum or pleural fluid, faeces, serum</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iii-blood-and-tissue-nematodes">III. BLOOD AND TISSUE NEMATODES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-blood-and-tissue-nematodes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The parasitic nematodes of the blood and tissue may be arranged in two groups:</p>
<p>A. The filarial worms and the guinea worm
B. The tissue nematodes that normally infect other hosts but occasionally infect humans.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="df583fb3">♦ FILARIAL NEMATODES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#df583fb3" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The slender filarial worms are arthropod-transmitted parasites of the circulatory and lymphatic systems, muscles, connective tissues or serous cavities of vertebrates. Out of more than 200 species of these parasites, only a few infect humans. The three major human pathogenes are <em>Wuchereria bancrofti</em>, <em>Brugia malayi</em> and <em>Onchocerca volvulus</em>, which account for most infections. <em>Loa loa</em> and <em>Mansonella ozardi</em> are less commonly involved. In the adult stages, the human filarial parasites inhabit the lymphatic system and deep or subcutaneous connective tissues. Each parasite has a complex life cycle. The life cycle of <em>Wuchereria bancrofti</em> is summarised in Figure 9.5.</p>
<p>A distinctive characteristic of filarial worms is that the viviparous female gives birth to pre-larvae i.e. microfilariae. Their morphology, location in the host and type of periodicity are useful in differentiating species as shown in Table 9.2. Microfilariae are highly motile, thread like and can be detected in the peripheral blood or connective tissues.</p>
<p><strong>Periodicity:</strong> The highest number of microfilariae appears in peripheral blood at specific periods. This phenomenon is known as periodicity. Nocturnal periodicity means the highest number of microfilariae occur in blood at night. In diurnal or non periodic type, the number of microfilariae during the day are about the same as that during the night. If the number during the day is one-third of that during the night, it is called nocturnally subperiodic (Tables 3.2, 9.2).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="life-cycle">Life cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The life cycle of a filarial worm requires an insect vector such as mosquito (for <em>W. bancrofti</em>) or a mango fly (for <em>Loa loa</em>), as an intermediate host. The life cycles of all the filarial worms are similar with minor differences which help in their identification. The life cycle involves:</p>
<ol>
<li>Penetration of the skin by the infective larva through the bite wound made by the insect vector.</li>
<li>The larvae reach their site of maturation, such as lymphatics (<em>Wuchereria bancrofti</em>, <em>Brugia malayi</em>), connective tissue or conjunctiva (<em>Loa loa</em>) or subcutaneous tissue (<em>Onchocerca volvulus</em>) and grow into adult worms.</li>
<li>The female worm discharges microfilariae in blood (<em>W. bancrofti</em>, <em>B. malayi</em>, <em>L. loa</em> or in skin, body fluids or urine (<em>O. volvulus</em>).</li>
<li>The microfilariae are taken up by the respective insect vector where they undergo development to form infective larvae to infect humans (Fig. 9.5, Table 9.2).</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Adult worms</strong> The worms are thread-like and creamy white in colour and range from 2cm to 50cm in length. The female is twice the size of the male. The mouth is simple, without lips and the buccal cavity is very small. The oesophagus is cylindrical.</p>
<p><strong>Microfilariae</strong> The microfilariae measure about 200 to 300μm in length. They contain a column of cells with deeply staining nuclei which represents the rudiments of the intestine and perhaps other organs. This column of cells with nuclei extends nearly the whole length and occupies almost the</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Table 9.2 Characteristics of human microfilariae</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Species</th><th>Geographic Area</th><th>Vector</th><th>Microfilariae Location</th><th>Periodicity</th><th>Microfilariae Features</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>Sheath</td><td>Mean Length(μm)</td><td>Tail Nuclei</td></tr><tr><td>Wuchereria bancrofti</td><td>Tropics and subtropics worldwide</td><td>Mosquito</td><td>Blood Hydrocoele fluid</td><td>Nocturnal, subperiodic</td><td>Present</td><td>260</td><td>Nuclei do not extend to tail tip</td></tr><tr><td>Brugia malayi</td><td>Southeast Asia</td><td>Mosquito</td><td>Blood</td><td>Nocturnal Subperiodic</td><td>Present</td><td>220</td><td>Subterminal and terminal nuclei</td></tr><tr><td>Loa loa</td><td>Africa</td><td>Mango fly</td><td>Blood</td><td>Diurnal</td><td>Present</td><td>275</td><td>Nuclei continuous to tail tip</td></tr><tr><td>Mansonella ozzardi</td><td>Central and South America</td><td>Midge</td><td>Blood</td><td>None</td><td>Absent</td><td>200</td><td>Nuclei do not extend to tail tip</td></tr><tr><td>Onchocerca volvulus</td><td>Africa and Central and South America</td><td>Black fly</td><td>Skin</td><td>None</td><td>Absent</td><td>254</td><td>Nuclei do not extend to tail tip</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>1038 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-92df13a7.jpeg" alt="img-121.jpeg">
Fig. 9.5 Life cycle of Wuchereria bancrofti</p>
<p>entire width of the body (Fig. 9.6(a)). The absence or presence of nuclei at the tip of the tail varies with species (Fig. 9.6b). Microfilariae of Wuchereria bancrofti, Brugia malayi and Loa loa are sheathed i.e. they have a delicate, close fitting membrane which is derived from the original egg shell. It is visible only where it projects beyond the head or the tail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Lymphatic filariasis is caused by Wuchereria bancrofti, and less frequently by Brugia malayi. It is characterised by fever and painful inflamed lymphatics. The lymphatic vessels are thickened and finally blocked, especially those of the limbs and genitals. When the lymphatic flow is obstructed due to blockage, it results in swelling, fibrosis and then elephantiasis.</p>
<p>An uncommon complication of chronic infection of W.bancrofti is chyluria. When the lymphatics which transport chyle from the intestine are blocked, the chyle appears in the urine giving it a milky appearance.</p>
<p>Calabar swellings are caused by Loa loa infection as an allergic response to the adult worm migrating in the subcutaneous tissue. The swellings last for a few days and do not contain any worms.</p>
<p>Loa loa adult worms can sometimes be seen migrating the conjunctiva but they do not cause blindness.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-47eaf3fa.jpeg" alt="img-122.jpeg">
Fig. 9.6 (a) General features of microfilaria</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Blood and Tissue Helminths 1039</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-ff2ff3f2.jpeg" alt="img-123.jpeg">
Fig. 9.6 (b) Diagnostic characteristics of microfilariae</p>
<p>ness. Generally Loa loa infections are symptomless.</p>
<p>Onchocercomas are subcutaneous nodules containing adult Onchocerca volvulus. O.volvulus infections can also cause dermatitis, lymphadenitis and blindness. The blindness or ocular lesions is a result of host's immune response to microfilariae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Definitive laboratory diagnosis of filarial infections depends on detecting microfilariae in blood, skin, hydrocoele, urine etc. depending on the infecting species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Blood</strong> Collection time of blood depends on the periodicity of the infecting species. For nocturnal periodicity, the blood should be collected between 10pm to 4 am.</p>
<p><strong>Skin snips</strong> If O.volvulus infection is suspected, multiple blood-free skin snips should be taken from scapula, gluteal or calf areas.</p>
<p>Other specimens include urine, hydrocoele fluid or lymph node biopsies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Examination of blood for microfilariae by
(i) Wet preparation
(ii) Thin blood film stained by Giemsa or Fields technique
(iii) Concentration methods.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examination of urine or hydrocoele fluid: Make a wet preparation from the centrifuged deposit of the specimen and examine microscopically for motile microfilariae.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examination of skin snip: Immerse the skin snip in saline on a glass slide, cover it with a coverslip and place it in a moist chamber to avoid drying. Examine under low power objective after 15 minutes, 30 minutes, one hour and overnight incubation for microfilariae emerging from the skin.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> It is necessary to stain microfilariae for identification of species on the basis of morphological characteristics (Fig. 9.6). Giemsa or Fields technique should be used for staining.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Serological diagnosis is of limited value in endemic areas because a high percentage of the population will show positive reaction.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-guinea-worm-dracunculus-medinensis">THE GUINEA WORM (Dracunculus medinensis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-guinea-worm-dracunculus-medinensis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>D.medinensis infection is common in tropical countries where shallow ponds or walk-in wells are used as sources of drinking water.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1040 Parasitology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Infection is acquired by drinking water containing infected cyclops. Larvae are released from the cyclops into the duodenum, penetrate through the duodenal wall and develop into mature worms in connective tissues. When fertilised by a male, the female migrates to the subcutaneous tissue, especially of the lower limbs while the male dies after the fertilization.</p>
<p>Near the anterior end of the worm, a papule is formed on the skin of the host which changes to a blister and later, ulcerates. On contact with water, the uterus of the female worm protrudes through the blister to release a large number of larvae in the water. The larvae are eventually ingested by cyclops where they undergo development and become infective for humans.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><strong>Adult worms</strong> The female is very long, measuring about one metre, while the male is only about 25 mm long. The worms are white in colour.</p>
<p><strong>Larva</strong> The larva is about 500 µm in length. It has a pointed tail. The larva is actively motile and coils and uncoils its tail while moving.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The female worm can cause severe pain and allergic reactions such as urticarial rash, nausea, vomiting, diarrhoea or asthma. These symptoms appear just before appearance of the blister. Complete removal of the worm from this site results in the healing of the blister. However, if the worm is broken or damaged in the skin, a severe inflammatory reaction can be produced. Secondary infection may occur at the site of the blister leading to arthritis, synovitis and rarely, tetanus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Diagnosis of <em>D. medinensis</em> infection depends on</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>The appearance of the female worm at the opening of a blister.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The detection of discharged larvae when the blister is covered with water.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The larvae are discharged when the blister comes in contact with the water. Cover the blister with a few drops of water and collect it back with a pasteur pipette after a few minutes. Examine the water microscopically for the larvae which move by coiling and uncoiling (Fig. 9.7).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tissue-nematodes-trichinella-spiralis">TISSUE NEMATODES (<em>Trichinella spiralis</em>)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tissue-nematodes-trichinella-spiralis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Trichinellosis has a world wide distribution, but is more common in Europe and United States.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="life-cycle">Life Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Human infection occurs by ingestion of raw or improperly cooked meat such as pork which contains encysted larvae of <em>Trichinella spiralis</em>.</p>
<p>After ingestion the larvae excyst in the stomach and invade the intestinal mucosa. They mature within 24–48 hours. The female worms after mat</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-89d69a21.jpeg" alt="img-124.jpeg">
Fig. 9.7 Larvae of <em>Dracunculus medinensis</em></p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Common Blood and Tissue Helminths 1041</p>
<p>ing begin to deposit motile larvae which are carried by the lymphatics and blood stream to various tissues. A single female produces about 500 larvae in four weeks. In the tissues a cyst wall is developed by the immune response of the host around the larva. The encysted larvae may survive for many years or may die and get calcified. The life cycle ends blindly in the human host (Fig. 9.8). Although the adult worms and larvae are present in the same host, another host is required to complete the life cycle. Pigs, bears, rodents and other animals are natural hosts for Trichinella.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology">Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Adult worms <em>Trichinella spiralis</em> are very small worms. The female is 4 mm long and the male is 1.5 mm.</p>
<p>Larvae The coiled larvae are seen encysted in the muscle. The cysts lie longitudinally in the muscle and each measures 0.5 mm × 0.2 mm (Fig. 9.9).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Symptoms of trichinellosis may be due to the maturation of the worms in the intestine or due to muscle penetration and encystation. Intestinal symptoms resemble those of food poisoning and include diarrhoea, vomiting and abdominal cramps. During the muscle invasion, there may be fever, facial oedema, muscle pain and swelling. The symptoms depend on the muscle involved, the most severe being myocarditis due to heart muscle invasion or neurological disorders due to the invasion of the central nervous system.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Trichinellosis is diagnosed by</p>
<ol>
<li>Detection of encysted larvae in muscle</li>
<li>Serodiagnosis</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Muscle biopsy, especially from the diaphragm, tongue, larynx, neck, ribs and shoulder</li>
<li>Serum</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Examination of the muscle biopsy:</li>
</ol>
<p>(i) Compress the tissue between two slides and examine microscopically for larvae encysted in the muscle (Fig. 9.9).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-055a1607.jpeg" alt="img-125.jpeg"></p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1042 Parasitology</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-361c9f6c.jpeg" alt="img-126.jpeg">
Fig. 9.9 Encysted larva of T. spiralis in muscle</p>
<p>(ii) Concentration of larvae by artificial digestion technique using acid pepsin solution.
(iii) Haematoxylin and eosin staining of tissue section (refer to Histopathology section)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Serodiagnosis: An agglutination or flocculation test using latex or bentonite particles can be performed for the detection of antibodies to antigens of Trichinella. The antibodies can be detected about two weeks after the onset of infection and last for about 10 months.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="toxocara-species-animal-roundworm">TOXOCARA SPECIES (Animal Roundworm)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#toxocara-species-animal-roundworm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The roundworms of animals; <em>T. canis</em> (roundworm of dogs) and <em>T. catis</em> (roundworm of cats) can cause visceral larvae migrans, especially in children under three years of age. The infection occurs by ingestion of eggs from food or hand contaminated by dog or cat faeces. The larvae, after being released in the intestine, are unable to mature, invade the intestinal wall and are carried to other organs such as the liver, lung, brain, heart and eyes. Rash and granulomas develop on the skin due to the inflammatory response to the toxicara antigens. The larvae get lodged in the tissues and eventually die. Eosinophilia is very common.</p>
<p>The laboratory diagnosis is made by serodiagnosis using ELISA technique.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ancylostoma-species-animal-hookworms">ANCYLOSTOMA SPECIES (Animal Hookworms)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ancylostoma-species-animal-hookworms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Animal hookworms such as <em>A. brasiliense</em> and <em>A. caninum</em> can cause cutaneous larvae migrans. The infection is caused by the larvae from soil, either by skin penetration or by ingestion. The larvae are unable to develop into adult worms in humans. They migrate superficially under the skin and produce linear tracks as a result of allergic reactions. The intense scratching may lead to secondary infection. Most larvae eventually die in the skin, but rarely may reach the lungs causing pneumonitis. There is marked eosinophilia. The diagnosis depends on the appearances of typical linear tracks on the skin.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 10</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="quality-control-procedures-in-parasitology">Quality Control Procedures in Parasitology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#quality-control-procedures-in-parasitology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Quality control measures are necessary in Parasitology to help in the assurance of accurate results. The quality control programme should include description of the steps and procedures for the generation and delivery of reliable results. The areas of Parasitology laboratory which need quality control are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Collection, transport and preservation of specimens.</li>
<li>Use of equipments and instruments.</li>
<li>Preparation of reagents and stains.</li>
<li>Techniques used for detection and recognition of parasites.</li>
<li>Reporting and recording of results.</li>
</ol>
<p>Quality control measure involve:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-adequate-supervision">1. Adequate supervision<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-adequate-supervision" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The section supervisor or chief technologist should ensure that the records maintained by the staff for controls of routine procedures, instrument function tests, temperature records, etc. are correct and up to date. If the controls do not fall within acceptable limits, the corrective measures should be taken.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-regulations-for-reception-recording-and-reporting-of-specimens">2. Regulations for reception, recording and reporting of specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-regulations-for-reception-recording-and-reporting-of-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Each specimen must be accompanied by a requisition with patient's relevant information, provisional diagnosis and time of collection of specimens. All the specimens received must be recorded in a log book, work-sheet or logged-in a computer.</p>
<p>While reporting a positive finding, the complete genus and species name of the parasite identified must be indicated. The specific stage of the parasite, such as, trophozoites, cysts, larvae, eggs, etc. should be reported. Quantitation of parasites should be done whenever necessary. Cellular elements such as pus cells, red cells, macrophages etc. if present, should be reported.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-procedure-manuals">3. Procedure manuals<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-procedure-manuals" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A well-written, complete manual for standard operating procedures (S.O.P.) should include:</p>
<p>(i) Instruction for the proper collection and handling of specimens.
(ii) Preparation of reagents and stains
(iii) Methodology for testing the specimens
(iv) Criteria for identification of parasites
(v) Interpretation and reporting
(vi) References</p>
<p>Each member of staff should be familiar with the contents of this manual.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-regular-maintenance-and-checking-of-instruments-and-equipments">4. Regular maintenance and checking of instruments and equipments<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-regular-maintenance-and-checking-of-instruments-and-equipments" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Quality control of instruments and equipments is achieved by the following methods:</p>
<p>(i) A preventive maintenance schedule for all instruments and equipment should be planned.
(ii) The temperature of equipment such as refrigerators, freezers, incubators and</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1044 Parasitology</p>
<p>water bath should be checked with calibrated thermometers every day and recorded.</p>
<p>(iii) Centrifuges should be calibrated for correct speed.</p>
<p>(iv) The microscopes should be taken care of as mentioned in Section 1. The microscopes should be calibrated using a stage and ocular micrometer for each objective and labelled accordingly (Micrometry).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-checking-of-reagents-and-stains-at-regular-intervals">5. Checking of reagents and stains at regular intervals<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-checking-of-reagents-and-stains-at-regular-intervals" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) Each and every reagent bottle or container must be labelled properly giving information about the name of the reagent, concentration, date of preparation and expiry.</p>
<p>(ii) Stains should be routinely checked for the correct staining properties using known positive specimens. The dates and results of the quality control check should be recorded.</p>
<p>(iii) Fixatives also should be checked to see if they fix the parasites and cellular elements within the recommended time limits.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-adequate-training-of-staff-members">6. Adequate training of staff members<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-adequate-training-of-staff-members" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>All members of staff should be aware of the methods of examination, parasites expected to be present in a particular specimen and their identification criteria. Charts and diagrams should be displayed to help in the identification.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-observation-of-safety-regulations">7. Observation of safety regulations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-observation-of-safety-regulations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) All the specimens received should be considered to be potentially pathogenic and handled carefully.</p>
<p>(ii) Inflammable, poisonous and other harmful chemicals must be properly stored.</p>
<p>(iii) Contaminated materials should be disposed off in autoclave plastic bags and autoclaved.</p>
<p>(iv) Liquid spills on the bench or on the floor should be properly disinfected and wiped off.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-participation-in-an-external-quality-assurance-programme">8. Participation in an external quality assurance programme:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-participation-in-an-external-quality-assurance-programme" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Participation in a scheme, in which a reference laboratory sends parasitological specimens for examination and reporting by the laboratory staff is the best way of ensuring that parasites are being detected and identified correctly.</p>
<p>The reference laboratory must send back a written evidence of evaluation of the results, and should also suggest corrective actions whenever necessary.</p>
